<!-- original page 109 -->

## ¬ß18. Complements on √©tale morphisms. Henselian local rings

In the present section, we study various properties special to √©tale morphisms. In addition, the notion of √©tale morphism
allows one to develop in a very natural fashion Nagata's theory of Henselian rings, as well as that of strictly local
rings. These rings play an important role in many recent developments, by appearing each time that one needs a
"localization" procedure finer than the one provided by the Zariski topology (cf. for example `[43]`, while awaiting the
appearance of the chapter of our Treatise devoted to the study of the "√©tale topology").

### 18.1. A remarkable equivalence of categories

**Proposition (18.1.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.1.1 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `S_0` a closed sub-prescheme of `S`, `X_0` an `S_0`-prescheme smooth (resp. √©tale) over `S_0`,
`x_0` a point of `X_0`. Then there exist an open neighbourhood `U_0` of `x_0` in `X_0`, an `S`-prescheme `U` smooth
(resp. √©tale) over `S`, and an `S_0`-isomorphism `U √ó_S S_0 ‚•≤ U_0`.*

Note that if `X_0` is √©tale over `S_0` at the point `x_0`, then it is a fortiori unramified over `S_0` at that point;
if one has constructed an `S`-prescheme `U` smooth over `S` such that `U √ó_S S_0` is isomorphic to `U_0`, then since the
fibres of the morphisms `U_0 ‚Üí S_0` and `U ‚Üí S` containing `x_0` are then isomorphic, it will follow that `U` is
unramified over `S` at the point `x_0` `(17.4.1, d)`, hence also in a neighbourhood of `x_0`; replacing `U` by this
neighbourhood, one concludes that `U` will be √©tale over `S`. It is therefore enough to prove the proposition when one
assumes only `X_0` smooth over `S_0`.

The question being local on `S` and on `X_0`, one may suppose that `S = Spec(A)` and `X_0 = Spec(C_0)` are affine, so
that `S_0 = Spec(A_0)`, where `A_0` is a quotient ring of `A`, `C_0 = B_0/ūĚĒć_0`, where `B_0 = A_0[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]` and
`ūĚĒć_0` is an ideal of finite type of `B_0`; finally, `C_0` is a formally smooth `A_0`-algebra for the discrete
topologies. Let `ūĚĒ≠_0` be the ideal `j_{x_0}` in `C_0`; one has `ūĚĒ≠_0 = ūĚĒģ_0/ūĚĒć_0`, where `ūĚĒģ_0` is a prime ideal of `B_0`.
The Jacobian criterion `(0, 22.6.4)` combined with `(0, 19.1.12)` shows that there exist in `ūĚĒć_0` a family of `r`
polynomials `u_i` `(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)` and `r` indices `j_h` `(1 ‚©Ĺ h ‚©Ĺ r)` such that the images of the `u_i` in
`(ūĚĒć_0)_{ūĚĒģ_0}/(ūĚĒć_0^2)_{ūĚĒģ_0}` generate this `(B_0)_{ūĚĒģ_0}`-module and such that

```text
  (18.1.1.1)    det(‚ąāu_i/‚ąāT_{j_h}) ‚ąČ ūĚĒģ_0.
```

Since `(B_0)_{ūĚĒģ_0}` is a local ring, it follows from Nakayama's lemma that one may suppose the images of the `u_i` in
`(ūĚĒć_0)_{ūĚĒģ_0}` generate this `(B_0)_{ūĚĒģ_0}`-module, then, by replacing if necessary `X_0` by an affine open neighbourhood
of `x_0`, that the `u_i` generate `ūĚĒć_0` `(0_I, 5.2.2)`. Put then `B = A[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]`; `B_0` is thus a quotient ring

<!-- original page 110 -->

of `B`, `ūĚĒ≠_0` is the image of a prime ideal `ūĚĒģ` of `B`, and `ūĚĒģ` is the inverse image of `ūĚĒģ_0`. For each `i`, let
`v_i ‚ąą B` be an element whose image is `u_i` in `B_0`, and let `ūĚĒć` be the ideal of `B` generated by the `v_i`, so that
`ūĚĒć_0` is the image of `ūĚĒć` in `B_0`. The proposition will be established by taking for `U` an open neighbourhood of the
point of `Spec(B/ūĚĒć)` corresponding to the prime ideal `ūĚĒ≠ = ūĚĒģ/ūĚĒć`, provided one proves that `B_ūĚĒģ/ūĚĒć_ūĚĒģ` is a formally smooth
`A`-algebra for the discrete topologies. Now, this follows from the Jacobian criterion, for the images of the `v_i` in
`ūĚĒć_ūĚĒģ/ūĚĒć_ūĚĒģ^2` generate this `B_ūĚĒģ`-module, and it follows from `(18.1.1.1)` that one has
`det(‚ąāv_i/‚ąāT_{j_h}) ‚ąČ ūĚĒģ`.

**Theorem (18.1.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.1.2 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `S_0` a closed sub-prescheme of `S` whose underlying space is identical to that of `S`. Then
the functor*

```text
  X ‚Ü¶ X √ó_S S_0
```

*from the category of `S`-preschemes √©tale over `S` to the category of `S_0`-preschemes √©tale over `S_0` is an
equivalence of categories.*

Let us show first that this functor is *fully faithful*. Let `X`, `Y` be two `S`-preschemes √©tale over `S`, and put
`X_0 = X √ó_S S_0`, `Y_0 = Y √ó_S S_0`. If `Z = X √ó_S Y`, the set `Hom_S(X, Y)` is in canonical bijective correspondence
with the set of `X`-sections `őď(Z/X)`, and similarly `Hom_{S_0}(X_0, Y_0)` is in canonical bijective correspondence with
`őď(Z_0/X_0)`, where `Z_0 = Z √ó_S S_0 = X_0 √ó_{S_0} Y_0`. Now `Z` is √©tale over `X`, `Z_0` √©tale over `X_0`, and `X_0`
(resp. `Z_0`) is a closed sub-prescheme of `X` (resp. `Z`) having the same underlying space. The open subsets of `Z`
such that the restriction of the morphism `Z ‚Üí X` is surjective and radicial are therefore the same as the subsets of
`Z_0` having the corresponding properties, and our assertion follows accordingly from `(17.9.3)`.

To complete the proof, it suffices to see that for every `S_0`-prescheme `X_0` √©tale over `S_0`, there exist an
`S`-prescheme `X` √©tale over `S` and an `S_0`-isomorphism `X_0 ‚•≤ X √ó_S S_0`. By virtue of Prop. `(18.1.1)`, there is an
open cover `(U_őĪ)` of `X_0` and, for each `őĪ`, an `S`-prescheme `V_őĪ` which is √©tale over `S`, and finally an
`S_0`-isomorphism `őł_őĪ : U_őĪ ‚•≤ V_őĪ √ó_S S_0`. Moreover, by the first part of the proof, there exists a unique
`S_0`-isomorphism `ŌÜ_{őĪő≤}` from `őł_őĪ(U_őĪ ‚ą© U_ő≤)` onto `őł_ő≤(U_őĪ ‚ą© U_ő≤)`, corresponding to the identity automorphism of
`U_őĪ ‚ą© U_ő≤`, and it is immediate, for the same reason, that these isomorphisms satisfy the gluing condition
`(0_I, 4.1.7)`. There is consequently an `S`-prescheme `X` such that the `V_őĪ` are canonically identified with
sub-preschemes induced on open sets of `X`, the `őł_őĪ` being identified with `S_0`-isomorphisms which coincide on the
intersections `U_őĪ ‚ą© U_ő≤` and therefore define an `S_0`-isomorphism `X_0 ‚•≤ X √ó_S S_0`. It is clear that `X` is √©tale
over `S` `(17.3.2)`, which completes the proof.

**Corollary (18.1.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.1.3 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `X` an `S`-prescheme √©tale over `S`, `S'` an `S`-prescheme, `S'_0` a closed sub-prescheme of
`S'` having the same underlying space. Then the canonical map `X(S')_S ‚Üí X(S'_0)_S` `(I, 3.4.3)` is bijective.*

Indeed, put `X' = X √ó_S S'`, `X'_0 = X √ó_S S'_0`, so that `X(S')_S = őď(X'/S')` and `X(S'_0)_S = őď(X'_0/S'_0)`; the
corollary follows from the fact that the functor defined in `(18.1.2)` (with `S` and `S_0` replaced by `S'` and `S'_0`
respectively) is fully faithful (or directly from `(17.9.3)`).

<!-- original page 111 -->

### 18.2. √Čtale covers

**(18.2.1)** Given a ring `A` and a commutative `A`-algebra `B` which is *finite* and is a *free* `A`-module, recall
(Bourbaki, _Alg._, chap. VIII, ¬ß12, n¬į 2) that one defines on `B` an `A`-linear form `Tr_{B/A}`, the "*trace form*"; from
this one deduces the definition of a *symmetric `A`-bilinear form* (also called the "*trace form*")

```text
  (18.2.1.1)    (x, y) ‚Ü¶ Tr_{B/A}(xy)
```

whose datum is equivalent to that of the associated `A`-linear map `astr_{B/A} : B ‚Üí BŐĆ` of the `A`-module `B` into its
*dual* `BŐĆ`, equal to its transpose. When `A` is a *field*, it is equivalent to say that this bilinear form is
*nondegenerate* or that `B` is a *separable* `A`-algebra (Bourbaki, _Alg._, chap. IX, ¬ß2, prop. 5).

Let `f : A ‚Üí A'` be a homomorphism of rings; put `B' = B ‚äó_A A'`, and let `g : B ‚Üí B'` be the canonical homomorphism;
the image under `g` of a basis of the `A`-module `B` is then a basis of the `A'`-module `B'`, and it follows from the
definitions that one has, for every `x ‚ąą B`,

```text
  (18.2.1.2)    Tr_{B'/A'}(g(x)) = f(Tr_{B/A}(x)).
```

**(18.2.2)** Consider now a ringed space `(X, ūĚí™_X)` and let `‚Ą¨` be an `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra which, as an `ūĚí™_X`-Module, is
*locally free of finite rank*; then, for every open `U ‚äā X` such that `‚Ą¨ | U` is (as an `ūĚí™_U`-Module) isomorphic to
`ūĚí™_U^n` (for an `n` depending on `U`), `őď(U, ‚Ą¨)` is a `őď(U, ūĚí™_X)`-algebra which, as a `őď(U, ūĚí™_X)`-module, is free of
finite rank and therefore defines a `őď(U, ūĚí™_X)`-linear form `Tr_{őď(U, ‚Ą¨)/őď(U, ūĚí™_X)}`, which we shall also denote
`Tr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X, U}`; from this one deduces an associated linear map

```text
  astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X, U} : őď(U, ‚Ą¨) ‚Üí őď(U, ‚Ą¨)ŐĆ = őď(U, ‚Ą¨ŐĆ).
```

Moreover, it follows from `(18.2.1.2)` that these linear maps are compatible with the operations of restriction from `U`
to a smaller open set, and therefore define on the one hand a homomorphism of `ūĚí™_X`-Modules, also called the *trace
homomorphism*:

```text
  (18.2.2.1)    Tr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X} : ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí ūĚí™_X
```

and on the other hand a homomorphism of `ūĚí™_X`-Modules

```text
  (18.2.2.2)    astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X} : ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí ‚Ą¨ŐĆ
```

said to be *associated with the trace*, and equal to its own transpose. It also follows from `(18.2.1.2)` that for every
`x ‚ąą X` one has

```text
  (18.2.2.3)    (Tr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X})_x = Tr_{‚Ą¨_x/ūĚí™_{X,x}}
  (18.2.2.4)    (astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X})_x = astr_{‚Ą¨_x/ūĚí™_{X,x}}.
```

Finally, under the conditions of `(18.2.1)`, if one puts `X = Spec(A)` and if `‚Ą¨ = BŐÉ` is the `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra corresponding
to `B`, the form `Tr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X}` (resp. the homomorphism `astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_X}`) corresponds to the form `Tr_{B/A}` (resp. to the
homomorphism of `A`-modules `astr_{B/A}`), as also follows from `(18.2.1.2)`.

<!-- original page 112 -->

**Proposition (18.2.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.3 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a finite morphism of preschemes and let `‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)`. The following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) `f` is √©tale.*

*a') `f` is a flat morphism of finite presentation and, for every `x ‚ąą X`, if one puts `y = f(x)`, then
`ūĚí™_{X, x}/ūĚĒ™_y ¬∑ ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a field, a finite separable extension of `k(y)`.*

*b) `‚Ą¨` is a locally free `ūĚí™_Y`-Module and, for every `y ‚ąą Y`, `‚Ą¨_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)` is a finite separable `k(y)`-algebra
(hence the direct composite of a finite number of fields, finite separable extensions of `k(y)`).*

*c) `‚Ą¨` is a locally free `ūĚí™_Y`-Module and the homomorphism `astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_Y} : ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí ‚Ą¨ŐĆ` `(18.2.2)` is bijective.*

Taking into account that `f` is quasi-compact, the equivalence of a) and a') has already been proved `(17.6.2)`. To
prove the rest of the proposition, one may restrict to the case where `Y = Spec(A)` and `X = Spec(B)` are affine, `B`
being a finite `A`-algebra and `‚Ą¨ = BŐÉ`. To say that `f` is a morphism of finite presentation amounts then to saying that
`B` is an `A`-module of finite presentation `(1.4.7)`. If in addition `f` is flat, hence `B` a flat `A`-module, one knows
(Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß5, n¬į 2, cor. 2 of th. 1) that `B` is a projective `A`-module, hence `‚Ą¨` a locally
free `ūĚí™_Y`-Module (loc. cit., n¬į 2, th. 1), and the converse is immediate. On the other hand, `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` is none other
than the spectrum of the `k(y)`-algebra `‚Ą¨(y) = ‚Ą¨_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)`, which completes the proof of the equivalence of
a') and b). To see that b) is equivalent to c), note that the second assertion of b) is equivalent to the fact that the
homomorphism `astr_{‚Ą¨(y)/k(y)} : ‚Ą¨(y) ‚Üí ‚Ą¨(y)ŐĆ` is bijective; since `‚Ą¨(y) = ‚Ą¨_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)` and
`‚Ą¨(y)ŐĆ = ‚Ą¨ŐĆ_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)`, and since `‚Ą¨_y` and `‚Ą¨ŐĆ_y` are free `ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-modules, it follows from `(18.2.2.4)` and from
Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß3, n¬į 3, cor. of prop. 6, that the homomorphism `astr_{‚Ą¨_y/ūĚí™_{Y,y}} : ‚Ą¨_y ‚Üí ‚Ą¨ŐĆ_y` is
also bijective; the converse being obvious, this completes the proof.

When an `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra `‚Ą¨` verifies the equivalent conditions b) and c) of `(18.2.3)`, one says that `‚Ą¨` is a *finite
√©tale `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra*. When `X = Spec(A)` is affine and one therefore has `‚Ą¨ = BŐÉ`, where `B` is an `A`-algebra, it
amounts to the same, by virtue of `(18.2.3)`, to say that `‚Ą¨` is a finite √©tale `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra or that `B` is a finite
√©tale `A`-algebra (in the sense of `(17.3.2)`).

**Corollary (18.2.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.4 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a finite morphism of finite presentation, and put `‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)`. Let `y` be a point of `Y`. The
following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) There exists an open neighbourhood `U` of `y` in `Y` such that the restriction `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí U` of `f` is an √©tale
morphism.*

*b) `‚Ą¨_y` is a free `ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-module of finite type and `‚Ą¨_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)` is a separable `k(y)`-algebra.*

It is clear that a) implies b) by virtue of `(18.2.3)`. On the other hand, `‚Ą¨` is an `ūĚí™_Y`-Module of finite presentation
`(1.6.3 and 1.4.7)`; hence, if `‚Ą¨_y` is a free `ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-module, there exists an open neighbourhood `U` of `y` in `Y`
such that `‚Ą¨ | U` is a locally free `ūĚí™_U`-Module `(0_I, 5.2.7)`; in addition, by hypothesis, the homomorphism
`astr_{‚Ą¨_y/ūĚí™_{Y,y}} : ‚Ą¨_y ‚Üí ‚Ą¨ŐĆ_y` being

<!-- original page 113 -->

bijective, it also follows from `(0_I, 5.2.7)` that one may suppose `U` chosen so that the homomorphism
`astr_{‚Ą¨ | U/ūĚí™_U}` is bijective. The fact that b) implies a) then follows from `(18.2.3)`.

**Corollary (18.2.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.5 -->

*Let `Y` be a quasi-compact or locally Noetherian prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a finite morphism of finite presentation; put
`‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)`. Suppose that for every closed point `y` of `Y`, `‚Ą¨_y` is a free `ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-module and
`‚Ą¨_y ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{Y,y}} k(y)` is a separable `k(y)`-algebra. Then `f` is √©tale.*

Indeed, it follows from `(18.2.4)` that every closed point `y` of `Y` has an open neighbourhood `U` such that the
restriction `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí U` of `f` is √©tale; the conclusion follows from the fact that in the two cases considered, every
non-empty closed subset of `Y` contains a closed point `(5.1.11 and 0_I, 2.1.3)`.

**Corollary (18.2.6).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.6 -->

*If `f : X ‚Üí Y` is a finite √©tale morphism, and if one puts `‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)`, then the `ūĚí™_Y`-homomorphism
`Tr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_Y} : ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí ūĚí™_Y` `(18.2.2)` (also denoted `Tr_f`) is surjective.*

The question being local, one may, by virtue of `(18.2.3)`, suppose that `Y = Spec(A)`, `X = Spec(B)` with `‚Ą¨ = BŐÉ`, `B`
being a free `A`-module; since by virtue of `(18.2.3)` the bilinear form `(18.2.1.1)` is nondegenerate, this entails in
particular that the linear form `Tr_{B/A}` is surjective.

**Remarks (18.2.7).** ‚ÄĒ (i) When `f : X ‚Üí Y` is a finite morphism such that `f_*(ūĚí™_X)` is a locally free `ūĚí™_Y`-Module
(resp. locally free of rank `n`), one says further that `f` is a *finite locally free morphism* (resp. *locally free of
rank `n`*). This condition, by virtue of `(18.2.3)`, is verified if `f` is a finite √©tale morphism, but does not by
itself imply that `f` be √©tale, as is shown by the example where `X = Spec(K)` and `Y = Spec(k)` are spectra of fields,
`K` being a finite non-separable extension of `k`. When `f : X ‚Üí Y` is a finite √©tale morphism, one also says that `X`
is an *√©tale cover* of `Y`. One will note that in that case, `f` is universally open and universally closed, and in
particular `f(X)` is a subset of `Y` both open and closed.

One says that an √©tale cover `X` of `Y` is *trivial* if `X` is a sum of a finite number of preschemes isomorphic to `Y`.
One says that an √©tale cover `X` of `Y` is *locally trivial* if the morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y` is such that every point
`y ‚ąą Y` has an open neighbourhood `U` for which the cover `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` of `U` is trivial.

(ii) Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a finite morphism, locally free of rank `n`; put `‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)` and let `u = astr_{‚Ą¨/ūĚí™_Y} : ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí ‚Ą¨ŐĆ`;
from this one deduces a homomorphism of `n`-th exterior power `őõ^n u : őõ^n ‚Ą¨ ‚Üí őõ^n ‚Ą¨ŐĆ = (őõ^n ‚Ą¨)ŐĆ` between invertible
`ūĚí™_Y`-Modules, and consequently `(0_I, 5.4.2)` an element

```text
  (18.2.7.1)    d_{X/Y} ‚ąą őď(Y, (őõ^n ‚Ą¨ŐĆ) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_Y} (őõ^n ‚Ą¨ŐĆ))
```

called the *discriminant* of `X` over `Y`. Moreover, since `(őõ^n ‚Ą¨ŐĆ) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_Y} (őõ^n ‚Ą¨ŐĆ)` is the dual of
`(őõ^n ‚Ą¨) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_Y} (őõ^n ‚Ą¨)`, `d_{X/Y}` may also be identified with a homomorphism

```text
  (18.2.7.2)    (őõ^n ‚Ą¨) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_Y} (őõ^n ‚Ą¨) ‚Üí ūĚí™_Y
```

and one denotes by `ūĚíü_{X/Y}` the quasi-coherent Ideal of finite type of `ūĚí™_Y`, image of the homomorphism `(18.2.7.2)`,
also called the *discriminant Ideal* of `X` over `Y`.

<!-- original page 114 -->

That being so, for the homomorphism `u` to be bijective, it is necessary and sufficient that `őõ^n u` be bijective, or
again that the section `d_{X/Y}` have an invertible germ at every point `y ‚ąą Y`, which one also writes
`d_{X/Y}(y) ‚Č† 0` for every `y` `(0_I, 5.5.2)`. It also amounts to the same to say that the discriminant Ideal `ūĚíü_{X/Y}`
is equal to `ūĚí™_Y`.

The terminology of "cover" introduced in `(18.2.7, (i))` is justified by the following proposition:

**Proposition (18.2.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.8 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism √©tale, separated and of finite type, and for every `y ‚ąą Y` let `n(y)` be the geometric
number of points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`. Then the function `y ‚Ü¶ n(y)` is lower semi-continuous in `Y`. For it to be continuous at a
point `y` (hence constant in a neighbourhood of `y`), it is necessary and sufficient that there exist an open
neighbourhood `U` of `y` such that the restriction `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí U` of `f` be a finite (√©tale) morphism.*

Since `f` is quasi-finite `(17.6.1)` and locally of finite presentation, it amounts to the same to say that `f` is
finite or that `f` is proper `(8.11.1)`: in addition, each fibre `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` is geometrically reduced over `k(y)`. The
conclusions then follow from `(15.5.9, (i) and (ii))` and from the fact that `f` is flat.

**Corollary (18.2.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.2.9 -->

*Let `Y` be a connected prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a morphism √©tale, separated and of finite type. For `f` to be finite (in
other words, for `X` to be an √©tale cover of `Y`), it is necessary and sufficient that all the fibres of `f` have the
same geometric number of points.*

**Remarks (18.2.10).** ‚ÄĒ (i) The example of the "affine line with one point doubled" `(I, 5.5.11)` shows that an √©tale
morphism of finite type of Noetherian preschemes can be *non-separated*; for this example, the first assertion of
`(18.2.8)` no longer holds.

(ii) For a separated, √©tale morphism of finite type `f : X ‚Üí Y` to make `X` into a *locally trivial* cover, it is
necessary and sufficient that for every `x ‚ąą X` there exist an open neighbourhood `U` of `y = f(x)` and a `U`-section
`g` of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` such that `g(y) = x`. Indeed, the condition is obviously necessary; the fact that it is sufficient
follows from the fact that every fibre `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` is finite (`(17.6.1)` and `(I, 6.2.2)`), from the characterization of
sections of an √©tale `Y`-scheme `(17.9.3)`, and from prop. `(18.2.8)`.

### 18.3. Finite √©tale algebras

**Proposition (18.3.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.3.1 -->

*Let `A` be a ring, `B` an `A`-algebra of finite presentation.*

*(i) For `B` to be an unramified `A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient that `B` be an `A`-module of finite
presentation and that `B` be a projective `(B ‚äó_A B)`-module.*

*(ii) Suppose moreover that `B` is a finite `A`-algebra. For `B` to be an √©tale `A`-algebra, it is necessary and
sufficient that `B` be a projective `A`-module and a projective `(B ‚äó_A B)`-module.*

It is understood that the `(B ‚äó_A B)`-module structure on `B` is the one coming from the `(B ‚äó_A B)`-algebra structure
on `B` corresponding to the canonical `A`-homomorphism of rings `B ‚äó_A B ‚Üí B`, which is surjective and of kernel
`ūĚĒć_{B/A}` `(0, 20.4.1)`.

(i) To say that the morphism `Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(A)` is locally of finite presentation is equivalent to saying that `B` is
an `A`-algebra of finite presentation `(1.4.6)`. To say

<!-- original page 115 -->

that `B` is an unramified `A`-algebra means then `(17.4.2)` that `Spec((B ‚äó_A B)/ūĚĒć_{B/A})` is a sub-scheme induced on an
open and closed subset of `Spec(B ‚äó_A B)`, and one knows that for this to be so, it is necessary and sufficient that
`ūĚĒć_{B/A}` be a direct factor ideal of `B ‚äó_A B` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß4, n¬į 3, prop. 15); but it amounts
to the same to say that the `(B ‚äó_A B)`-module quotient `(B ‚äó_A B)/ūĚĒć_{B/A}` is projective (Bourbaki, _Alg._, chap. II,
3rd ed., ¬ß2, n¬į 2, prop. 4).

(ii) If one recalls that a flat `A`-module of finite presentation is projective and conversely (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._,
chap. II, ¬ß5, n¬į 2, cor. 2 of th. 1), the assertion of (ii) follows from that of (i) and from `(17.6.2)`.

**Proposition (18.3.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.3.2 -->

*Let `A` be a ring, `ūĚĒć` an ideal of `A` such that, for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology, `A` is separated and complete; put
`A_0 = A/ūĚĒć`. Then the functor*

```text
  B ‚Ü¶ B ‚äó_A A_0
```

*is an equivalence from the category of finite √©tale `A`-algebras to the category of finite √©tale `A_0`-algebras.*

We shall first prove the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.3.2.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.3.2.1 -->

*Let `A` be a ring, `ūĚĒć` an ideal of `A` such that, for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology, `A` is separated and complete.*

*(i) Every projective `A`-module of finite type `M` is separated and complete for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology, hence the
projective limit of the projective `(A/ūĚĒć^{n+1})`-modules `M/ūĚĒć^{n+1} M`.*

*(ii) Conversely, put `A_n = A/ūĚĒć^{n+1}`, and let `(M_n)` be a projective system of `A_n`-modules such that, for each
`n`, the homomorphism `M_{n+1} ‚äó_{A_{n+1}} A_n ‚Üí M_n` deduced from the di-homomorphism of transition
`M_{n+1} ‚Üí M_n` is bijective. Suppose moreover that the `M_n` are projective and `M_0` of finite type. Then
`M = lim_‚Üź M_n` is a projective `A`-module of finite type such that the canonical homomorphism `M ‚äó_A A_0 ‚Üí M_0` is
bijective.*

(i) There exists a free `A`-module of finite type `L` such that `M` is isomorphic to a direct factor of `L`; since `L`
is separated for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology, so is every submodule `N` of `L`, since `ūĚĒć^{n+1} N ‚äā ūĚĒć^{n+1} L`; in
particular `M` is separated for this topology, and since the surjective homomorphism `f : L ‚Üí M` is continuous for the
`ūĚĒć`-preadic topology, its kernel `N` is closed for the topology induced by that of `L`; since `L` is complete and `f` a
strict morphism, one concludes that `M` is complete (Bourbaki, _Top. g√©n._, chap. IX, 2nd ed., ¬ß3, n¬į 1, prop. 4).

(ii) It follows from Nakayama's lemma that if `M_0` is generated by a finite family `(x_{i,0})` of `r` elements and if
for each `n`, `x_{i,n}` is an element of `M_n` whose image in `M_{n-1}` is `x_{i, n-1}`, then `(x_{i,n})_{1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r}` is
a system of generators of `M_n` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß3, cor. 2 of prop. 4). That being so, for each `n`,
put `L_n = A_n^r`; if `(e_{i,n})_{1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r}` is the canonical basis of `L_n`, let `u_n : L_n ‚Üí M_n` be the
`A`-linear map such that `u_n(e_{i,n}) = x_{i,n}` for each `i`. By hypothesis one has a split exact sequence

```text
  0 ‚Üí N_n ‚Üí^{v_n} L_n ‚Üí^{u_n} M_n ‚Üí 0
```

<!-- original page 116 -->

and since `L_n = L_{n+1}/ūĚĒć^{n+1} L_{n+1}` and `M_n = M_{n+1}/ūĚĒć^{n+1} M_{n+1}`, the vertical arrows in the commutative
diagram

```text
  0 ‚Üí N_{n+1} ‚Üí^{v_{n+1}} L_{n+1} ‚Üí^{u_{n+1}} M_{n+1} ‚Üí 0
              ‚Üď                  ‚Üď                    ‚Üď
  0 ‚Üí N_n     ‚Üí^{v_n}     L_n    ‚Üí^{u_n}     M_n      ‚Üí 0
```

are all three *surjective*. Now one has `M = lim_‚Üź M_n` and `L = A^r = lim_‚Üź L_n`; if one puts `N = lim_‚Üź N_n`,
`u = lim_‚Üź u_n`, `v = lim_‚Üź v_n`, one has, by virtue of `(0_III, 13.2.2)`, the exact sequence

```text
  (18.3.2.2)    0 ‚Üí N ‚Üí^v L ‚Üí^u M ‚Üí 0.
```

Moreover, since for each `n`, `v_n` is left-invertible and `M_n` is a projective `A_n`-module, it follows from
`(0, 19.1.8)` that the exact sequence `(18.3.2.2)` is split, which proves the lemma.

That being so, let us show first that the functor in the statement of `(18.3.2)` is *fully faithful*. Put, as in the
lemma, `A_n = A/ūĚĒć^{n+1}`; let `B`, `C` be two finite √©tale `A`-algebras, and put, for each `n`, `B_n = B ‚äó_A A_n`,
`C_n = C ‚äó_A A_n`; by virtue of `(18.3.1)` and `(18.3.2.1)`, `B` and `C` are separated and complete for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic
topology, and one has `B = lim_‚Üź B_n`, `C = lim_‚Üź C_n`; moreover every homomorphism of `A`-algebras `u : B ‚Üí C` is
continuous for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topologies, and therefore gives a projective system of homomorphisms of `A_n`-algebras
`u_n = u ‚äó 1 : B_n ‚Üí C_n` of which it is the projective limit; the converse being obvious, one has therefore a
canonical bijection

```text
  Hom_{A-alg.}(B, C) ‚•≤ lim_‚Üź Hom_{A_n-alg.}(B_n, C_n).
```

But since `B` and `C` are *√©tale* `A`-algebras, it follows at once from `(18.1.2)` that the canonical map

```text
  Hom_{A_{n+1}-alg.}(B_{n+1}, C_{n+1}) ‚Üí Hom_{A_n-alg.}(B_n, C_n)
```

is bijective for `n ‚©ĺ 0`, which finishes proving that the canonical map
`Hom_{A-alg.}(B, C) ‚Üí Hom_{A_0-alg.}(B_0, C_0)` is bijective.

To complete the proof of `(18.3.2)`, it suffices to see that for every finite √©tale `A_0`-algebra `B_0`, there exist a
finite √©tale `A`-algebra `B` and an `A_0`-isomorphism `B_0 ‚•≤ B ‚äó_A A_0`. Now, it follows from `(18.1.2)` that there is a
projective system `(B_n)` such that `B_n` is a finite √©tale `A_n`-algebra and the homomorphisms
`B_{n+1} ‚äó_{A_{n+1}} A_n ‚Üí B_n` are bijective. It follows from `(18.3.1)` and `(18.3.2.1)` that the `A`-algebra
`B = lim_‚Üź B_n` is a projective `A`-module of finite type and that `B_0` is isomorphic to `B ‚äó_A A_0`. To prove that
`B` is an √©tale `A`-algebra, it suffices, by virtue of `(18.2.5)`, to show that for every maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™` of `A`,
`B_ūĚĒ™/ūĚĒ™ B_ūĚĒ™` is a separable `(A/ūĚĒ™)`-algebra. Now, since `ūĚĒć` is contained in the radical of `A` `(0_I, 7.1.10)`,

<!-- original page 117 -->

one has `ūĚĒć ‚äā ūĚĒ™`, and if `ūĚĒ™_0 = ūĚĒ™/ūĚĒć`, one has `A_0/ūĚĒ™_0 = A/ūĚĒ™` and `B_ūĚĒ™/ūĚĒ™ B_ūĚĒ™ = (B_0)_{ūĚĒ™_0}/ūĚĒ™_0 (B_0)_{ūĚĒ™_0}`; the
conclusion therefore follows from the fact that `B_0` is a finite √©tale `A_0`-algebra `(18.2.5)`.

**Example (18.3.3).** ‚ÄĒ Prop. `(18.3.2)` applies in particular when `A` is a *separated and complete local ring*, `ūĚĒć`
being the maximal ideal of `A`, so that `A_0` is a *field* and the category of finite √©tale `A_0`-algebras is identical
to that of finite separable `A_0`-algebras, hence isomorphic to direct composites of fields, separable and finite
extensions of `A_0`. In particular, if the field `A_0` is *separably closed*, these extensions are all identical to
`A_0`, and consequently every √©tale cover of `Spec(A)` is *trivial* `(18.2.7)` by virtue of `(18.3.2)`.

**Theorem (18.3.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.3.4 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian ring, `ūĚĒć` an ideal of `A` such that `A` is separated and complete for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology,
`A_0 = A/ūĚĒć`. Put `S = Spec(A)`, `S_0 = Spec(A_0)`. Let `X` be an `S`-scheme proper over `S`, and put `X_0 = X √ó_S S_0`.
Then the functor*

```text
  Z ‚Ü¶ Z √ó_X X_0
```

*from the category of `X`-schemes finite and √©tale over `X` to the category of `X_0`-schemes finite and √©tale over
`X_0` is an equivalence of categories.*

Let us show first that this functor is *fully faithful*. Let `Z'` and `Z''` be two `X`-schemes finite and √©tale over
`X`. Put `S_n = Spec(A/ūĚĒć^{n+1})`, `X_n = X √ó_S S_n`, `Z'_n = Z' √ó_S S_n`, `Z''_n = Z'' √ó_S S_n` for each `n ‚©ĺ 0`. It
follows from `(III, 5.4.1)` that one has a canonical bijection
`Hom_X(Z', Z'') ‚•≤ lim_‚Üź Hom_{X_n}(Z'_n, Z''_n)`. Now, by virtue of `(18.1.2)`, the canonical map
`Hom_{X_{n+1}}(Z'_{n+1}, Z''_{n+1}) ‚Üí Hom_{X_n}(Z'_n, Z''_n)` is bijective, which completes the proof of our assertion.

It remains to prove that if `‚Ą¨_0` is a finite √©tale `ūĚí™_{X_0}`-Algebra, there exists an `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra `‚Ą¨` finite and
√©tale and an isomorphism `‚Ą¨_0 ‚•≤ ‚Ą¨ ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X_0}`. It follows from `(18.1.2)` that there is a projective system
`(‚Ą¨_n)`, where `‚Ą¨_n` is a finite √©tale `ūĚí™_{X_n}`-Algebra, and the second comparison theorem `(III, 5.1.4)` proves the
existence of a coherent `ūĚí™_X`-Module `‚Ą¨` and of a projective system of isomorphisms `‚Ą¨_n ‚•≤ ‚Ą¨ ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X_n}`. The
datum of an Algebra structure on a Module `‚ĄĪ` being equivalent to that of a homomorphism `‚ĄĪ ‚äó ‚ĄĪ ‚Üí ‚ĄĪ` making commutative
diagrams in which only tensor powers of `‚ĄĪ` intervene, it follows from `(III, 5.1.3)`, `(I, 10.11.4)` and
`(I, 10.11.7)` that `‚Ą¨` is naturally endowed with an `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra structure for which the isomorphism
`‚Ą¨_n ‚•≤ ‚Ą¨ ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X_n}` is an isomorphism of Algebras. Moreover, `‚Ą¨` is a *locally free* `ūĚí™_X`-Module; this also
follows from `(III, 5.1.3)`, `(I, 10.11.4)`, `(I, 10.11.7)` and from the fact that, in the category of coherent
`ūĚí™_X`-Modules, locally free `ūĚí™_X`-Modules `‚ĄĪ` may be defined as those for which the functor `ūĚíĘ ‚Ü¶ ‚Ąčom_{ūĚí™_X}(‚ĄĪ, ūĚíĘ)` is
exact. Finally, to see that `‚Ą¨` is an √©tale `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra, it suffices `(18.2.5)` to show that for every closed point
`x ‚ąą X`, `‚Ą¨_x ‚äó_{ūĚí™_{X,x}} k(x)` is a separable `k(x)`-algebra. But since the structure morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` is proper,
`f(x)` is a closed point of `S`, hence belongs to `S_0`, since `ūĚĒć` is contained in the radical of `A` `(0_I, 7.1.10)`;
the conclusion therefore follows from the fact that `X_0 = f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0)` and that `‚Ą¨_0` is a finite √©tale
`ūĚí™_{X_0}`-Algebra.

<!-- original page 118 -->

### 18.4. Local structure of unramified and √©tale morphisms

**Lemma (18.4.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.1 -->

*Let `A` be a ring, `B` a finite monogenic `A`-algebra, `u` a generator of the `A`-algebra `B`, `F ‚ąą A[T]` a polynomial
such that `F(u) = 0`, `F'` the derived polynomial; put `u' = F'(u)`. Then the ideal of `B`, annihilator of
`ő©^1_{B/A}`, contains `u' B`; it is equal to `u' B` if the ideal `ūĚĒć` of `A[T]` formed by the polynomials `G` such that
`G(u) = 0` is generated by `F`, in other words if the canonical surjective homomorphism
`ŌÜ : A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T] ‚Üí B` transforming the image of `T` into `u` is bijective.*

Put `C = A[T]`, so that `B = C/ūĚĒć`. One has the exact sequence `(0, 20.5.12.1)`

```text
  ūĚĒć/ūĚĒć¬≤ ‚Üí ő©^1_{C/A} ‚äó_C B ‚Üí ő©^1_{B/A} ‚Üí 0
```

and `ő©^1_{B/A}` is therefore identified with the quotient `B/ūĚĒć'`, `ūĚĒć'` being the ideal generated by the elements
`G'(u)`, where `G` ranges over a system of generators of the ideal `ūĚĒć` `(0, 20.5.13)`; the lemma follows immediately.

**Proposition (18.4.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.2 -->

*With the notations of `(18.4.1)`, let `ūĚĒģ` be a prime ideal of `B`. Then:*

*(i) If `ūĚĒģ` does not contain `u'`, `B_ūĚĒģ` is a formally unramified `A_ūĚĒ≠`-algebra (`ūĚĒ≠` being the inverse image of `ūĚĒģ` in
`A`); in other words, `Spec(B_ūĚĒģ)` is formally unramified over `Spec(A)`.*

*(ii) Suppose moreover that `F` is unitary and generates `ūĚĒć`. Then, for `Spec(B)` to be √©tale over `Spec(A)` at the
point `ūĚĒģ`, it is necessary and sufficient that `u' ‚ąČ ūĚĒģ`.*

The hypothesis that `u' ‚ąČ ūĚĒģ` entails that `ő©^1_{B_ūĚĒģ/A_ūĚĒ≠} = 0` `(0, 20.5.9)`, hence (i) follows from `(17.2.1)`. Moreover,
under the hypotheses of (ii), `B` is a free `A`-module by virtue of Euclidean division; since the annihilator `ūĚĒć'` of
`ő©^1_{B/A}` is then equal to `u' B` by virtue of `(18.4.1)`, and `ő©^1_{B/A}` is a `B`-module of finite presentation
`(16.4.22)`, the annihilator of `ő©^1_{B_ūĚĒģ/A_ūĚĒ≠}` is equal to `u' B_ūĚĒģ` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß2, n¬į 4, formula
(9)), and (ii) follows therefore from (i) and from the implication c) ‚áí a) in `(17.6.1)`.

**Corollary (18.4.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.3 -->

*With the notations of `(18.4.2)`, suppose that `F` is unitary and generates `ūĚĒć`. Then, for `B` to be an √©tale
`A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient that `u'` be invertible in `B` (or, what amounts to the same, that the ideal
of `A[T]` generated by `F` and `F'` be equal to `A[T]`).*

Taking `(18.4.2, (ii))` into account, to say that `Spec(B)` is √©tale over `Spec(A)` means indeed that `u'` does not
belong to any prime ideal of `B`, i.e. that it is invertible in `B`.

One says that a unitary polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` such that the ideal of `A[T]` generated by `F` and `F'` is equal to `A[T]`
itself is *separable*; it is immediate that this definition coincides with the usual definition (Bourbaki, _Alg._, chap.
V, ¬ß7, n¬į 6) when `A` is a field.

**Lemma (18.4.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.4 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `B` a finite monogenic `A`-algebra, `u` a generator of the `A`-algebra `B`. Let `ūĚĒę_i`
`(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)` be maximal ideals of `B` such that `Spec(B)` is formally unramified over `Spec(A)` at the points `ūĚĒę_i`.
Then there exists a unitary polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` such that `F(u) = 0` and `F'(u) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę_i` for every index `i`
`(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)`. Moreover, if `k` is the residue field*

<!-- original page 119 -->

*of `A`, `f ‚ąą k[T]` the minimal polynomial of the image of `u` in `B ‚äó_A k`, there exists an `F ‚ąą A[T]` of which `f` is
the canonical image and such that `F(u) = 0`; such a polynomial `F` verifies the conditions `F'(u) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę_i` for
`1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r`.*

The maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™` of `A` is the inverse image of each of the `ūĚĒę_i` `(II, 6.1.10)`; let `L = B ‚äó_A k`, which is a
finite `k`-algebra. Let `ő∂` be the image of `u` in `L`, and let `n` be the rank of `L` over `k`; the minimal polynomial
`f ‚ąą k[T]` of `ő∂` over `k` is therefore of degree `n`, and `L` is isomorphic to `k[T]/f ¬∑ k[T]`. If
`ūĚĒę'_i = ūĚĒę_i/ūĚĒ™ B`, the hypothesis entails that `Spec(L)` is √©tale over `k` at the points `ūĚĒę'_i` `(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)` by virtue
of `(17.6.1, c)`, hence `f'(ő∂) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę'_i` by virtue of `(18.4.2, (ii))`. Note now that `ūĚĒ™ B` is contained in the radical of
`B`; since `1, ő∂, ‚Ä¶, ő∂^{n-1}` form a basis of `L` over `k`, it follows from Nakayama's lemma that `1, u, ‚Ä¶, u^{n-1}`
generate the `A`-module `B`, and consequently there exists a unitary polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` of degree `n` such that
`F(u) = 0`; in addition, since `ő∂` is a root of the canonical image of `F` in `k[T]`, this image is necessarily equal to
`f`. But then the image of `F'(u)` in `L` is `f'(ő∂)`, and since `f'(ő∂) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę'_i`, one has `F'(u) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę_i` for each `i`.

**Proposition (18.4.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.5 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `k` its residue field, `B` a finite (resp. finite and of finite presentation) `A`-algebra.
Suppose moreover either that the field `k` is infinite, or that `B` is a local ring. Let `n` be the rank of
`L = B ‚äó_A k` over `k`. For `B` to be a formally unramified (resp. √©tale) `A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient
that there exist a unitary separable polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` `(18.4.3)` such that `B` is isomorphic to a quotient of
`A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]` (resp. isomorphic to `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`). Moreover, one may suppose `F` of degree `n` (resp. `F` is
necessarily of degree `n`).*

The conditions are sufficient by virtue of `(18.4.2)`, without assuming `k` infinite or `B` local. To see that the
conditions are necessary, note that if `B` is a formally unramified `A`-algebra, `L` is a finite and separable algebra
over `k`, hence the direct composite of a finite number of finite and separable extensions `k_j` of `k`
`(1 ‚©Ĺ j ‚©Ĺ r)`, `k_j` being therefore generated by an element `őĺ_j` of minimal polynomial `f_j` `(1 ‚©Ĺ j ‚©Ĺ r)` (Bourbaki,
_Alg._, chap. V, ¬ß11, n¬į 4, prop. 4). Let us show that by virtue of the hypotheses made on `k` or `B`, there exists an
element `ő∂` of `L` generating the `k`-algebra `L`. This is immediate if `B` is local, since then `r = 1`. Otherwise,
`k` being supposed infinite, one may suppose that the irreducible polynomials `f_j ‚ąą k[T]` are all distinct (by
replacing if necessary each `őĺ_j` by `őĺ_j + a_j`, for a suitable element `a_j ‚ąą k`); if one puts `f = f_1 f_2 ‚čĮ f_r`, it
is clear that `L` is isomorphic to `k[T]/f ¬∑ k[T]` in both cases considered, hence is generated by an element `ő∂` of
minimal polynomial `f ‚ąą k[T]` of degree `n`. If `u ‚ąą B` is an element whose image is `ő∂` in `L`, Nakayama's lemma shows
that the elements `1, u, ‚Ä¶, u^{n-1}` generate the `A`-module `B`; this already shows that there exists a unitary
polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` of degree `n` such that `F(u) = 0`, `u` generating the `A`-algebra `B`, which is consequently
isomorphic to a quotient algebra of `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`; moreover `B` is a semi-local ring, and at each of its maximal
ideals `ūĚĒę_i` one has `F'(u) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę_i` by `(18.4.4)`, which proves that `F'(u)` is invertible in `B`, hence that `F` is a
separable polynomial. Finally, if `B` is an √©tale `A`-algebra, `B` being a flat `A`-module of finite presentation
`(1.4.7)` is a free `A`-module, and `1, u, ‚Ä¶, u^{n-1}` form a basis of the `A`-module `B` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._,
chap. II, ¬ß3, n¬į 2, prop. 5), in other words the `A`-algebra `B` is

<!-- original page 120 -->

isomorphic to `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`, and for every other unitary polynomial `G ‚ąą A[T]` such that `B` is isomorphic to
`A[T]/G ¬∑ A[T]`, `G` is necessarily of degree `n`.

**Theorem (18.4.6) (Chevalley).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.6 -->

*(i) Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite type, `x` a point of `X`, `y = f(x)`, and put `A = ūĚí™_{Y, y}`. For
`ūĚí™_{X, x}` to be a formally √©tale (resp. formally unramified) `A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient that there
exist a unitary polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]` and a maximal ideal `ūĚĒę` of `B = A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]` (resp. of a quotient algebra `B` of
`A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`) such that `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is `A`-isomorphic to `B_ūĚĒę` and such that, if `u` is the image of `T` in `B`, one
has `F'(u) ‚ąČ ūĚĒę`.*

*(ii) Suppose moreover that `f` is locally of finite presentation. Then, for `f` to be √©tale at the point `x`, it is
necessary and sufficient that one can in addition take `B = A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`.*

The conditions are sufficient by virtue of `(18.4.2)`. To see that they are necessary, one may obviously restrict to
the case where `X` and `Y` are affine, and, taking remark `(17.4.1.2)` into account, to the case where `f` is formally
unramified and quasi-finite. Since `f` is affine, it follows from `(8.12.8)` that there exist a finite `A`-algebra `C`
and a maximal ideal `ūĚĒĮ` of `C` (necessarily above the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™` of `A`) such that `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is `A`-isomorphic
to `C_ūĚĒĮ`. Moreover `(17.4.1.2)`, the residue field `C/ūĚĒĮ = C_ūĚĒĮ/ūĚĒĮ C_ūĚĒĮ` is a finite separable extension of `k = A/ūĚĒ™`, hence
of the form `k[v]`, where `v` is separable over `k`. Let `ūĚĒĮ_i` `(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ h)` be the maximal ideals of the semi-local
ring `C` other than `ūĚĒĮ`; there exists an element `u ‚ąą C` belonging to all the `ūĚĒĮ_i` and such that its image in `C/ūĚĒĮ`
is equal to `v` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß1, n¬į 2, prop. 5). We shall show that the sub-`A`-algebra
`B = A[u]` of `C` and the ideal (necessarily maximal since `C` is finite over `B`) `ūĚĒę = ūĚĒĮ ‚ą© B` of `B` answer the
question.

To handle the case where `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a formally unramified `A`-algebra, it will suffice to prove that `B_ūĚĒę` is
isomorphic to `C_ūĚĒĮ`; indeed, `B_ūĚĒę` will then be formally unramified over `A`, and the existence of the polynomial
`F ‚ąą A[T]` having the properties of the statement will follow from `(18.4.4)`. Note now that, since `f` is formally
unramified, `C/ūĚĒĮ` is isomorphic to the `A`-algebra `C_ūĚĒĮ/ūĚĒ™ C_ūĚĒĮ` `(17.4.1.2)`. One is thus reduced to proving the
following lemma:

**Lemma (18.4.6.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.6.1 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `ūĚĒ™` its maximal ideal, `C` a finite `A`-algebra, `ūĚĒĮ` a maximal ideal of `C`. Let `u` be an
element of `C` belonging to all the maximal ideals of `C` distinct from `ūĚĒĮ`, not belonging to `ūĚĒĮ`, and such that
`C_ūĚĒĮ/ūĚĒ™ C_ūĚĒĮ` is a monogenic algebra over `k = A/ūĚĒ™`, generated by the image of `u` in `C_ūĚĒĮ/ūĚĒ™ C_ūĚĒĮ`. Put `B = A[u]`,
`ūĚĒę = ūĚĒĮ ‚ą© B`. Then the canonical homomorphism `B_ūĚĒę ‚Üí C_ūĚĒĮ` is an isomorphism.*

Put `R = B ‚ąí ūĚĒę`, `S = C ‚ąí ūĚĒĮ`, so that `B_ūĚĒę = R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B` and `C_ūĚĒĮ = S‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C`; the canonical homomorphism `B_ūĚĒę ‚Üí C_ūĚĒĮ` may be
written as the composite

```text
  R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B ‚Üí R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C ‚Üí S‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C
```

and it suffices to show that each of these two homomorphisms is bijective.

Let us first show that `h : R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C ‚Üí S‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C = C_ūĚĒĮ` is bijective; it suffices to see that the images in `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` of the
elements of `S` are invertible, or again that every maximal ideal `ūĚĒ≠` of `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` has an inverse image in `C` not meeting
`S`, hence necessarily equal to `ūĚĒĮ`. Now, since `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` is a `(R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B)`-algebra finite, the inverse image of `ūĚĒ≠` in

<!-- original page 121 -->

`R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B = B_ūĚĒę` is the unique maximal ideal `ūĚĒę B_ūĚĒę` of this ring, hence the inverse image of `ūĚĒ≠` in `B` is equal to `ūĚĒę`.
But on the other hand, if `ūĚĒģ` is the inverse image of `ūĚĒ≠` in `C`, one has `ūĚĒģ ‚ą© B = ūĚĒę`, and since `ūĚĒę` is maximal in `B`
and `C` is a `B`-algebra finite, `ūĚĒģ` is necessarily one of the maximal ideals of `C`; in addition, one has `u ‚ąČ ūĚĒģ`
since `u ‚ąČ ūĚĒę` and `u ‚ąą B`, hence by hypothesis one has necessarily `ūĚĒģ = ūĚĒĮ`.

On the other hand, since `B ‚äā C`, the homomorphism `g : R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B ‚Üí R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` is injective `(0_I, 1.3.2)`; to see that it is
surjective, note that `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` is a `(R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B)`-module of finite type, and on the other hand that `ūĚĒ™ R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B` is contained in
the maximal ideal of the local ring `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B`; by virtue of Nakayama's lemma, it suffices to prove that the homomorphism
`R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B/ūĚĒ™ R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B ‚Üí R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C/ūĚĒ™ R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` is surjective. But by virtue of the first part of the proof, `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C/ūĚĒ™ R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ C` is
identified with `C_ūĚĒĮ/ūĚĒ™ C_ūĚĒĮ`; by hypothesis this `k`-algebra is generated by the image of `u`, and a fortiori it is
equal to the image of `R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B/ūĚĒ™ R‚ĀĽ¬Ļ B`.

Consider in the second place the case where `f` is √©tale at the point `x`. Replacing `X` by a neighbourhood of `x`, one
may suppose that `X` is a neighbourhood of `ūĚĒę` in `Spec(B)` `(1.7.2)`. Put `B' = Spec(A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T])` and let `ūĚĒę'` be
the inverse image of `ūĚĒę` in `B'`; since the image of `F'(T)` in `B'` does not belong to `ūĚĒę'` by hypothesis, the
morphism `Spec(B') ‚Üí Spec(A)` is √©tale at the point `ūĚĒę'` by `(18.4.2, (ii))`. Since by hypothesis
`Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(A)` is √©tale at the point `ūĚĒę`, one concludes `(17.3.4)` that `Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(B')` is √©tale at the
point `ūĚĒę`; but since this morphism is an immersion, it can be √©tale at a point only if it is a local isomorphism at
that point `(17.9.1)`, hence `B_ūĚĒę` and `B'_{ūĚĒę'}` are isomorphic.

Finally, suppose that `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a formally √©tale `A`-algebra; with the previous notations, it follows from
`(17.1.5)` that `B_ūĚĒę` is a formally √©tale `B_ūĚĒę`-algebra; but since the homomorphism `B_ūĚĒę ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` is surjective,
this can hold only if this homomorphism is bijective `(0, 19.10.3, (i))`. This completes the proof of `(18.4.6)`.

**Corollary (18.4.7).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.7 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite type, `x` a point of `X`. For `f` to be formally unramified at the
point `x`, it is necessary and sufficient that there exist an open neighbourhood `U` of `x` such that `f | U` factors
as `U ‚Üí^j X' ‚Üí^h Y`, where `h` is an √©tale morphism and `j` a closed immersion.*

One may obviously restrict to the case where `Y = Spec(R)` is affine and `f` of finite type. If `A = ūĚí™_{Y, y}` with
`y = f(x)`, the condition for `f` to be formally unramified at the point `x` is equivalent, by virtue of `(17.4.1.2)`,
to saying that `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a formally unramified `A`-algebra. If this is so, one may apply `(18.4.6, (i))`; replacing
if necessary `Y` by an affine neighbourhood of `y`, one may suppose (with the notations of `(18.4.6)`) that the
polynomial `F` is the image in `A[T]` of a unitary polynomial `G ‚ąą R[T]`. One then puts `X' = Spec(R[T]/G ¬∑ R[T])`;
let `x'` be the image of the point `ūĚĒę` of `Spec(B)` under the morphism corresponding to the composite homomorphism
`R[T]/G ¬∑ R[T] ‚Üí A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T] ‚Üí B`. It follows from `(18.4.2)` that the morphism `h : X' ‚Üí Y` corresponding to the
canonical homomorphism `R ‚Üí R[T]/G ¬∑ R[T]` is √©tale at the point `x'`, hence, by restricting if necessary `X'` and
`Y` to open neighbourhoods of `x'` and `y` respectively, one may suppose `h` √©tale. On the other hand, by virtue of
`(I, 6.5.1, (ii))` and of `(1.7.2)`, it follows from the fact that one has a local homomorphism
`ŌÜ : ūĚí™_{X', x'} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` that this homomorphism corresponds to a

<!-- original page 122 -->

morphism `j : U ‚Üí X'` of an open neighbourhood `U` of `x` in `X'`; by restricting `U` if necessary, one may, by
applying `(I, 6.5.1, (i))`, suppose that `h ‚ąė j = f | U`, hence `j` is of finite type `(I, 6.3.4, (v))`; finally, the
homomorphism `ŌÜ` is surjective by `(18.4.6, (i))`, hence it follows from `(I, 6.5.4, (i))` that one may, by restricting
`U` and `X'` again, suppose that `j` is a closed immersion. This proves therefore the necessity of the stated condition;
its sufficiency is immediate `(17.1.3, (i) and (ii))`.

**Corollary (18.4.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.8 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite presentation. For `f` to be unramified (resp. √©tale), it is necessary
and sufficient that there exist a family of flat morphisms `g_őĪ : Y_őĪ ‚Üí Y` and, for each `őĪ`, an open set `U_őĪ` in
`X_őĪ = X √ó_Y Y_őĪ`, such that, if `g'_őĪ : X_őĪ ‚Üí X` and `f_őĪ : X_őĪ ‚Üí Y_őĪ` are the canonical projections, the `g'_őĪ(U_őĪ)`
form a cover of `X`, and each of the composite morphisms `U_őĪ ‚Üí X_őĪ ‚Üí Y_őĪ` is a closed immersion (resp. an open
immersion). One may moreover then take the `g_őĪ` √©tale.*

The necessity of the condition for √©tale morphisms is trivial, by taking a single `Y_őĪ` equal to `X`, the morphism
`g_őĪ` being equal to `f`, and the open set `U ‚äā X √ó_Y X` being the diagonal. When `f` is unramified, the necessity of
the condition follows from `(18.4.7)`; one takes an open cover `(V_őĪ)` of `X` such that for each `őĪ`, `f | V_őĪ` factors
as `V_őĪ ‚Üí^{j_őĪ} Y_őĪ ‚Üí Y`, where `j_őĪ` is a closed immersion and `g_őĪ` an √©tale morphism. Then
`j_őĪ : V_őĪ ‚Üí Y_őĪ` factors as `V_őĪ ‚Üí^{s_őĪ} X_őĪ ‚Üí^{f_őĪ} Y_őĪ`, where `s_őĪ` is a `V_őĪ`-section of `X_őĪ`, and since the
morphism `g'_őĪ : X_őĪ ‚Üí X` is √©tale, `s_őĪ` is an open immersion `(17.4.1)`, and it suffices to take `U_őĪ = s_őĪ(V_őĪ)` to
answer the question.

The sufficiency of the conditions follows from `(17.7.1)`; one concludes from it that `f` is unramified (resp. √©tale)
at each point of `g'_őĪ(U_őĪ)`, hence in `X` entirely since the `g'_őĪ(U_őĪ)` cover `X`.

**Proposition (18.4.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.9 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí S` a morphism, `h : Y ‚Üí S` a morphism locally of finite presentation, `g : X ‚Üí Y`
an `S`-morphism, `x` a point of `X`, `y = g(x)`. The following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) `h` is √©tale at the point `y` and `g` is flat at the point `x`.*

*b) `h` is unramified at the point `y` and `f` is flat at the point `x`.*

Since `f = h ‚ąė g`, a) entails that `f` is flat at the point `x` `(2.1.6)`, and obviously that `h` is unramified at the
point `y`, hence a) implies b).

To prove that b) entails a), one may first suppose that `h` is unramified (by replacing `Y` by a neighbourhood of `y`);
then, by replacing `S` by an open neighbourhood of `s = h(y) = f(x)`, one may suppose that there exist an √©tale
morphism `u : S' ‚Üí S`, a point `y'` of `Y' = Y √ó_S S'` above `y`, and an open neighbourhood `V` of `y'` in `Y'`
such that, if `h' = h_{(S')} : Y' ‚Üí S'`, the restriction of `h'` to `V` is a closed immersion `(18.4.8)`. If one then
proves that `h'` is √©tale at the point `y'`, it will follow that `h` is √©tale at the point `y` `(17.7.1, (ii))`; in
addition, `f' = f_{(S')} : X √ó_S S' ‚Üí S'` is flat at every point `x'` above `x`. Since the projections
`v : Y' ‚Üí Y`, `w : X' ‚Üí X` are √©tale morphisms (hence flat), if one proves that `g' = g_{(S')} : X_{(S')} ‚Üí Y_{(S')}`
is flat at the point `x'`, it will follow from `(2.2.11, (iv))` that `g` will be flat at the point `x`. One may
therefore restrict to the case where `h` is a closed immersion

<!-- original page 123 -->

of finite presentation, `f` being supposed flat at the point `x`. Let `ūĚí•` be the quasi-coherent Ideal of finite type of
`ūĚí™_S` which defines the closed sub-prescheme `Y` of `S`. The hypothesis that `f` is flat at the point `x` entails that
the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{S, s} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` is injective `(0_I, 6.5.1)`; a fortiori the homomorphism
`ūĚí•_s ‚Üí ūĚí™_{Y, y}` is injective, which means that `ūĚí•_s = 0`, since this is the kernel of the preceding
homomorphism. Since `ūĚí•` is of finite type, there is an open neighbourhood `U` of `s` in `S` such that `ūĚí• | U = 0`
`(0_I, 5.2.2)`. One may therefore suppose that `h` is an open immersion, and then it is clear that `g` is flat at the
point `x`.

**Proposition (18.4.10).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.10 -->

*Let `P(f, x)` denote a property verifying the following conditions:*

*1¬į For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y` and every local isomorphism `h : Y ‚Üí Z`, `P(f, x)` is equivalent to `P(h ‚ąė f, x)`
for `x ‚ąą X`.*

*2¬į For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y`, every √©tale morphism `g : Y' ‚Üí Y`, every point `x ‚ąą X`, if one puts
`X' = X √ó_Y Y'`, `f' = f_{(Y')} : X' ‚Üí Y'`, and if `x' ‚ąą X'` is above `x`, the properties `P(f, x)` and `P(f', x')`
are equivalent ("invariance under √©tale base change").*

*Let then `S` be a prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí S` and `h : Y ‚Üí S` two morphisms, `g : X ‚Üí Y` an `S`-morphism, `x` a point of
`X`, `y = g(x)`, and suppose `h` √©tale at the point `y`. Then the properties `P(f, x)` and `P(g, x)` are equivalent.*

With the same notations as in the proof of `(18.4.9)` and by replacing `S` (resp. `Y`) by a neighbourhood of `s = h(y)`
(resp. of `y`), one may here, by virtue of `(18.4.8)`, find an √©tale morphism `u : S' ‚Üí S` such that `h'` is an open
immersion; if `x' ‚ąą X'` is above `x`, `P(f', x')` (resp. `P(g', x')`) is then by hypothesis equivalent to `P(f, x)`
(resp. `P(g, x)`). One may therefore restrict to the case where `h` is an open immersion. Since the hypothesis entails
that `P(g, x)` is then equivalent to `P(h ‚ąė g, x)`, one deduces the conclusion.

**Examples (18.4.11).** ‚ÄĒ One may take for property `P(f, x)` any one of the following, taking `(17.7.4, (ii))` into
account:

(i) `f` is flat at the point `x` `(2.2.11, (iv))`;

(ii) `f` is locally of finite presentation and of codepth `‚©Ĺ n` at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(iii) `f` is locally of finite presentation and Cohen-Macaulay at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(iv) `f` is locally of finite presentation and possesses property `(S_n)` at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(v) `f` is locally of finite presentation and possesses property `(R_n)` at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(vi) `f` is locally of finite presentation and normal at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(vii) `f` is locally of finite presentation and reduced at the point `x` `(6.8.1 and 6.7.8)`;

(viii) `f` is unramified at the point `x` `(17.7.4)`;

(ix) `f` is smooth at the point `x` `(17.7.4)`;

(x) `f` is √©tale at the point `x` `(17.7.4)`.

<!-- original page 124 -->

**Corollary (18.4.12).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.12 -->

*(i) Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite type, `x` a point of `X`. If `f` is flat and formally unramified
at the point `x`, then, in every factorization `f | U : U ‚Üí^j X' ‚Üí^h Y`, where `U` is an open neighbourhood of `x`,
`j` a closed immersion and `h` an √©tale morphism `(18.4.7)`, the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{X', j(x)} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` corresponding
to `j` is bijective (in particular `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is an essentially √©tale `ūĚí™_{Y, y}`-algebra `(18.6.1)`).*

*(ii) For a morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y` to be √©tale, it is necessary and sufficient that it be locally of finite type,
formally unramified and flat.*

(i) Since the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{X', j(x)} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` is surjective, it suffices to prove that it is injective, and for
that it suffices to make `ūĚí™_{X, x}` into a faithfully flat `ūĚí™_{X', j(x)}`-module, or merely a flat one
`(0_I, 6.5.1 and 6.6.2)`; in other words, it is a matter of showing that `j` is a flat morphism at the point `x`; but
since `h ‚ąė j = f` is by hypothesis flat at the point `x` and `h` is √©tale, this follows from `(18.4.10)` and
`(18.4.11, (i))`.

(ii) There is only the sufficiency of the stated conditions to prove. For every `x ‚ąą X`, one has in an open
neighbourhood `U` of `x` a factorization of `f | U` having the properties considered in (i). Since by hypothesis `f` is
flat and formally unramified at all points of `U`, the result of (i) applies not only to `x` but to all points of `U`;
this means that if `ūĚí•` is the quasi-coherent Ideal of `ūĚí™_{X'}` corresponding to the closed sub-prescheme of `X'`
associated with `j`, one has `ūĚí•_z = 0` for every `z ‚ąą U`, hence `j` is an open immersion, and `f` is consequently
√©tale at every point of `U`, hence at every point of `X`.

**Corollary (18.4.13).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.13 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite type, `x` a point of `X`, `y = f(x)`. Suppose that `y` admits an
open neighbourhood which is a reduced prescheme having only a finite number of irreducible components. Then, for `f` to
be √©tale at the point `x`, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` be flat and formally unramified at the point `x`.*

There is only the sufficiency of the condition to prove. The question being local on `X` and `Y`, one may suppose
`(18.4.7)` that `f` factors as `X ‚Üí^j X' ‚Üí^h Y` where `h` is √©tale and `j` a closed immersion, and moreover that `Y` is
reduced and has only a finite number of irreducible components. Then `X'` is reduced `(17.5.7)` and, by replacing if
necessary `X'` by an open neighbourhood of `j(x)`, one may suppose that `X'` has only a finite number of irreducible
components: indeed, one may suppose `h` quasi-finite `(17.6.1)`, and since the maximal points of `X'` lie above the
maximal points of `Y` `(2.3.4)`, their number is finite. Everything comes down to showing, with the notations of the
proof of `(18.4.12)`, that one has `ūĚí•_{x'} = 0` for all the points `x'` of a neighbourhood of `j(x)` in `X'`, knowing
that `ūĚí•_{j(x)} = 0`. Now, by replacing `X'` by an affine neighbourhood of `j(x)`, one may suppose that all the
irreducible components of `X'` contain `j(x)`; if `X' = Spec(A')`, and if `ūĚĒ≠'` is the prime ideal of `A'`
corresponding to the point `j(x)`, the morphism `Spec(A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}) ‚Üí Spec(A')` is dominant, hence the corresponding
homomorphism `A' ‚Üí A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}` is injective since `A'` is reduced `(I, 1.2.7)`. If `ūĚí• = ūĚĒćŐÉ`, where `ūĚĒć` is an ideal of
`A'`, `ūĚĒć` is therefore identified with a subset of `ūĚĒć_{ūĚĒ≠'}`, and the hypothesis `ūĚĒć_{ūĚĒ≠'} = 0` therefore entails
`ūĚĒć = 0`.

**Corollary (18.4.14).**

<!-- label: IV.18.4.14 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring of maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™`, of residue field `k`, `B` a finite `A`-algebra.*

<!-- original page 125 -->

*(i) For `B` to be a formally unramified `A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient that `B ‚äó_A k` be an √©tale
`k`-algebra.*

*(ii) For `B` to be an √©tale `A`-algebra, it is necessary and sufficient that `B ‚äó_A k` be an √©tale `k`-algebra and that
`B` be a flat `A`-module (which is equivalent to saying `(0_I, 6.3.3)` that for every maximal ideal `ūĚĒę` of `B`
(necessarily above `ūĚĒ™`), `B_ūĚĒę` is a flat `A`-module).*

One obviously has only to prove the sufficiency of the stated conditions. It is clear that (ii) follows from (i) and
from `(18.4.12, (ii))`, taking `(17.1.2, (i))` into account. To prove (i), note that if `B ‚äó_A k` is an √©tale
`k`-algebra, one has `ő©^1_{B ‚äó_A k/k} = 0` `(17.2.1)`. Now one has `ő©^1_{B ‚äó_A k/k} = ő©^1_{B/A} ‚äó_B (B ‚äó_A k)`
`(0, 20.5.5)`, and since `B` is an `A`-algebra of finite type, `ő©^1_{B/A}` is a `B`-module of finite type
`(0, 20.4.7)`. But since `B` is a finite `A`-algebra, `ūĚĒ™ B` is contained in the radical of `B` (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._,
chap. V, ¬ß2, n¬į 1, prop. 1), hence Nakayama's lemma proves that `ő©^1_{B/A} = 0`, and consequently `B` is a formally
unramified `A`-algebra `(17.2.1)`.

### 18.5. Henselian local rings[^18.5-azumaya-nagata]

[^18.5-azumaya-nagata]: The notion of Henselian local ring is due to Azumaya, that of Henselization to Nagata, to whom one also owes the principal results of this theory.

**(18.5.1)** Let `X` be a prescheme, `‚Ąį` a locally free `ūĚí™_X`-Module of finite rank; the dual `‚ĄįŐĆ = ‚Ąčom_{ūĚí™_X}(‚Ąį, ūĚí™_X)`
is thus a locally free `ūĚí™_X`-Module whose rank at every point of `X` is equal to that of `‚Ąį` at that point, and the
canonical homomorphism `‚Ąį ‚Üí ‚Ąčom_{ūĚí™_X}(‚ĄįŐĆ, ūĚí™_X) = (‚ĄįŐĆ)ŐĆ` is bijective. For every morphism `X' ‚Üí X`, put
`‚Ąį_{(X')} = ‚Ąį ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X'}`, which is a locally free `ūĚí™_{X'}`-Module, and consider the set `őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})` of
sections of this `ūĚí™_{X'}`-Module over `X'`. We are going to see that one thereby defines a representable contravariant
functor

```text
  (18.5.1.1)    ūĚíĪ : X' ‚Ü¶ őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})
```

from the category of `X`-preschemes to that of sets `(0_III, 8.1.8)`.

First of all, one has indeed defined a functor, for if `f : X'' ‚Üí X'` is an `X`-morphism of `X`-preschemes, one has
`‚Ąį_{(X'')} = f*(‚Ąį_{(X')})`, whence `(0_I, 4.4.3.2)` a map
`őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')}) ‚Üí őď(X'', f*(‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})) = őď(X'', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X'')})` which completes the definition of the functor `ūĚíĪ`.
Let us show next that the `X`-prescheme `ūĚźē(‚Ąį)` `(II, 1.7.8)` represents the functor `ūĚíĪ`. Indeed, it is immediate that
one has `(‚Ąį_{(X')})ŐĆ = ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')}`, hence `ūĚíĪ(X') = őď(X', (‚Ąį_{(X')})ŐĆ)`; taking `(I, 3.3.14)` into account, one is reduced
to defining a bijection `őď(ūĚźē(‚Ąį)/X) ‚•≤ őď(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ)`, and to verifying that the bijection
`őď(ūĚźē(‚Ąį_{(X')})/X') ‚•≤ őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})` is functorial in `X'`. Now, one has a canonical bijection of `őď(ūĚźē(‚Ąį)/X)` onto
`Hom_{ūĚí™_X}(‚Ąį, ūĚí™_X)` `(II, 1.7.8)`, and the transposition `u ‚Ü¶ Ň•u` is a canonical bijection of `Hom_{ūĚí™_X}(‚Ąį, ūĚí™_X)` onto
`Hom_{ūĚí™_X}(ūĚí™_X, ‚ĄįŐĆ) = őď(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ)` by reason of the identification of `(‚ĄįŐĆ)ŐĆ` with `‚Ąį`. The verification of the functorial
property is immediate.

<!-- original page 126 -->

Let us further note that, in conformity with the general theory `(0_III, 8.1.6)`, the identity automorphism of `ūĚźē(‚Ąį)`
corresponds canonically to a section `c` of `‚ĄįŐĆ_{(ūĚźē(‚Ąį))}` over `ūĚźē(‚Ąį)`, that is to say `(II, 1.4.1)` to a homomorphism
of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)`-Modules `u : ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį) ‚Üí ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ‚ĄįŐĆ`; for every affine open `W` of `X`, if one puts
`őď(W, ūĚí™_X) = A`, if one identifies `őď(W, ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį))` with a polynomial algebra `C = A[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]`, so that the
`T_i` form a basis of `őď(W, ‚Ąį)`, and if finally one denotes by `(e_i)` the dual basis of `(T_i)` in
`őď(W, ‚ĄįŐĆ) = őď(W, ‚Ąį)ŐĆ`, one sees at once that `u` corresponds to the homomorphism of `C`-modules such that
`u(1) = ‚ąĎ_{i=1}^n T_i ‚äó e_i`.

If `X' = Spec(A)` is affine and such that `‚Ąį_{(X')}` is isomorphic to `ūĚí™_{X'}^n`, `őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})` is a free
`A`-module of rank `n`; in a figurative way, one may say that the object `ūĚźē(‚Ąį)` represents "the set of points of the
affine space twisted over `X` defined by `‚Ąį`".

**(18.5.2)** Recall `(II, 1.7.8)` that one has by definition `ūĚźē(‚Ąį) = Spec(ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį))`. Let `‚Ąź` be a quasi-coherent
Ideal of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)`, so that `Spec(ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)/‚Ąź)` is a closed sub-prescheme of `ūĚźē(‚Ąį)`; we are going to
interpret it as representing a functor from the category of `X`-preschemes to that of sets. Note for this that a section
`u ‚ąą őď(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ)` is canonically identified with an `ūĚí™_X`-homomorphism `u : ūĚí™_X ‚Üí ‚ĄįŐĆ`, to which corresponds by transposition
an `ūĚí™_X`-homomorphism `Ň•u : ‚Ąį ‚Üí ūĚí™_X`, and consequently a homomorphism of `ūĚí™_X`-Algebras
`v : ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį) ‚Üí ūĚí™_X`. Let `Al(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ, ‚Ąź)` denote the set of `u ‚ąą őď(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ)` such that `‚Ąź` is contained in the kernel
of `v`; it follows at once from these definitions that

```text
  X' ‚Ü¶ Al(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')}, ‚Ąź ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X'})
```

is a functor represented by `Spec(ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)/‚Ąź)`. If `X' = Spec(A)` is affine and such that `‚Ąį_{(X')}` is isomorphic
to `ūĚí™_{X'}^n`, `őď(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')})` can be identified with the set `A^n`, and `‚Ąź ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X'}` with an
`ūĚí™_{X'}`-Module of the form `ūĚĒćŐÉ`, where `ūĚĒć` is an ideal of the polynomial ring `A[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]`; the set
`Al(X', ‚ĄįŐĆ_{(X')}, ‚Ąź ‚äó_{ūĚí™_X} ūĚí™_{X'})` is then identified with the subset of `A^n` formed by the points
`(t_1, ‚Ä¶, t_n)` such that `P(t_1, ‚Ä¶, t_n) = 0` for every polynomial `P ‚ąą ūĚĒć`; in a figurative way, one may
therefore say that the object `Spec(ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)/‚Ąź)` represents "the algebraic subset of the affine space twisted
`őď(X, ‚ĄįŐĆ)` formed by the points annulling the ideal `őď(X, ‚Ąź)`". One also denotes this `X`-prescheme by
`ūĚźÄūĚź•(‚Ąį, ‚Ąź)`. One will note that if `‚Ąź` is an Ideal of finite type of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)`, `ūĚźÄūĚź•(‚Ąį, ‚Ąź)` is an
`X`-prescheme of finite presentation, since `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_X}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ąį)` is an `ūĚí™_X`-Algebra of finite presentation.

**Lemma (18.5.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.3 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí S` a morphism finite and locally free `(18.2.7)`. Consider the contravariant functor
from the category of `S`-preschemes to the category of sets*

```text
  (18.5.3.1)    S' ‚Ü¶ Of(X √ó_S S')
```

*where `Of(X √ó_S S')` is the set of subsets simultaneously open and closed of the space underlying `X √ó_S S'`. Then
this functor is representable by an `S`-prescheme `ūĚźéūĚźü(X)`, which is affine, √©tale and of finite presentation over
`S`.*

<!-- original page 127 -->

One has by hypothesis `X = Spec(‚Ą¨)`, where `‚Ą¨ = f_*(ūĚí™_X)` is a finite and locally free `ūĚí™_S`-Algebra. Put
`X' = X √ó_S S'`, `f' = f_{(S')}`, `‚Ą¨' = ‚Ą¨ ‚äó_{ūĚí™_S} ūĚí™_{S'} = f'_*(ūĚí™_{X'})`, so that `X' = Spec(‚Ą¨')`; there is then a
canonical bijection, functorial in `S'`, of the set `Of(X')` onto the set `Id(‚Ą¨')` of *idempotents* of the ring
`őď(S', ‚Ą¨') = őď(X', ūĚí™_{X'})`. Indeed, by virtue of the equivalence of the category of `S'`-schemes affine over `S'` and
the opposite category of the category of quasi-coherent `ūĚí™_{S'}`-Algebras `(II, 1.2.7 and 1.3.1)`, there is canonical
biunivocal correspondence between the decompositions of `X'` into a sum `X'_1 ‚®Ņ X'_2` of two sub-preschemes induced
on open sets of `X'` and the decompositions of `‚Ą¨'` into direct composite of two Ideals `‚Ą¨'_1`, `‚Ą¨'_2`; these latter
in turn form a set in canonical biunivocal correspondence (and functorial in `S'`) with `Id(‚Ą¨')`. It therefore suffices
to prove the lemma for the functor `S' ‚Ü¶ Id(‚Ą¨')`.

For this, we are going to show that there exists an Ideal of finite type `‚Ąź` of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_S}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ą¨ŐĆ)` such that `Id(‚Ą¨')` is
of the form `Al(S', ‚Ą¨ŐĆ', ‚Ąź ‚äó_{ūĚí™_S} ūĚí™_{S'})` `(18.5.2)`. Note for this effect that since `‚Ą¨` is an `ūĚí™_S`-Algebra, its
inverse image `‚Ą¨_{(ūĚźē(‚Ą¨ŐĆ))}` is an `ūĚí™_{ūĚźē(‚Ą¨ŐĆ)}`-Algebra, and one may therefore form the *square* `c¬≤`, in this Algebra, of
the canonical section `c` `(18.5.1)`; it corresponds canonically to a homomorphism
`u^{(2)} : ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_S}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ą¨) ‚Üí ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_S}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ą¨) ‚äó_{ūĚí™_S} ‚Ą¨` of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_S}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ą¨)`-Modules, and one verifies at once that for an
affine open `W` of `S`, with the notations of `(18.5.1)`, `u^{(2)}` corresponds to the homomorphism of `C`-modules such
that `u^{(2)}(1) = ‚ąĎ_{k} (‚ąĎ_{i,j} c_{ijk} T_i T_j) ‚äó e_k`, where `(c_{ijk})` is the multiplication table of the
algebra `őď(W, ‚Ą¨)`. Let us show that the Ideal `‚Ąź` of `ūĚźí_{ūĚí™_S}^‚ÄĘ(‚Ą¨)` generated by the kernel of the homomorphism
`u ‚ąí u^{(2)}` answers the question. It suffices indeed to note that the ideal `őď(W, ‚Ąź)` of `C` is generated by the
polynomials `P_k(T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n) = T_k ‚ąí ‚ąĎ_{i,j} c_{ijk} T_i T_j` and that the *idempotents* of `őď(W, ‚Ą¨)` are
precisely the elements `‚ąĎ_i t_i e_i` of this algebra such that `(t_1, ‚Ä¶, t_n)` annuls all the polynomials `P_k`
`(1 ‚©Ĺ k ‚©Ĺ n)`. One therefore deduces from `(18.5.2)` that the `S`-prescheme affine of finite presentation
`ūĚźéūĚźü(X) = ūĚźÄūĚź•(‚Ą¨, ‚Ąź)` indeed represents the functor `(18.5.3.1)`. It remains to prove that `ūĚźéūĚźü(X)` is √©tale over
`S`, or, what amounts to the same, that it is *formally √©tale* over `S`. But if `S'` is an `S`-prescheme, `S'_0` a
closed sub-prescheme of `S'` defined by a locally nilpotent Ideal of `ūĚí™_{S'}` (and having therefore the same underlying
space as `S'`), it is clear that `X' = X √ó_S S'` and `X'_0 = X √ó_S S'_0` have the same underlying space, hence the
canonical map `Of(X') ‚Üí Of(X'_0)` is bijective, which completes the proof `(17.1.1)`.

**Proposition (18.5.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.4 -->

*Let `S` be a prescheme, `S_0` a closed sub-prescheme of `S`; consider the following properties:*

*a) For every finite morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S`, the canonical map*

```text
  (18.5.4.1)    Of(S') ‚Üí Of(S' √ó_S S_0)    (cf. (18.5.3))
```

*is bijective.*

*a') For every finite and locally free morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S`, the map `(18.5.4.1)` is bijective.*

<!-- original page 128 -->

*b) For every √©tale and separated morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S`, the canonical map*

```text
  (18.5.4.2)    őď(S'/S) ‚Üí őď(S' √ó_S S_0/S_0)
```

*is bijective.*

*The condition b) entails a'); if moreover `S` is quasi-compact and quasi-separated, the condition a) entails b).*

Let us first prove that b) entails a'). Suppose then b) verified, and let `g : S' ‚Üí S` be a finite and locally free
morphism; put `S'_0 = S' √ó_S S_0`, so that `g_0 = g_{(S_0)} : S'_0 ‚Üí S_0` is finite and locally free. Then it follows
from `(18.5.3)` that `P = ūĚźéūĚźü(S')` is an √©tale and separated `S`-prescheme; moreover, the definition of the functor
`ūĚźéūĚźü` shows at once that if one puts `P_0 = ūĚźéūĚźü(S'_0)` (for the category of `S_0`-preschemes), one has `P_0 = P √ó_S S_0`.
That being so, one has by definition the commutative diagram

```text
        őď(P/S)   ‚Üí  őď(P_0/S_0)
         ‚Üď ‚ČÄ          ‚Üď ‚ČÄ
        Of(S')   ‚Üí  Of(S'_0)
```

where the vertical arrows are the canonical bijections. Since hypothesis b), applied to the morphism `P ‚Üí S`, entails
that the top row is a bijection, so is the bottom row, which establishes our assertion.

Before proving that a) entails b) when `S` is quasi-compact and quasi-separated, we shall establish the

**Lemma (18.5.4.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.4.3 -->

*If `S` and `S_0` verify condition a) of `(18.5.4)`, then, for every finite morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S`, `S'` is the unique
neighbourhood of `S'_0 = g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0) = S' √ó_S S_0` in `S'`.*

Indeed, it amounts to the same to say that if `T'` is a closed subset of `S'` such that `T' ‚ą© S'_0 = ‚ąÖ`, then
`T' = ‚ąÖ`. Now, if one denotes again by `T'` a closed sub-prescheme of `S'` having `T'` for underlying space, the
composite morphism `h : T' ‚Üí S' ‚Üí S` is finite and `h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0)` is empty; condition a) applied to the morphism `h`
entails that `T'` is necessarily empty.

This lemma being established, let us prove first that under hypothesis a), the map `(18.5.4.2)` is injective. Indeed,
if `u'`, `u''` are two `S`-sections of `S'`, the fact that the morphism `S' ‚Üí S` is unramified entails that the
prescheme of coincidences of `u'` and `u''` is induced on an open set `U` of `S` `(17.4.6)`. If the restrictions to
`S_0` of `u'` and `u''` are the same, the fact that `u'` and `u''` are open immersions `(17.4.1)` entails that `U`
contains `S_0`, hence is equal to `S` by virtue of the lemma `(18.5.4.3)` applied to the case `S' = S`.

It remains to show that under hypothesis a), the map `(18.5.4.2)` is surjective (`S` being quasi-compact and
quasi-separated). Let then `u_0 : S_0 ‚Üí S'_0` be an `S_0`-section of `S'_0`; `u_0(S_0)` being quasi-compact in `S'`,
may be covered by a finite number of affine open sets

<!-- original page 129 -->

`V_i` such that the restriction `g | V_i` is a morphism of finite type; if `V` is the union of the `V_i`, one concludes
that `g | V : V ‚Üí S` is a morphism of finite presentation, being separated and locally of finite presentation by
hypothesis `(1.6.1)`. Replacing `S'` by `V`, one may therefore suppose that `g` is of finite presentation. Since `S` is
quasi-compact and quasi-separated and `g` is quasi-finite and separated `(17.6.1)`, it follows from the "Main theorem"
`(8.12.6)` that `g` factors as `S' ‚Üí^j S'' ‚Üí^f S`, where `j` is an open immersion and `f` a finite morphism. Put
`S''_0 = S'' √ó_S S_0`, `j_0 = j_{(S_0)} : S'_0 ‚Üí S''_0`, which is an open immersion, and `f_0 = f_{(S_0)} : S''_0 ‚Üí S_0`,
which is a finite morphism. Then `u_0` is also an `S_0`-section of `S''_0`. Since `g_0 : S'_0 ‚Üí S_0` is √©tale, `u_0` is
an open immersion of `S_0` in `S'_0` `(17.4.1)`, hence `u_0(S_0)` is open in `S'_0`, and a fortiori in `S''_0`; but on
the other hand, since `f_0` is a finite morphism, hence separated, `u_0` is a closed immersion of `S_0` in `S''_0`
`(I, 5.4.6)`, hence `X_0 = u_0(S_0)` is both open and closed in `S''_0`. By virtue of hypothesis a), there exists a
subset both open and closed `X` of `S''` such that `X ‚ą© S''_0 = X_0`. Let us show first that the morphism
`f : S'' ‚Üí S` is *√©tale at the points of `X`*: indeed, the set `U` of points of `X` where `f | X` is √©tale is open and
contains by hypothesis `X_0 ‚äā S'_0`. But the lemma `(18.5.4.3)` applied to the finite morphism `f | X` proves that
`U = X`. On the other hand, `S' ‚ą© X` is open in `X` and contains `X_0` by hypothesis, hence the same reasoning proves
that `S' ‚ą© X = X`, that is to say `X ‚äā S'`. It remains to show that, for every `s ‚ąą S`, the geometric number `n(s)` of
points of `X ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)` is equal to `1`, for it will follow that `f | X` is radicial and surjective, and since `f | X`
is √©tale, one will have shown `(17.9.1)` that `f | X` is an isomorphism of the open `X ‚äā S'` onto `S`, of which the
inverse isomorphism `u` will be the `S`-section sought extending `u_0`. But since `f | X` is √©tale and finite,
`s ‚Ü¶ n(s)` is continuous on `S` `(18.2.8)`, and since `X ‚ą© S_0 = X_0`, one has `n(s) = 1` on `S_0`; the set of points
`s ‚ąą S` such that `n(s) = 1` being open in `S` and containing `S_0`, it is equal to `S` by `(18.5.4.3)`. Q.E.D.

**Remark (18.5.4.4).** ‚ÄĒ One can show that the statement `(18.5.4)` remains valid when, in condition b), one assumes
only the morphism `g` √©tale (but not necessarily separated) `[43, exp. XII]`.

**Definition (18.5.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.5 -->

*One says that a prescheme `S` and a closed sub-prescheme `S_0` of `S` form a **Henselian couple** if they verify
condition a) of `(18.5.4)`.*

Taking `(I, 5.1.8)` into account, it amounts to the same to say that `(S, S_0)` is a Henselian couple or that
`(S_red, (S_0)_red)` is one.

**Proposition (18.5.6).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.6 -->

*(i) If `(S, S_0)` is a Henselian couple, then, for every finite morphism `f : S' ‚Üí S`, if `S'_0` is the sub-prescheme
`f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0)` of `S'`, the couple `(S', S'_0)` is Henselian.*

*(ii) Let `S = ‚®Ņ_őĪ S^{(őĪ)}` be a sum of preschemes, `S_0` a closed sub-prescheme of `S`, sum of the closed
sub-preschemes `S_0^{(őĪ)}` of the `S^{(őĪ)}`. For the couple `(S, S_0)` to be Henselian, it is necessary and sufficient
that each of the couples `(S^{(őĪ)}, S_0^{(őĪ)})` be so.*

Assertion (i) is an immediate consequence of the definition, since for every finite morphism `g : S'' ‚Üí S'`,
`f ‚ąė g : S'' ‚Üí S` is a finite morphism. Similarly, under the conditions of (ii), for a morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S` to be
finite, it is necessary and sufficient that each

<!-- original page 130 -->

of its restrictions `g^{(őĪ)} : S'^{(őĪ)} = g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S^{(őĪ)}) ‚Üí S^{(őĪ)}` be so, and if one puts `S'_0 = g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0)`,
`S'^{(őĪ)}_0 = (g^{(őĪ)})‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(S_0^{(őĪ)})`, there is biunivocal correspondence between the open and closed subsets `U` (resp.
`U_0`) of `S'` (resp. `S'_0`) and the families `(U^{(őĪ)})` (resp. `U_0^{(őĪ)}`), where `U^{(őĪ)}` (resp. `U_0^{(őĪ)}`) is
an open and closed subset of `S'^{(őĪ)}` (resp. `S'^{(őĪ)}_0`), whence assertion (ii).

**Remark (18.5.7).** ‚ÄĒ Let `S = Spec(A)` be an affine scheme, `S_0` a closed sub-scheme of `S` defined by an ideal `ūĚĒć`
of `A`. Then, if the couple `(S, S_0)` is Henselian, the ideal `ūĚĒć` is necessarily contained in the radical of `A`.
Indeed, if `ūĚĒ™` is a maximal ideal of `A`, `ūĚĒ™` must belong to `V(ūĚĒć) = S_0`, by virtue of `(18.5.4.3)`, in other words
one must have `ūĚĒć ‚äā ūĚĒ™`, whence the conclusion. In particular, suppose that `S_0` is reduced to a point, that is to say
that the ideal `ūĚĒć` is *maximal*; then `ūĚĒć` must be the radical of `A`, in other words `A` must be a *local* ring, `S_0`
being the unique closed point of `Spec(A)`.

**Definition (18.5.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.8 -->

*One says that a ring `A` is **Henselian** if it is semi-local and if, denoting by `ūĚĒĮ` the radical of `A`, the couple
`(Spec(A), Spec(A/ūĚĒĮ))` is Henselian. One calls **Henselian local scheme** a scheme isomorphic to the spectrum of a
Henselian local ring.*

**Proposition (18.5.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.9 -->

*(i) For a semi-local ring `A` to be Henselian, it is necessary and sufficient that it be a direct composite of
Henselian local rings.*

*(ii) For a local ring `A` to be Henselian, it is necessary and sufficient that every finite `A`-algebra `B` be
isomorphic to a product of local rings.*

(i) Indeed, the definition, applied to `S = Spec(A)` and `S_0 = Spec(A/ūĚĒĮ)`, shows, since `S_0` is a finite discrete
closed subset in `S`, that `S` is the union of a finite number of open and closed subsets `S_i` `(1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ n)` pairwise
disjoint, each of which contains exactly one of the maximal ideals `ūĚĒ™_i` of `A`; the conclusion follows from
`(18.5.6, (ii))` and from remark `(18.5.7)`.

(ii) Every finite morphism `S' ‚Üí Spec(A)` is of the form `Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(A)`, where `B` is a finite `A`-algebra. If
`k` is the residue field of `A`, `Spec(B ‚äó_A k)` is a spectrum of an Artinian ring, hence finite and discrete. To say
that the couple `(Spec(A), Spec(k))` is Henselian therefore signifies that `B` is direct composite of rings `A_i` such
that `Spec(A_i ‚äó_A k)` is reduced to a point, that is to say that `A_i` (which is a finite `A`-algebra) must have only
one maximal ideal (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. V, ¬ß2, n¬į 1, prop. 1).

The study of Henselian rings is therefore essentially reduced to that of Henselian local rings.

**Proposition (18.5.10).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.10 -->

*If `A` is a Henselian ring, every finite `A`-algebra `B` is a Henselian ring (hence direct composite of Henselian local
rings `(18.5.9)`).*

Indeed, if `ūĚĒĮ'` is the radical of `B`, the inverse image of `ūĚĒĮ'` in `A` is the radical `ūĚĒĮ` of `A`, and every ideal of
`B` above a maximal ideal of `A` is a maximal ideal of `B`, hence the set `V(ūĚĒĮ')` in `Spec(B)` is the inverse image of
the set `V(ūĚĒĮ)` in `Spec(A)`. The proposition is then a consequence of `(18.5.6, (i))`.

**Theorem (18.5.11).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.11 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `ūĚĒ™` its maximal ideal. The following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) `A` is Henselian, in other words, every finite `A`-algebra `B` is isomorphic to a product of local rings.*

<!-- original page 131 -->

*a') Condition a) is satisfied for all `A`-algebras `B` of the form `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`, where `F ‚ąą A[T]` is a unitary
polynomial.*

*b) Let `S = Spec(A)`, `S_0 = Spec(k)`. For every √©tale morphism `g : S' ‚Üí S`, if one puts `S'_0 = S' ‚äó_A k`, every
`S_0`-section `u_0` of `S'_0` is the restriction of an `S`-section `u` of `S'`.*

*c) For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí S`, separated and locally of finite type, and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f(x)` is
equal to the closed point `s` of `S` and that `f` is quasi-finite at the point `x` `(Err_{III}, 20)`, `X` is a sum of
two preschemes `X'`, `X''` such that `X' = Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x})` and `f | X' : X' ‚Üí S` is a finite morphism.*

*c') For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` locally of finite type, and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f` is quasi-finite at
the point `x` and `f(x)` is equal to the closed point `s` of `S`, `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a finite algebra over
`ūĚí™_{S, s} = A`.*

*c'') For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` locally of finite presentation, and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f` is
quasi-finite at the point `x` and that `f(x)` is equal to the closed point `s` of `S`, `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a finite algebra
of finite presentation over `ūĚí™_{S, s} = A`.*

Note first that condition c') (resp. c'')) is equivalent to the same condition where one supposes in addition `f`
separated, the question being local on `X`. Similarly, condition b) is equivalent to the same condition where one
supposes in addition `g` separated: indeed, it suffices to apply this latter to the restriction of `g` to an affine
open neighbourhood of the point `u_0(S_0)` in `S'`. Let us restrict ourselves therefore from now on to the case where,
in b), c') and c''), the given morphisms are separated.

The fact that a) implies b) and that b) implies a') is a particular case of `(18.5.4)`. Let us moreover show that a')
implies a). It is a matter of proving that if `e_0` is an idempotent of `C = B ‚äó_A k`, there exists an idempotent
`e ‚ąą B` whose canonical image is `e_0`. If `b` is an element of `B` whose image in `C` is `e_0`, the sub-`A`-algebra
`A[b] = B'` of `B` is finite, and the canonical image `C'` of `B' ‚äó_A k` in `C` contains `e_0`. Now, `B' ‚äó_A k` is a
finite `k`-algebra, hence the direct composite of finite local `k`-algebras, and consequently `e_0` is the image in
`C'` of an idempotent `e'_0` of `B' ‚äó_A k`. One is thus reduced to the case where `B` is monogenic, and consequently
isomorphic to an `A`-algebra quotient of an algebra of the form `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`, where `F` is a unitary polynomial.
Now, by virtue of a'), `A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]` is direct composite of local rings, hence so are all its quotient algebras,
which completes the proof of the existence of the idempotent `e`.

It is immediate that c) entails a), as one sees by reasoning by induction on the number of maximal ideals of the
semi-local ring `B`. To see that a) implies c), one may, by virtue of `(13.1.4)`, restrict to the case where `f` is an
affine and quasi-finite morphism. Then, by application of the "Main theorem" `(8.12.8)`, `f` may be written as a
composite morphism `X ‚Üí^j Y ‚Üí^g S`, where `g` is a finite morphism and `j` an open immersion. Since `Y = Spec(B)`,
where `B` is a finite `A`-algebra, it follows from a) that `B` is direct composite of local rings, which are obviously
finite `A`-algebras, and `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is identified with one of these local rings since `f(x) = s`; in addition, every
open of `Y` containing `x` necessarily contains `Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x})`.

It is trivial that c) implies c'), by virtue of the remark at the start. Let us prove that c') implies c''). Suppose
indeed c') verified, and prove that under the conditions

<!-- original page 132 -->
