of c''), the set `Z = Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x})` is then identified with an open and closed subset of `X`, which will establish
c'' `(I, 2.4.2)`. In the first place, the composite morphism `Z ‚Üí^j X ‚Üí^f S`, where `j` is the canonical morphism
`(I, 2.4.1)`, is finite and of finite presentation by hypothesis, and since `f` is separated and locally of finite
presentation, `j` is also a morphism finite and of finite presentation (`(II, 6.1.5)` and `(1.4.3)`); `j` is
consequently a closed morphism `(II, 6.1.10)`, which proves that `Z` is closed in `X`. It then follows from
`(I, 2.4.2)` and `(I, 4.2.2)` that `j` is a closed immersion. But if `ūĚí•` is the Ideal of `ūĚí™_X` defining `Z`, one has
then by hypothesis `ūĚí•_x = 0`, hence also `ūĚí•_z = 0` at every point `z` of an open neighbourhood `V` of `x` in `X`,
since by hypothesis `ūĚí•` is a quasi-coherent `ūĚí™_X`-Module of finite type (`(1.4.7)` and `(0_I, 5.2.2)`). Now, such a
neighbourhood contains `Z`, hence `Z` is open in `X` since `ūĚí• | V = 0`.

Finally, c'') implies a'): indeed, if `B = A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`, the morphism `X = Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(A) = Y` is finite and of
finite presentation, and the preceding proof shows that `B` is direct composite of the finite `A`-algebras
`ūĚí™_{X, x_i}`, where the `x_i` are the points of the fibre of the closed point of `Y`.

**Corollary (18.5.12).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.12 -->

*Let `A` be a semi-local ring, `ūĚĒĮ` its radical; put `S = Spec(A)`, `S_0 = Spec(A/ūĚĒĮ)`. For `A` to be Henselian, it is
necessary and sufficient that, for every finite morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` and every √©tale and separated morphism
`g : Y ‚Üí S`, if one puts `X_0 = X √ó_S S_0` and `Y_0 = Y √ó_S S_0`, the canonical map*

```text
  Hom_S(X, Y) ‚Üí Hom_{S_0}(X_0, Y_0)
```

*is bijective.*

The sufficiency of the condition follows from the equivalence of a) and b) in `(18.5.11)`, by applying this condition
to the case where `f = 1_S`. To see that the condition is necessary, note that if `A` is Henselian, the couple
`(X, X_0)` is Henselian by `(18.5.6, (i))`; in addition `Hom_S(X, Y) = őď(X √ó_S Y/X)` and
`Hom_{S_0}(X_0, Y_0) = őď(X_0 √ó_{S_0} Y_0/X_0)`; since `X √ó_S Y` is √©tale and separated over `X`, the conclusion
follows from the fact that a) implies b) in `(18.5.4)`.

**Remark (18.5.13).** ‚ÄĒ The equivalent conditions a), a'), b) of theorem `(18.5.11)` are also equivalent to the
following ("*Hensel's lemma*"):

*a'') For every unitary polynomial `F ‚ąą A[T]`, of canonical image `F_0 ‚ąą k[T]`, and every decomposition
`F_0 = G_0 H_0` of `F_0` into a product of two coprime unitary polynomials `G_0`, `H_0` of `k[T]`, there exists a
unique couple `(G, H)` of unitary polynomials of `A[T]` having the following properties: `G_0` and `H_0` are the
canonical images respectively of `G` and `H`, one has `F = GH`, and the ideal of `A[T]` generated by `G` and `H` is
equal to `A[T]`.*

We shall first establish the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.5.13.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.13.1 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring of residue field `k`, `F ‚ąą A[T]` a unitary polynomial, `B` the `A`-algebra
`A[T]/F ¬∑ A[T]`. There exists a canonical correspondence between the decompositions of `B` into direct composite of
two `A`-algebras quotients `B'`, `B''` and the decompositions `F = GH` of `F` into product of two unitary polynomials
`G`, `H` of `A[T]`, such that the ideal generated by `G` and `H` is equal to `A[T]`; the quotient algebras `B'`, `B''`
corresponding to such a pair of polynomials `G`, `H` are respectively `A[T]/H ¬∑ A[T]` and `A[T]/G ¬∑ A[T]`.*

<!-- original page 133 -->

If `F = GH` and if `G` and `H` generate the ideal `A[T]`, there are two polynomials `P`, `Q` of `A[T]` such that
`1 = PG + QH`. One deduces that the intersection of the principal ideals `ūĚĒě = G ¬∑ A[T]` and `ūĚĒü = H ¬∑ A[T]` is equal
to `ūĚĒ† = F ¬∑ A[T]`: indeed, if `R ‚ąą G ¬∑ A[T] ‚ą© H ¬∑ A[T]`, one may write `R = PRG + QRH`; now `RH` (resp. `RG`) is a
multiple of `F` since `R` is a multiple of `G` (resp. `H`), hence `R ‚ąą F ¬∑ A[T]`. Since `A[T] = ūĚĒě + ūĚĒü`,
`A[T]/ūĚĒ†` is direct sum of the ideals `ūĚĒě/(ūĚĒě ‚ą© ūĚĒü)` and `ūĚĒü/(ūĚĒě ‚ą© ūĚĒü)`, canonically isomorphic respectively to
`A[T]/ūĚĒü` and `A[T]/ūĚĒě`.

Conversely, suppose given a decomposition of `B` into direct composite of two `A`-algebras `B'`, `B''`, which are
canonically identified with two ideals `e'B`, `e''B` of `B`, corresponding to a decomposition `1 = e' + e''` of `1`
into orthogonal idempotents `e'`, `e''` of `B`. Put further `B_0 = B ‚äó_A k = k[T]/F_0 ¬∑ k[T]`, where `F_0` is the
canonical image of `F` in `k[T]`, of the same degree `n` as `F`; if `e'_0`, `e''_0` are the canonical images of `e'`,
`e''` in `B_0`, these are two orthogonal idempotents such that `1 = e'_0 + e''_0`, and `B_0` is therefore direct
composite of `B'_0 = e'_0 B_0` and `B''_0 = e''_0 B_0`. Let `t` and `t_0` be the canonical images of `T` in `B` and
`B_0`; since `B'_0` (resp. `B''_0`) is a finite `k`-algebra generated by `t'_0 = e'_0 t_0` (resp. `t''_0 = e''_0 t_0`),
it admits a basis of the form `{e'_0, t'_0, t'_0^2, ‚Ä¶, t'_0^{s-1}}` (resp.
`{e''_0, t''_0, t''_0^2, ‚Ä¶, t''_0^{r-1}}`) with `r + s = n`. On the other hand, `B` being a free `A`-module (with
basis `{1, t, ‚Ä¶, t^{n-1}}`), `B'` and `B''` are projective `A`-modules, hence free since `A` is a local ring
(Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß5, n¬į 3, cor. of prop. 5); it therefore follows from what precedes and from
Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. II, ¬ß3, n¬į 3, prop. 5, that if one puts `t' = e't`, `t'' = e''t`,
`{e', t', ‚Ä¶, t'^{s-1}}` (resp. `{e'', t'', ‚Ä¶, t''^{r-1}}`) is a basis of the `A`-module `B'` (resp. `B''`).
There is therefore a unitary polynomial `H` (resp. `G`) of degree `s` (resp. `r`) of `A[T]` such that `e' H(t') = 0`
and `e'' G(t'') = 0`; since `t^h = t'^h + t''^h` for every integer `h ‚©ĺ 1`, and `t'^h = e' t^h`, `t''^h = e'' t^h`, one
has also `G(t) = e' G(t')` and `H(t) = e'' H(t'')`, whence `G(t) H(t) = 0`; one concludes that the polynomial
`G(T) H(T)` is divisible by `F(T)`; but since the degrees of these two unitary polynomials are the same, one has
`GH = F`. In addition, `B'` (resp. `B''`) is isomorphic to `A[T]/H ¬∑ A[T]` (resp. `A[T]/G ¬∑ A[T]`). Finally, there are
two polynomials `R`, `S` of `A[T]` such that `e' = R(t)` and `e'' = S(t)`; since `R(t) = e' R(t') + e'' R(t'')`, one has
necessarily `e'' R(t'') = 0` and similarly `e' S(t') = 0`, so that, by definition of `G` and `H`, `R = QH` and
`S = PG`, where `P`, `Q` belong to `A[T]`; the relation `1 = R(t) + S(t)` in `B` thus gives by definition
`PG + QH = 1 + LF` for some polynomial `L ‚ąą A[T]`, and since `F = GH`, this proves that the ideal generated by `G` and
`H` is `A[T]`, and completes the proof of the lemma.

This lemma being established, it suffices to apply it to the local ring `A` on the one hand, to the field `k` on the
other, to see at once that conditions a') and a'') are equivalent.

**Proposition (18.5.14).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.14 -->

*Every semi-local ring `A`, separated and complete for the `ūĚĒĮ`-preadic topology (where `ūĚĒĮ` is the radical of `A`) is
Henselian.*

Indeed, `A` is direct composite of separated and complete local rings (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. III, ¬ß2, n¬į 13,
cor. of prop. 19), hence one is reduced to the case where `A` is a local ring. Let us verify criterion a') of
`(18.5.11)`. Since `B` is a free `A`-module of finite type, it is obviously separated and complete for the
`ūĚĒĮ`-preadic topology,

<!-- original page 134 -->

which is also the `ūĚĒį`-preadic topology, where `ūĚĒį` is the radical of the semi-local ring `B`, for `B/ūĚĒĮ B` is an Artinian
ring of radical `ūĚĒį/ūĚĒĮ B`. One then knows (Bourbaki, _Alg. comm._, chap. III, ¬ß2, n¬į 13, cor. of prop. 19) that `B` is
direct composite of local rings.

**Proposition (18.5.15).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.15 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian ring, `ūĚĒĮ` its radical. Then the functor `B ‚Ü¶ B/ūĚĒĮ B` is an equivalence from the category of
finite √©tale `A`-algebras with the category of finite √©tale `(A/ūĚĒĮ)`-algebras.*

The fact that the functor in the statement is fully faithful is a particular case of `(18.5.12)`. To show that this
functor is an equivalence of categories, one may restrict to the case where `A` is local; it then suffices to apply
`(18.1.1)` (for √©tale morphisms), to `S = Spec(A)` and `S_0 = Spec(A/ūĚĒĮ)`, reduced to a single point.

**Remarks (18.5.16).** ‚ÄĒ (i) We do not know whether proposition `(18.5.15)` generalizes to a Henselian couple
`(S, S_0)`, even when `S` is affine and Noetherian.

(ii) Let `A` be a Noetherian ring, `ūĚĒć` an ideal of `A` such that `A` is separated and complete for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic
topology. Then the couple `(Spec(A), Spec(A/ūĚĒć))` is Henselian: indeed, for every finite `A`-algebra `B`, `B` is
separated and complete for the `ūĚĒć`-preadic topology `(0_I, 7.3.6)`. Replacing `B` by `A` and `ūĚĒć B` by `ūĚĒć`, everything
comes down to seeing that the map which, to every idempotent of `A`, associates its class `mod ūĚĒć`, is bijective. Now,
one has `A = lim_‚Üź (A/ūĚĒć^n)`. Let `Idem(A)` denote the set of idempotents of `A`, and for every ring homomorphism
`ŌÜ : A ‚Üí B`, let `Idem(ŌÜ)` denote the map from `Idem(A)` to `Idem(B)` restriction of `ŌÜ`; it follows from the
definition of the projective limit that one has `Idem(A) = lim_‚Üź Idem(A/ūĚĒć^n)` for the maps
`Ōą_{nm} : Idem(A/ūĚĒć^m) ‚Üí Idem(A/ūĚĒć^n)` restriction of the canonical maps `A/ūĚĒć^m ‚Üí A/ūĚĒć^n`. But since
`Spec(A/ūĚĒć^n) ‚Üí Spec(A/ūĚĒć^m)` is a homeomorphism, the `Ōą_{nm}` are bijections (as one saw in the proof of `(18.5.3)`);
this therefore proves our assertion.

**Theorem (18.5.17).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.17 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian local ring, `S = Spec(A)`, `s` the closed point of `S`. For every smooth morphism
`f : X ‚Üí S`, the canonical map*

```text
  őď(X/S) ‚Üí őď(X_s/k(s))
```

*(where `X_s = f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)`) is surjective.*

The datum of a `k(s)`-section of `X_s` is equivalent to that of a point `x ‚ąą X` above `s` rational over `k(s)`, and it
is a matter of proving that there exists an `S`-section `u : S ‚Üí X` such that `u(s) = x`. Taking
`(17.16.3, (i))` into account, one may suppose that `f` is √©tale. Then the conclusion follows from criterion
`(18.5.11, b))`. (The reader will note that by virtue of this criterion, the validity of `(18.5.17)` for a given local
ring `A` is necessary and sufficient for `A` to be Henselian.)

**Remark (18.5.18).** ‚ÄĒ The conditions of `(18.5.11)` are also equivalent to the following:

*d) For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` locally of finite type and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f(x)` is the closed point
`s` of `S` and that `ūĚí™_{X, x}/ūĚĒ™_s ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a field canonically isomorphic to `k(s) = A/ūĚĒ™_s`, there exists an open
neighbourhood `U` of `x` in `X` such that `f | U` is a closed immersion.*

<!-- original page 135 -->

It is immediate that d) implies condition b) of `(18.5.11)` since a closed √©tale immersion is an open immersion
`(18.9.1)`. Conversely, suppose the conditions of `(18.5.11)` verified and let us prove d). The hypothesis of d) implies
that `f` is quasi-finite at the point `x` `(Err_{III}, 20)`, by virtue of `(13.1.4)`. Hence by virtue of condition c)
of `(18.5.11)`, `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a finite `A`-algebra and `Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x})` an open neighbourhood `U` of `x` in `X`. In
addition, since `ūĚí™_{X, x}/ūĚĒ™_s ūĚí™_{X, x}` is isomorphic to `k = A/ūĚĒ™_s`, Nakayama's lemma proves that the homomorphism
`A ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X, x}` is surjective, hence `f | U` is a closed immersion.

**Proposition (18.5.19).**

<!-- label: IV.18.5.19 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian and Henselian local ring, `S = Spec(A)`, `s` the closed point of `S`, `f : X ‚Üí S` a proper
morphism; put `X_s = f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)`. Then the map `Y ‚Ü¶ Y ‚ą© X_s` is a bijection of the set of connected components of `X`
onto the set of connected components of `X_s`.*

By considering a closed sub-prescheme of `X` having for underlying space a connected component of `X`, one is reduced
to proving that if `X` is connected and non-empty, then `X_s` is connected and non-empty. The fact that `X_s` is
non-empty follows from `(II, 7.2.1)`; to prove that `X_s` is connected, let us reason by contradiction, considering the
Stein factorization `f : X ‚Üí^{f'} S' ‚Üí^g S` of the proper morphism `f` `(III, 4.3.3)`; by hypothesis, the discrete
finite set `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)` would contain at least two points. Since `A` is Henselian and `g` separated and of finite type, it
would then follow from `(18.5.11, c)` that `S'` would be a sum of two non-empty preschemes `S'_1`, `S'_2` (since the
intersection of one of them with `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)` is reduced to a single point). Since `f'` is surjective `(III, 4.3.1)`, one
would conclude that `X` is a sum of two non-empty preschemes, contrary to the hypothesis.

<!-- original page 135 -->

### 18.6. Henselization

**(18.6.1)** Given a local ring `A`, we say that a local `A`-algebra `B` is **essentially √©tale** if there exist an
√©tale `A`-algebra `C` and a prime ideal `ūĚĒę` of `C` such that `B` is `A`-isomorphic to `C_ūĚĒę` and the composite
homomorphism `A ‚Üí C ‚Üí C_ūĚĒę` is local (in other words, that `ūĚĒę` lies over the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™` of `A`); this entails
`(17.6.1)` that `ūĚĒę` is the only prime ideal of `B` lying over `ūĚĒ™`, since `B` is a local ring. If `B`, `B'` are two
essentially √©tale local `A`-algebras, then every `A`-homomorphism `B ‚Üí B'` is local.

If `A'` is an essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra and `A''` an essentially √©tale local `A'`-algebra, then `A''` is an
essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra. Indeed, by hypothesis we have `A' = B_ūĚĒę`, where `B` is an √©tale `A`-algebra and `ūĚĒę`
a prime ideal of `B` lying over the maximal ideal of `A`, and `A'' = B'_{ūĚĒę'}`, where `B'` is an √©tale `A'`-algebra and
`ūĚĒę'` a prime ideal of `B'` lying over the ideal `ūĚĒę B_ūĚĒę`. Setting `S = B ‚ąí ūĚĒę`, so that `A' = S^{‚ąí1} B`, `B'` is of the
form `S^{‚ąí1} C`, where `C` is a `B`-algebra of finite presentation. Consequently `C` is an `A`-algebra of finite
presentation and `A''` is of the form `C_ūĚĒĮ`, where `ūĚĒĮ` is a prime ideal of `C` lying over the maximal ideal of `A`.
Since `A'` is a formally √©tale `A`-algebra and `A''` a formally √©tale `A'`-algebra, `A''` is a formally √©tale

<!-- original page 136 -->

`A`-algebra (for the discrete topologies `(0_IV, 19.3.5)` `(17.1.3)`); the morphism `Spec(C) ‚Üí Spec(A)` is therefore
√©tale at the point `ūĚĒĮ` `(17.6.1)`, and consequently there exists an element `g ‚ąą C` such that `C_g` is an √©tale
`A`-algebra, which proves that `A''` is an essentially √©tale `A`-algebra.

Given a local ring `A`, there exists a set `ūĚĒą` of essentially √©tale local `A`-algebras such that every essentially √©tale
local `A`-algebra is `A`-isomorphic to an algebra belonging to `ūĚĒą`. To see this, it evidently suffices to observe that
there exists a set `ūĚĒČ` of `A`-algebras of finite type such that every `A`-algebra of finite type is isomorphic to an
algebra belonging to `ūĚĒČ`; one may take `ūĚĒČ` to be the set of quotients of the polynomial algebras `A[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]`
`(n ‚ąą ‚Ąē)`.

In what follows, if `A` and `B` are two local rings, we shall denote by `Hom.loc(A, B)` the set of local homomorphisms
from `A` to `B`.

**Lemma (18.6.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.2 -->

*Let `A`, `A'` be two local rings, `k`, `k'` their respective residue fields, `ŌÜ : A ‚Üí A'` a (local) homomorphism making
`A'` into an essentially √©tale `A`-algebra `(18.6.1)` and such that the corresponding homomorphism `k ‚Üí k'` is
bijective. Then, for every Henselian local ring `B`, the canonical map*

```text
  (18.6.2.1)    Hom(ŌÜ, 1_B) : Hom.loc(A', B) ‚Üí Hom.loc(A, B)
```

*is bijective.*

Set `S = Spec(A)`, `Y = Spec(B)`, and let `s` and `y` be the closed points of `S` and `Y` respectively. By hypothesis,
`A'` is isomorphic to a local ring `ūĚí™_{X, x}` of an `S`-scheme `X` √©tale over `S`, at a point `x ‚ąą X` lying over `s`.
Suppose given a local homomorphism `Ōą : A ‚Üí B`, making `Y` into an `S`-scheme; the problem is to see that there exists
one and only one `S`-morphism `f : Y ‚Üí X` such that `f(y) = x`. Set `X' = X √ó_S Y`, and note that since
`k(x) = k(s)`, there exists a single point `x' ‚ąą X'` lying over `x` and over `y`, and that `k(x') = k(y)`. It must be
shown that there exists a unique `Y`-section `f'` of `X'` such that `f'(y) = x'`. Now, the morphism `g : X' ‚Üí Y` is
√©tale and separated, and the fibre `X'_y = g^{‚ąí1}(y)` has at the point `x'` the local ring `k(x') = k(y)` for its local
ring. If one sets `Y_0 = Spec(k(y))`, there exists therefore a unique `Y_0`-section `f_0` of `X'_y` such that
`f_0(y) = x'`, and the conclusion follows from the assumption that `B` is Henselian and from `(18.5.11, e)`.

We shall say that a local `A`-algebra `A'` satisfying the conditions of `(18.6.2)` is **strictly essentially √©tale**.
Note that the criterion `(18.5.11, b)` signifies that, in order for `A` to be Henselian, it is necessary and sufficient
that every strictly essentially √©tale `A`-algebra be `A`-isomorphic to `A`.

**Lemma (18.6.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.3 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `A_1`, `A_2` two strictly essentially √©tale local `A`-algebras.*

*(i) There exists at most one `A`-homomorphism (necessarily local) from `A_1` to `A_2`.*

*(ii) There exist a strictly essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra `A_3` and two `A`-homomorphisms `A_1 ‚Üí A_3`,
`A_2 ‚Üí A_3`.*

Set `S = Spec(A)`; by hypothesis there are two `S`-schemes `X_1`, `X_2` √©tale over `S`, and two points `x_1 ‚ąą X_1`,
`x_2 ‚ąą X_2` lying over the closed point `s` of `S` and such that `A_1 = ūĚí™_{X_1, x_1}`, `A_2 = ūĚí™_{X_2, x_2}`. Set
`X_3 = X_1 √ó_S X_2`; the hypotheses `k(x_1) = k(x_2) = k(s)` entail that there exists a single point `x_3 ‚ąą X_3` lying
over `x_1` and `x_2` and that `k(x_3) = k(s)` `(I, 3.4.9)`. Moreover, `X_3` is √©tale over `S` `(17.3.3)`, so
`A_3 = ūĚí™_{X_3, x_3}` satisfies the conditions of (ii). On the other hand,

<!-- original page 137 -->

we have seen that an `A`-homomorphism from `A_1` to `A_2` is necessarily local; it corresponds to an `S`-morphism `f`
from `X_2 = Spec(A_2)` to `X_1` such that `f(x_2) = x_1`, or again, setting `X_3 = X_1 √ó_S X_2`, to an `X_2`-section
`f'` of `X_3` such that `f'(x_2) = x_3`. Since `k(x_3) = k(x_2)` and `X_3` is connected, the uniqueness of `f` follows
from `(17.4.9)`, whence (i).

**(18.6.4)** Denote by `ūĚĒĖ` the subset of the set `ūĚĒą` defined in `(18.6.1)` consisting of the strictly essentially √©tale
`A`-algebras belonging to `ūĚĒą`. It follows from `(18.6.3)` that the relation "there exists an `A`-homomorphism from `A_őĽ`
to `A_őľ`" is a preorder relation on `ūĚĒĖ` and makes `ūĚĒĖ` into an increasing filtered set. Indexing `ūĚĒĖ` by itself by means
of the identity map, it follows from `(18.6.3, (i))` that if `őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ` in `ūĚĒĖ`, there exists a unique `A`-homomorphism
`ŌÜ_{őľőĽ} : A_őĽ ‚Üí A_őľ` and `(A_őĽ, ŌÜ_{őľőĽ})` is evidently an inductive system of local `A`-algebras, the `ŌÜ_{őľőĽ}` being
local homomorphisms. Moreover, it follows from `(17.3.5)` that for `őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ`, `A_őľ` is a strictly essentially √©tale
`A_őĽ`-algebra.

**Definition (18.6.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.5 -->

*One calls the **Henselization** of a local ring `A`, and denotes by `^h A`, the inductive-limit `A`-algebra of the
inductive system `(A_őĽ, ŌÜ_{őľőĽ})` defined in `(18.6.4)`.*

This definition depends on the choice of `ūĚĒą` only in appearance; if `ūĚĒą'` is another set of essentially √©tale local
`A`-algebras with the same property as `ūĚĒą`, and `ūĚĒĖ'` is the subset of `ūĚĒą'` consisting of the strictly essentially √©tale
`A`-algebras, it follows from `(18.6.3, (ii))` that, for the preorder relations considered, `ūĚĒĖ` and `ūĚĒĖ'` are cofinal in
`ūĚĒĖ'' = ūĚĒą ‚ą™ ūĚĒą'`, hence give the same inductive limit up to isomorphism. We shall also see below `(18.6.6, (i) and (ii))`
that `^h A` and the canonical homomorphism `A ‚Üí ^h A` form a solution of a universal problem, and consequently are
determined up to unique isomorphism.

One will note that if `A` is a field, one obviously has `^h A = A`. In the general case, `^h A` is also the
Henselization of all the strictly essentially √©tale `A`-algebras `A_őĽ (őĽ ‚ąą ūĚĒĖ)`, for if `B` is a strictly essentially
√©tale `A_őĽ`-algebra, `B` is also a strictly essentially √©tale `A`-algebra `(18.6.1)`, hence (up to `A`-isomorphism) one
of the `A_őľ` for `őľ ‚©ĺ őĽ`, and the `A_őľ` such that `őľ ‚©ĺ őĽ` and such that `A_őľ` is a strictly essentially √©tale
`A_őĽ`-algebra form a cofinal set in the preordered set of the `A_őľ`, by virtue of `(18.6.1)` and `(18.6.3)`.

**Theorem (18.6.6).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.6 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `^h A` its Henselization.*

*(i) `^h A` is a Henselian local ring and the structure homomorphism `A ‚Üí ^h A` is local.*

*(ii) For every Henselian local ring `B`, the canonical map*

```text
  Hom.loc(^h A, B) ‚Üí Hom.loc(A, B)
```

*is bijective.*

*(iii) `^h A` is a faithfully flat `A`-module, and if `ūĚĒ™` is the maximal ideal of `A`, `ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^h A` is the maximal ideal
of `^h A`, and the homomorphism `A/ūĚĒ™ ‚Üí ^h A / ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^h A` of residue fields is bijective.*

*(iv) If `√ā` and `(^h A)^‚ąß` are the separated completions of the local rings `A` and `^h A`, the homomorphism
`√ā ‚Üí (^h A)^‚ąß` deduced from the structure homomorphism `A ‚Üí ^h A` by completion is bijective.*

*(v) In order for `^h A` to be Noetherian, it is necessary and sufficient that `A` be so.*

*(vi) If `A` is Henselian, the canonical homomorphism `A ‚Üí ^h A` is bijective.*

<!-- original page 138 -->

Let `ūĚĒ™_őĽ` be the maximal ideal of `A_őĽ`; it follows from the fact that, in `(17.6.1)`, a) implies c'), that for
`őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ`, one has `ūĚĒ™_őľ = ūĚĒ™_őĽ ¬∑ A_őľ`, the homomorphism `A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™_őĽ ‚Üí A_őľ / ūĚĒ™_őľ` is bijective, and `A_őľ` is a flat
`A_őĽ`-module. The fact that `^h A` is local, that the homomorphism `A ‚Üí ^h A` is local, assertion (iii), and the
sufficiency of (v) follow therefore from `(0_III, 10.3.1.3)`; the necessity of (v) follows from the fact that `^h A` is
a faithfully flat `A`-module `(0_I, 6.5.2)`.

To prove that `^h A` is Henselian, let us apply the criterion `(18.5.11, b)`. Set `S = Spec(^h A)`,
`S_0 = Spec(^h A / ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^h A)`, and let `g : S' ‚Üí S` be an √©tale morphism; set `S'_0 = g^{‚ąí1}(S_0)`, and let
`f_0 : S_0 ‚Üí S'_0` be an `S_0`-section of `S'_0`. Reasoning as in `(18.5.4)`, one may suppose that `g` is of finite
presentation. It then follows from `(8.8.2)` and `(17.7.5)` that there exist an index `őĽ ‚ąą ūĚĒĖ`, an √©tale morphism
`g^{(őĽ)} : S'^{(őĽ)} ‚Üí S^{(őĽ)} = Spec(A_őĽ)` and, on setting `S_0^{(őĽ)} = Spec(A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™ A_őĽ)` and
`S'_0^{(őĽ)} = g^{(őĽ)‚ąí1}(S_0^{(őĽ)})`, an `S_0^{(őĽ)}`-section `f_0^{(őĽ)} : S_0^{(őĽ)} ‚Üí S'_0^{(őĽ)}` such that
`S' = S'^{(őĽ)} √ó_{S^{(őĽ)}} S`, `g = g^{(őĽ)} √ó 1` and `f_0 = f_0^{(őĽ)} √ó 1`. Let `s` be the closed point of `S`,
`x = f_0(s)`, `x^{(őĽ)}` the projection of `x` in `S'^{(őĽ)}`; since `x^{(őĽ)}` lies over the closed point `s^{(őĽ)}` of
`S^{(őĽ)}`, the local ring `C^{(őĽ)}` of `S'^{(őĽ)}` at the point `x^{(őĽ)}` is an essentially √©tale `A_őĽ`-algebra;
moreover, since `f_0^{(őĽ)}(s^{(őĽ)}) = x^{(őĽ)}`, one has `k(x^{(őĽ)}) = k(s^{(őĽ)})`, in other words, `C^{(őĽ)}` is a
strictly essentially √©tale local `A_őĽ`-algebra, and consequently `(18.6.1)` is `A_őĽ`-isomorphic to an `A`-algebra `A_őľ`
with `őľ ‚©ĺ őĽ`. There is therefore an `A_őĽ`-homomorphism `C^{(őĽ)} ‚Üí ^h A`, that is to say an `S^{(őĽ)}`-morphism
`h : S ‚Üí S'^{(őĽ)}` such that `h(s) = x^{(őĽ)}`, and consequently there does exist an `S`-section `f` of `S'` such that
`f(s) = x`, which completes the proof of (i).

To prove (ii), it suffices to note that one has `Hom.loc(^h A, B) = lim_‚Üí Hom.loc(A_őĽ, B)` and that by `(18.6.2)` the
canonical homomorphisms `Hom.loc(A_őĽ, B) ‚Üź Hom.loc(A, B)` are bijective.

To establish (iv), note that for every `őĽ` and every integer `n > 0`, one has `ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n = ūĚĒ™^n ¬∑ A_őĽ`, and
`(ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^h A)^n = ūĚĒ™^n ¬∑ ^h A = lim_‚Üí ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n`. One concludes, by the exactness of the functor `lim_‚Üí` in the category of
`A`-modules, that `^h A / (ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^h A)^n = lim_‚Üí (A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n)`, and consequently it suffices to show that, for every
integer `n` and every index `őĽ`, the homomorphism `A / ūĚĒ™^n ‚Üí A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n` is bijective. Now, this is true by hypothesis
for `n = 1`; on the other hand, since `A_őĽ` is a flat `A`-module, one has

```text
  ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n / ūĚĒ™_őĽ^{n+1} = (ūĚĒ™^n / ūĚĒ™^{n+1}) ‚äó_A A_őĽ = (ūĚĒ™^n / ūĚĒ™^{n+1}) ‚äó_{A/ūĚĒ™} (A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™ A_őĽ),
```

and since `A/ūĚĒ™ ‚Üí A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™ A_őĽ = A_őĽ / ūĚĒ™_őĽ` is bijective, the homomorphism `ūĚĒ™^n / ūĚĒ™^{n+1} ‚Üí ūĚĒ™_őĽ^n / ūĚĒ™_őĽ^{n+1}` is also
bijective; the conclusion follows therefore from `(Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. III, ¬ß 2, n¬į 8, cor. 3 of th. 1)`.

Finally, if `A` is Henselian, it follows from the remark preceding `(18.6.3)` that the homomorphisms `A ‚Üí A_őĽ` are
bijective, which proves (vi) by the definition of `^h A`.

**(18.6.7)** Now let `A` be a semi-local ring, `ūĚĒ™_i (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)` its maximal ideals. One calls the **Henselization** of
`A` and one still denotes by `^h A` the product ring `‚ąŹ ^h(A_{ūĚĒ™_i})` of the Henselizations of the local rings
`A_{ūĚĒ™_i}`. It is a faithfully flat `A`-module and a semi-local `A`-algebra whose maximal ideals are the
`ūĚĒ™_i ¬∑ ^h A` by virtue of `(18.6.6, (iii))`; moreover, if `ūĚĒĮ = ‚čā ūĚĒ™_i` is the radical of `A`, it follows from what
precedes that `ūĚĒĮ ¬∑ ^h A`

<!-- original page 139 -->

is the radical of `^h A` and that the canonical map `A / ūĚĒĮ ‚Üí ^h A / ūĚĒĮ ¬∑ ^h A` is bijective. As the separated completion
`√ā` of `A` for the `ūĚĒĮ`-preadic topology is the product of the separated completions `√ā_{ūĚĒ™_i}`
`(Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. III, ¬ß 2, n¬į 13, prop. 18)`, the canonical homomorphism `√ā ‚Üí (^h A)^‚ąß` is bijective by
`(18.6.6, (iv))`, and it is clear by `(18.6.6, (v))` that in order for `^h A` to be Noetherian it is necessary and
sufficient that `A` be so.

To obtain the analogue of the universal property `(18.6.6, (ii))`, let us agree, when `A` and `B` are two semi-local
rings of radicals `ūĚĒĮ`, `ūĚĒį` respectively, to call a **semi-local homomorphism** from `A` to `B` any homomorphism `ŌÜ`
such that `ŌÜ(ūĚĒĮ) ‚äā ūĚĒį`. In the particular case where `B` is a product of local rings `B_j (1 ‚©Ĺ j ‚©Ĺ n)`, the datum of
such a homomorphism amounts to that of its projections `ŌÜ_j : A ‚Üí B_j`, which are homomorphisms subject only to the
condition that `ŌÜ_j(ūĚĒĮ) ‚äā ūĚĒę_j`, where `ūĚĒę_j` is the maximal ideal of `B_j`. Furthermore, if `ūĚĒ™_i (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ m)` are the
maximal ideals of `A`, the prime ideal `ŌÜ_j^{‚ąí1}(ūĚĒę_j)` must contain one of the `ūĚĒ™_i` since it contains their
intersection `ūĚĒĮ`, hence it equals one of the `ūĚĒ™_i` and `ŌÜ_j` factors as `A ‚Üí A_{ūĚĒ™_i} ‚Üí^{ŌÜ'_j} B_j`, where `ŌÜ'_j` is a
local homomorphism. If `Hom.sloc(A, B)` denotes the set of semi-local homomorphisms from `A` to `B`, one can thus
identify this set with `‚ąŹ_j (‚čÉ_i Hom.loc(A_{ūĚĒ™_i}, B_j))` in the case considered; as a Henselian semi-local ring is a
direct product of Henselian local rings, one sees that the universal property `(18.6.6, (ii))` is still valid for a
semi-local ring `A`, on replacing local homomorphisms by semi-local homomorphisms.

**Proposition (18.6.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.8 -->

*Let `A` be a semi-local ring, `B` a semi-local `A`-algebra that is integral over `A`. Then `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` is a
semi-local `B`-algebra isomorphic to `^h B`.*

Since `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` is integral over `^h A`, each of its maximal ideals lies over one of the maximal ideals of `^h A`,
hence over a maximal ideal of `A`, and since `B` is integral over `A`, the prime ideals of `B` lying over a maximal
ideal of `A` are maximal ideals of `B`, so that finally a maximal ideal of `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` has as projections in
`Spec(^h A)` and `Spec(B)` maximal ideals; taking into account `(18.6.6, (iii))` and `(I, 3.4.9)`, one concludes that
the ring `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` is semi-local. Moreover, for every Henselian local ring `C`,
`Hom.sloc(B ‚äó_A (^h A), C)` is in bijective correspondence with the set of pairs `(ŌÜ, Ōą)` of semi-local homomorphisms
`ŌÜ : B ‚Üí C`, `Ōą : ^h A ‚Üí C` such that the composites `A ‚Üí B ‚Üí^ŌÜ C`, `A ‚Üí ^h A ‚Üí^Ōą C` are equal. But by virtue of the
bijective correspondence between `Hom.sloc(A, C)` and `Hom.sloc(^h A, C)`, one sees that for every
`ŌÜ ‚ąą Hom.sloc(B, C)` there exists one and only one `Ōą` having the previous property, hence the map
`Hom.sloc(B ‚äó_A (^h A), C) ‚Üí Hom.sloc(B, C)` is bijective, which proves the proposition by virtue of the uniqueness of
the solution of a universal problem.

**Theorem (18.6.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.9 -->

*Let `A` be a semi-local ring.*

*(i) In order for `^h A` to be reduced (resp. normal), it is necessary and sufficient that `A` be so.*

*(ii) Suppose `A` Noetherian. Then, for every prime ideal `ūĚĒ≠` of `A`, the ring `(^h A)_ūĚĒ≠ / ūĚĒ≠ ¬∑ (^h A)_ūĚĒ≠` is a direct
composite of a finite number of separable algebraic extensions of `k(ūĚĒ≠)` (which implies that the fibres of the
canonical morphism `Spec(^h A) ‚Üí Spec(A)` are geometrically regular).*

<!-- original page 140 -->

(i) Since `^h A` is a faithfully flat `A`-module, it follows from `(2.1.13)` that if `^h A` is reduced (resp. normal),
the same holds for `A`. To prove the converse, one may restrict to the case where the ring `A` is local, so that with
the notations of `(18.6.4)`, one has `^h A = lim_‚Üí A_őĽ`. Now, it follows from the definition of the `A_őĽ` and from
`(17.5.7)` that if `A` is reduced (resp. integral and integrally closed), the same holds for the `A_őĽ`; moreover, the
homomorphisms `A_őĪ ‚Üí A_ő≤` for `őĪ ‚©Ĺ ő≤` are injective by virtue of `(0_I, 6.5.1)` since `A_ő≤` is a faithfully flat
`A_őĪ`-module; hence the morphisms `Spec(A_ő≤) ‚Üí Spec(A_őĪ)` are dominant, and one concludes from `(5.13.2)` (resp.
`(5.13.4)`) that `^h A` is reduced (resp. integral and integrally closed).

(ii) One may restrict to the case where `A` is local. By virtue of the fact that the functor `lim_‚Üí` commutes with
tensor products, the fibre of the morphism `Spec(^h A) ‚Üí Spec(A)` at a point `x` is the inductive limit of the fibres of
the morphisms `Spec(A_őĽ) ‚Üí Spec(A)` at this point. Since `^h A` is Noetherian, one sees, taking into account
`(17.6.2)`, that one is reduced to proving the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.6.9.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.9.1 -->

*Let `(B_őĪ)` be an inductive system of Artinian rings, `B = lim_‚Üí B_őĪ` its inductive limit. If `B` is Noetherian, then
`B` is Artinian; if moreover, for `őĪ ‚©Ĺ ő≤`, the homomorphisms `ŌÜ_{ő≤őĪ} : B_őĪ ‚Üí B_ő≤` are injective, then there exists `őĽ`
such that, for `őĪ ‚©ĺ őĽ`, the number of local components `B_{őĪi}` of `B_őĪ` is constant, `ŌÜ_{ő≤őĪ}(B_{őĪi}) ‚äā B_{ő≤i}` for
every `i`, and the `B_i = lim_‚Üí B_{őĪi}` are the local components of `B`.*

In effect, let `Y_őĪ = Spec(B_őĪ)`, `Y = Spec(B)`, which as a topological space is equal to `lim_‚Üź Y_őĪ` `(8.2.10)`. The
spaces `Y_őĪ` are finite and discrete; moreover, if the `ŌÜ_{ő≤őĪ}` are injective, the canonical morphisms `Y_ő≤ ‚Üí Y_őĪ` are
dominant, hence surjective. The lemma will therefore result from the following purely topological lemma:

**Lemma (18.6.9.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.9.2 -->

*Let `(Y_őĪ, Ōą_{őĪő≤})` be a projective system of finite discrete topological spaces. If `Y = lim_‚Üź Y_őĪ` is Noetherian,
`Y` is finite and discrete. If moreover the `Ōą_{őĪő≤}` are surjective, there exists `őĽ` such that for `őĪ ‚©ĺ őĽ`, the number
of elements of `Y_őĪ` is constant and equal to the number of elements of `Y`.*

In effect, `Y` being compact and Noetherian, every part of `Y` is compact, hence closed, which implies that `Y` is
discrete, hence finite since it is compact. If moreover the `Ōą_{őĪő≤}` are surjective, the same holds for
`Ōą_őĪ : Y ‚Üí Y_őĪ`, hence `Card(Y) ‚©Ĺ Card(Y_őĪ)`, and as `Card(Y_őĪ)` is an increasing function of `őĪ`, there exists `őĽ`
such that for `őĪ ‚©ĺ őĽ`, `Card(Y_őĪ) = Card(Y_őĽ)`; the `Ōą_{őĪő≤}` are then bijective for `őĪ ‚©ĺ őĽ` and one has therefore
`Card(Y) = Card(Y_őĽ)`.

**Corollary (18.6.10).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.10 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. In order for `^h A` to possess one of the following properties:*

*a) being a Cohen-Macaulay ring `(0, 16.5.3)`;*

*b) satisfying property `(S_n)` `(5.7.3)`;*

*c) being regular;*

*d) satisfying property `(R_n)` `(5.8.2)`;*

*it is necessary and sufficient that `A` possess this same property.*

<!-- original page 141 -->

This follows immediately from the fact that the fibres of the morphism `Spec(^h A) ‚Üí Spec(A)` are geometrically regular
`(18.6.9)` and from `(6.4.1)`, `(6.5.1)` and `(6.5.3)`.

**Corollary (18.6.11).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.11 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian semi-local ring. In order for a prime ideal `ūĚĒ≠` of `^h A` to belong to `Ass(^h A)`, it is
necessary and sufficient that `ūĚĒ≠ ‚ą© A` belong to `Ass(A)`.*

Taking into account the description of the fibres of the morphism `Spec(^h A) ‚Üí Spec(A)`, this follows immediately from
`(3.3.1)`.

**Proposition (18.6.12).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.12 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring; the following properties are equivalent:*

*a) `A` is unibranch `(0, 23.2.1)` (resp. reduced and unibranch).*

*b) For every strictly essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra `A'`, `Spec(A')` is irreducible (resp. integral).*

*c) `Spec(^h A)` is irreducible (resp. integral).*

Let us first note that `^h(A_{red}) = (^h A)_{red}`, for `^h(A_{red}) = A_{red} ‚äó_A (^h A)` `(18.6.8)`, and since
`^h(A_{red})` is reduced `(18.6.9)`, it equals `(^h A)_{red}`. This permits us, in the proof, to consider only the case
where `A` is reduced, so that `A'` is also reduced `(17.5.7)` and `^h A` is also reduced `(18.6.9)`.

With the notations of `(18.6.4)`, condition b) signifies that all the `A_őĽ` are integral; one concludes that
`^h A = lim_‚Üí A_őĽ` is integral `(5.13.3)`, so b) entails c).

It is clear that if `^h A` is integral, the same holds for the `A_őĽ ‚äā ^h A`, so c) entails b). Let us show that c)
entails a). Note first that `A ‚äā ^h A` is integral; let `K` and `L` be the fields of fractions of `A` and `^h A`
respectively. It suffices to see that for every finite `A`-algebra `B ‚äā K`, `B` is a local ring. Now `B` is a
semi-local ring, whose Henselization is therefore `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` `(18.6.8)`. By flatness, `B ‚äó_A (^h A)` identifies
with a subring of `K ‚äó_A (^h A)` and the latter with a subring of `L`, so `^h B` is integral. But since `^h B` is a
Henselian semi-local ring, it is a direct composite of local rings `(18.5.9)`, hence can be integral only if it is
local, which entails that `B` is local.

Let us finally prove that a) implies c). Let `C` be the integral closure of the integral ring `A`. Since `C` is a local
ring by hypothesis, `C ‚äó_A (^h A)` is the Henselization of `C` `(18.6.8)`, hence is integral and integrally closed
`(18.6.9)`. Since `A` is a subring of `C`, `^h A` identifies with a subring of `^h C` by flatness, so it too is
integral, which completes the proof.

**Corollary (18.6.13).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.13 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian local ring. Then, in order for `A` to be unibranch, it is necessary and sufficient that `A` be
integral.*

**Proposition (18.6.14).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.14 -->

*(i) Every filtered inductive limit of Henselian local rings (where the transition homomorphisms are local) is a
Henselian local ring.*

*(ii) The functor `A ‚Ü¶ ^h A` on the category of local rings (where the morphisms are the local homomorphisms) commutes
with filtered inductive limits.*

*(iii) Every Henselian local ring `A` is the inductive limit of a filtered inductive system of Henselian and
Noetherian local rings `A_őĽ` (the transition homomorphisms `A_őĽ ‚Üí A_őľ` being local).*

(i) Let `(A_őĽ)` be a filtered inductive system of Henselian local rings, where the transition homomorphisms are local.
Let us show that `A' = lim_‚Üí A_őĽ`, which is local `(0_III, 10.3.1.3)`, is Henselian. Let `C` be an √©tale `A'`-algebra,
`ūĚĒę` a prime ideal

<!-- original page 142 -->

of `C` lying over the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™'` of `A'`, such that `C_ūĚĒę` is strictly essentially √©tale over `A'` `(18.6.1)`,
in other words such that `k(ūĚĒ™') ‚Üí k(ūĚĒę)` is an isomorphism. By virtue of `(8.8.2, (ii))` and `(17.7.8)`, there exist an
index `őĽ` and an √©tale `A_őĽ`-algebra `C_őĽ` such that `C = C_őĽ ‚äó_{A_őĽ} A'` up to isomorphism; moreover, if `ūĚĒę_őĽ` is the
inverse image of `ūĚĒę` in `C_őĽ`, and `ūĚĒ™_őĽ` the maximal ideal of `A_őĽ`, one may, by virtue of `(8.8.2.4)` and the
transitivity of fibres `(I, 3.6.4)`, suppose that `(C_őĽ)_{ūĚĒę_őĽ}` is strictly essentially √©tale over `A_őĽ`; since `A_őĽ` is
Henselian, `(C_őĽ)_{ūĚĒę_őĽ}` is necessarily isomorphic to `A_őĽ` `(18.5.11, b)`; there is consequently a neighbourhood of
`ūĚĒę_őĽ` in `Spec(C_őĽ)` isomorphic to `Spec(A_őĽ)` `(I, 6.5.4)`; one concludes that there is a neighbourhood of `ūĚĒę` in
`Spec(C)` isomorphic to `Spec(A')`, hence `C_ūĚĒę` is isomorphic to `A'`, which proves that `A'` is Henselian `(18.6.2)`
and completes the proof.

(ii) Suppose that `B = lim_‚Üí B_őĽ`, where the `B_őĽ` are local rings, the transition homomorphisms `B_őĽ ‚Üí B_őľ (őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ)`
being local. Set `A_őĽ = ^h B_őĽ`; the `A_őĽ` are Henselian local rings `(18.6.6, (vi))`, and by virtue of
`(18.6.6, (ii))` the transition homomorphisms `B_őĽ ‚Üí B_őľ` determine in a unique way local homomorphisms
`A_őĽ ‚Üí A_őľ`, so that `(A_őĽ)` is an inductive system. Everything reduces to seeing that `A' = lim_‚Üí A_őĽ` is canonically
isomorphic to `A = ^h B`. By virtue of `(18.6.6, (ii))` and the definition of inductive limits, one has, for every
Henselian local ring `E`,

```text
  Hom.loc(A', E) = lim_‚Üź Hom.loc(A_őĽ, E) = lim_‚Üź Hom.loc(B_őĽ, E) = Hom.loc(B, E).
```

But since `A'` is Henselian by (i), this proves our assertion.

(iii) The ring `A` is a filtered inductive limit of its Noetherian local subrings `B_őĽ`, the transition homomorphisms
being local `(5.13.3, (iii))`. Since the `^h B_őĽ` are Henselian and Noetherian local rings `(18.6.6, (v))` and
`A = ^h A` `(18.6.6, (vi))`, it suffices to apply (ii) to the inductive system `(B_őĽ)`.

**Corollary (18.6.15).**

<!-- label: IV.18.6.15 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian local ring, `X` an `A`-prescheme of finite presentation over `A`. There then exist a Henselian
Noetherian local ring `A_0`, a local homomorphism `A_0 ‚Üí A`, a prescheme `X_0` of finite type over `A_0`, and an
`A`-isomorphism `X_0 ‚äó_{A_0} A ‚•≤ X`.*

This results from `(18.6.14)` and `(8.8.2, (ii))`.

### 18.7. Henselization and excellent rings

**(18.7.1)** We shall denote in this n¬į by `P(Z, k)` a property of the form considered in `(7.3.1)`, where we suppose in
addition that the property `Q(A, k)` satisfies the following condition:

For every separable algebraic extension `k'` of `k`, and every Noetherian local `k'`-algebra `A`, the property `Q(A, k)`
is equivalent to `Q(A, k')`.

It is immediate that all the properties `P` considered in `(7.3.8)` satisfy the preceding condition, taking into
account that if `K` is a finite extension of `k`, `k' ‚äó_k K` is a direct composite of separable algebraic extensions of
`K`.

<!-- original page 143 -->

**Proposition (18.7.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.2 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. In order for `^h A` to be a `P`-ring `(7.3.13)`, it is necessary and sufficient
that `A` be so.*

In effect, by virtue of `(18.6.6, (iv))`, the completion `√ā` of `A` is also the completion of `^h A`, and one has
therefore `A ‚äā ^h A ‚äā √ā`. According to `(18.6.9)`, for every `x ‚ąą Spec(A)`, the fibre at `x` of the morphism
`Spec(^h A) ‚Üí Spec(A)` is discrete and finite, and at each of the points `y_i` of this fibre, `k(y_i)` is a separable
algebraic extension of `k(x)`. The formal fibre of `A` at the point `x` is therefore a prescheme that is the sum of the
formal fibres of `^h A` at the points `y_i`. The conclusion then follows from the hypothesis made on `P` in
`(18.7.1)`.

**Corollary (18.7.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.3 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. In order for `^h A` to be universally Japanese, it is necessary and sufficient that
`A` be so.*

In effect, for a Noetherian local ring `B`, it amounts to the same thing to say that `B` is universally Japanese, or
that the formal fibres of `B` are geometrically reduced `(7.6.4 and 7.7.1)`; the conclusion follows therefore from
`(18.7.2)`.

**Corollary (18.7.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.4 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. In order for the formal fibres of `^h A` to be geometrically regular, it is
necessary and sufficient that those of `A` be so.*

This is a particular case of `(18.7.2)`.

**Proposition (18.7.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.5 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. If `A` is universally catenary `(5.6.2)` (resp. formally catenary `(7.1.9)`,
resp. strictly formally catenary `(7.2.6)`), the same holds for `^h A`.*

With the notations of `(18.6.4)`, if `A` is universally catenary, the same holds for the `A_őĽ`, which are essentially
of finite type `A`-algebras `(5.6.3)`. To prove that in this case `^h A` is universally catenary, it will therefore
suffice to establish the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.7.5.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.5.1 -->

*Let `(B_őĪ, ŌÜ_{ő≤őĪ})` be an inductive system of Noetherian rings and suppose that, for `őĪ ‚©Ĺ ő≤`, `ŌÜ_{ő≤őĪ}` makes `B_ő≤` a
faithfully flat `B_őĪ`-module and that `B = lim_‚Üí B_őĪ` is Noetherian. Then, if the `B_őĪ` are catenary (resp. universally
catenary), the same holds for `B`.*

To say that `B` is universally catenary amounts to saying that `B[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]` is catenary for every `n` `(5.6.2)`,
and it is immediate that the inductive system `(B_őĪ[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n])` satisfies the same conditions as `(B_őĪ)`; it
suffices therefore to prove that if the `B_őĪ` are catenary, the same holds for `B`. If `ūĚĒ≠_0 ‚äÉ ūĚĒ≠_1 ‚äÉ ‚čĮ ‚äÉ ūĚĒ≠_n` is a
saturated chain of prime ideals of `B`, the inverse images `ūĚĒ≠_{0őĪ} ‚äÉ ūĚĒ≠_{1őĪ} ‚äÉ ‚čĮ ‚äÉ ūĚĒ≠_{nőĪ}` of these ideals in `B_őĪ`
form a chain of prime ideals, and it suffices to show that for `őĪ` sufficiently large, this chain is saturated. Now,
each `ūĚĒ≠_i` is an ideal of finite type, hence there exists `őĽ` such that, for `őĪ ‚©ĺ őĽ`, one has `ūĚĒ≠_{iőĪ} = ūĚĒ≠_{iőĪ+1}`. One
has therefore `1 = codim(V(ūĚĒ≠_i), V(ūĚĒ≠_{i+1})) = codim(V(ūĚĒ≠_{iőĪ}), V(ūĚĒ≠_{i+1, őĪ}))` since `B` is a flat `B_őĪ`-module
(`(6.1.4)` and `(0_I, 6.2.3)`), which completes the proof of the lemma.

Suppose now `A` formally catenary. Let `ūĚĒ≠` be a prime ideal of `^h A`, `ūĚĒģ` its inverse image in `A`, so that
`ūĚĒģ ¬∑ ^h A ‚äā ūĚĒ≠`, and consequently `^h A / ūĚĒ≠` is a quotient ring of `^h A / ūĚĒģ ¬∑ ^h A`. It suffices therefore to prove
that `^h A / ūĚĒģ ¬∑ ^h A` is formally catenary for every prime ideal `ūĚĒģ` of `A` `(7.1.9)`, and since
`^h A / ūĚĒģ ¬∑ ^h A = ^h(A/ūĚĒģ)` by `(18.6.8)`, it suffices `(7.1.11)` to see that if `A` is integral and formally catenary,
`^h A` is formally equidimensional. But since the completion of `^h A` is equal to `√ā`, this follows from the
hypothesis that `A` is formally catenary.

<!-- original page 144 -->

Suppose finally `A` strictly formally catenary; then we have just seen that `^h A` is formally catenary, and it remains
to prove that the fibres of the morphism `Spec(√ā) ‚Üí Spec(^h A)` satisfy property `(S_1)` `(7.2.5, b)`. But this follows
from the hypothesis on `A` and from `(18.7.2)` applied to the case where `P(Z, k)` is the property `(S_1)` for `Z`.

**Corollary (18.7.6).**

<!-- label: IV.18.7.6 -->

*If a Noetherian local ring `A` is excellent `(7.8.2)`, the same holds for `^h A`.*

**Remark (18.7.7).** ‚ÄĒ It can happen that `^h A` is an excellent local ring without `A` being universally catenary (nor
consequently excellent). To see this, let us take up again the example of the ring `A` of `(5.6.11)`, with the same
notations. The ring `E` constructed in `(5.6.11)` is excellent when `k_0` is of characteristic `0` `(7.8.3, (ii) and
(iii))`; consider two rings `E_1`, `E_2` isomorphic to `E`, and "glue" `Spec(E_1)` and `Spec(E_2)` so that if `ūĚĒ™_i` and
`ūĚĒ™'_i` are the maximal ideals of `E_i (i = 1, 2)` corresponding to `ūĚĒ™` and `ūĚĒ™'`, the point `ūĚĒ™_1` is "glued" to `ūĚĒ™_2`
and the point `ūĚĒ™'_1` to `ūĚĒ™'_2`; in precise terms, if `őĶ_i` and `őĶ'_i` are the canonical homomorphisms of `E_i` onto
`k(ūĚĒ™_i)` and `k(ūĚĒ™'_i) (i = 1, 2)`, the scheme obtained is `Spec(R)`, where `R` is the subring of `E_1 √ó E_2` consisting
of pairs `(x_1, x_2)` such that `őĶ_2(x_2) = őĶ_1(x_1)` and `őĶ'_1(x_1) = őĶ'_2(x_2)`. One verifies easily (for example
with the help of `(17.6.3)`) that `R` is a finite √©tale `C`-algebra, and that there are two maximal ideals `ūĚĒĮ_1`,
`ūĚĒĮ_2` of `R` lying over the maximal ideal `ūĚĒę` of `C`, the completions of `R_{ūĚĒĮ_1}` and `R_{ūĚĒĮ_2}` being canonically
isomorphic to that of `A = C_ūĚĒę`; `R_{ūĚĒĮ_1}` is therefore a strictly essentially √©tale `A`-algebra, and consequently its
Henselization is equal to `^h A`. Moreover, one has seen `(7.8.4, (ii))` that the formal fibres of `A` are
geometrically regular, hence the same holds for those of `^h A` `(18.7.2)`. Finally, the ring `R` is universally
catenary, for its quotients by its two minimal prime ideals are isomorphic to `E`, whence the conclusion by
`(5.6.3, (iii))`. The ring `R_{ūĚĒĮ_1}` is therefore excellent, and consequently the same holds for `^h A` `(18.7.6)`,
whereas `A` is not universally catenary.

### 18.8. Strictly local rings and strict Henselization

**Proposition (18.8.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.1 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring. The following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) `A` is a Henselian ring and its residue field `k` is separably closed.*

*b) `A` is a Henselian ring and every √©tale cover of `S = Spec(A)` is trivial.*

*c) For every √©tale morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f(x)` is the closed point `s` of `S`, there
exists an `S`-section `u : S ‚Üí X` of `X` such that `u(s) = x`.*

The hypothesis c) implies that for every √©tale morphism `f : X ‚Üí S`, the residue fields at the points of `f^{‚ąí1}(s)`
are all isomorphic to `k`, and moreover that condition b) of `(18.5.11)` is satisfied. Hence `A` is Henselian, and the
fact that every √©tale cover of `S` is trivial follows from `(18.2.10, (ii))`; so c) implies b). As the √©tale covers of
`Spec(k)` are trivial if and only if `k` is separably closed, b) implies a) by virtue of `(18.5.11, b)`. Finally, it
also follows from `(18.5.11, b)` that a) implies c).

**Definition (18.8.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.2 -->

*One says that a local ring is a **strictly local ring** if it satisfies the equivalent conditions of `(18.8.1)`. One
calls a **strictly local scheme** a scheme isomorphic to the spectrum of a strictly local ring.*

**Remark (18.8.3).** ‚ÄĒ The conditions of `(18.8.1)` are also equivalent to the following:

*d) For every morphism `f : X ‚Üí S` locally of finite type and every point `x ‚ąą X` such that `f(x)` is the closed point
`s` of `S` and that `ūĚí™_{X, x} / ūĚĒ™_s ¬∑ ūĚí™_{X, x}` is a finite separable extension of `k(s) = k`, there exists an open
neighbourhood `U` of `x` in `X` such that `f | U` is a closed immersion.*

<!-- original page 145 -->

(One will note that the hypothesis of d) is satisfied if `f` is formally unramified at the point `x` `(17.4.1.2)`.)

We leave the proof to the reader, it being substantially the same as that of `(18.5.18)`.

**Lemma (18.8.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.4 -->

*Let `A`, `A'` be two local rings, `ŌÜ : A ‚Üí A'` a local homomorphism making `A'` into an essentially √©tale `A`-algebra
`(18.6.1)`. Then, for every strictly local ring `B`, every local homomorphism `Ōą : A ‚Üí B`, and every
`k(A)`-algebra homomorphism `őĪ : k(A') ‚Üí k(B)`, there exist one and only one local homomorphism `Ōą' : A' ‚Üí B` such
that `Ōą = Ōą' ‚ąė ŌÜ` and that `őĪ` equals the homomorphism `ŌąŐĄ'` deduced from `Ōą'` by passage to the quotients.*

With the notations of `(18.6.2)`, the homomorphism `őĪ` corresponds to a `k(y)`-morphism `Spec(k(y)) ‚Üí Spec(k(x'))`,
hence to a well-determined point `x'` in `X'` lying over `y`. The existence of the `Y`-section `f'` of `X'` such that
`f'(y) = x'` follows therefore from `(18.8.1, c)` since `f'` is √©tale and `B` strictly local, and its uniqueness
follows from the fact that `f'` is separated and from `(17.4.9)`.

**Lemma (18.8.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.5 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `A_1`, `A_2` two essentially √©tale local `A`-algebras, `K` a field, extension of `k(A)`.*

*(i) For every `k(A)`-homomorphism `ő≥ : k(A_1) ‚Üí k(A_2)`, there exists at most one `A`-homomorphism (necessarily local)
`Ōą : A_1 ‚Üí A_2` such that `ő≥` is the homomorphism `ŌąŐĄ` deduced from `Ōą` by passage to the quotients.*

*(ii) For every pair of local `A`-homomorphisms `ő≤_1 : A_1 ‚Üí K`, `ő≤_2 : A_2 ‚Üí K`, there exist an essentially √©tale local
`A`-algebra `A_3`, two `A`-homomorphisms `ŌÜ_1 : A_1 ‚Üí A_3`, `ŌÜ_2 : A_2 ‚Üí A_3`, and an `A`-homomorphism `ő≤ : A_3 ‚Üí K`
such that `ő≤_1 = ő≤ ‚ąė ŌÜ_1` and `ő≤_2 = ő≤ ‚ąė ŌÜ_2`.*

With the notations of `(18.6.3)`, the homomorphisms `ő≤_1`, `ő≤_2` in (ii) correspond to two `S`-morphisms
`Spec(K) ‚Üí X_1`, `Spec(K) ‚Üí X_2`, with respective images `x_1`, `x_2`; one deduces from them a well-determined
`S`-morphism `Spec(K) ‚Üí X_3` `(I, 3.2.1)` with image `x_3` lying over `x_1` and `x_2`; the `A`-algebra
`A_3 = ūĚí™_{X_3, x_3}` and the homomorphism `A_3 ‚Üí K` corresponding to these data satisfy the conditions of (ii). On the
other hand, the datum of `ő≥` in (i) corresponds to an `S`-morphism `Spec(k(x_2)) ‚Üí Spec(k(x_1))`, or again to a point
`x_3` of `X_3` lying over `x_2`; the uniqueness of `Ōą` follows from the uniqueness of an `X_2`-section of `X_3` passing
through `x_3` `(17.4.9)`.

**(18.8.6)** Let `A` be a local ring, `ūĚĒ™` its maximal ideal, `k` its residue field, `ő©` a separable closure of `k`.
Consider the set `ūĚĒą` of essentially √©tale `A`-algebras defined in `(18.6.1)`, and denote by `L` the set of local
`A`-homomorphisms `A' ‚Üí ő©`, where `A'` ranges over `ūĚĒą`; one will note that such a homomorphism factors as
`őĽ : A' ‚Üí^{ŌČ_őĽ} k(A') ‚Üí ő©`, where `ŌČ_őĽ : k(A') ‚Üí ő©` is an `A`-homomorphism (since `ūĚĒ™ A'` is the maximal ideal of `A'`);
conversely, the datum of such a homomorphism `ŌČ_őĽ` uniquely determines a homomorphism `őĽ ‚ąą L`. For every `őĽ ‚ąą L`, we
shall denote by `A_őĽ` the essentially √©tale `A`-algebra belonging to `ūĚĒą` on which `őĽ` is defined. The set `L` is
preordered by the relation "there exists an `A`-homomorphism `ŌÜ_{őľőĽ} : A_őĽ ‚Üí A_őľ` such that `őĽ = ŌÜ_{őľőĽ}^* ‚ąė őľ`", and it
follows from `(18.8.5, (i))` that this homomorphism `ŌÜ_{őľőĽ}` is unique. Moreover, `L` is increasingly filtered for the
preceding preorder relation (which we still note `őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ`), by virtue

<!-- original page 146 -->

of `(18.8.5, (ii))`. It is clear that `(A_őĽ, ŌÜ_{őľőĽ})` is an inductive system of local `A`-algebras, the `ŌÜ_{őľőĽ}` being
local homomorphisms; moreover, it follows from `(17.3.5)` that for `őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ`, `A_őľ` is an essentially √©tale
`A_őĽ`-algebra. If `ŌÜŐĄ_{őľőĽ} : k(A_őĽ) ‚Üí k(A_őľ)` is the `A`-homomorphism deduced from `ŌÜ_{őľőĽ}` by passage to the
quotients, `(k(A_őĽ), ŌÜŐĄ_{őľőĽ})` is an inductive system of separable algebraic extensions of `k`, and the
`ŌČ_őĽ : k(A_őĽ) ‚Üí ő©` form an inductive system of `k`-homomorphisms. Moreover, the inductive limit

```text
  (18.8.6.1)    ŌČ : lim_‚Üí k(A_őĽ) ‚Üí ő©
```

is a `k`-isomorphism. It suffices in effect to prove that for every finite separable extension `k'` of `k`, there
exists an essentially √©tale `A`-algebra `A'` such that `k'` is the residue field of `A'` and that the homomorphism
`k ‚Üí k'` is deduced from `A ‚Üí A'` by passage to the quotients. But this follows from `(18.1.1)` applied to √©tale
morphisms, taking into account that `Spec(A)` is the only neighbourhood of its closed point.

Note now that if `ūĚĒ™_őĽ` is the maximal ideal of `A_őĽ`, it follows from `(17.6.1)` that for `őĽ ‚©Ĺ őľ`, one has
`ūĚĒ™_őľ = ūĚĒ™_őĽ ¬∑ A_őľ` and that `A_őľ` is a flat `A_őĽ`-module. Hence it follows from `(0_III, 10.3.1.3)` that the ring
`lim_‚Üí A_őĽ` is local, that the canonical homomorphism `w : A ‚Üí lim_‚Üí A_őĽ` is local, and that one has a canonical
`k`-isomorphism

```text
  lim_‚Üí k(A_őĽ) ‚•≤ k(lim_‚Üí A_őĽ);
```

identifying these two fields, one deduces a canonical `k`-isomorphism

```text
  (18.8.6.2)    ŌČ^{‚ąí1} : ő© ‚•≤ k(lim_‚Üí A_őĽ).
```

**Definition (18.8.7).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.7 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `k = k(A)` its residue field, `i : k ‚Üí ő©` a homomorphism of `k` into a separable closure `ő©`
of `k`; one calls the **strict Henselization** of `A` relative to `i`, and one denotes by `^{hs} A_{(i)}` (or `^{hs} A`
when this leads to no confusion) the inductive limit of the inductive system `(A_őĽ, ŌÜ_{őľőĽ})` defined in `(18.8.6)`.*

As one has a canonical `k`-isomorphism `ŌČ^{‚ąí1} : ő© ‚•≤ k(^{hs} A_{(i)})`, it is canonically endowed with a structure of
`^{hs} A_{(i)}`-algebra by the local homomorphism

```text
  ^{hs} A_{(i)} ‚Üí k(^{hs} A_{(i)}) ‚•≤ ő©.
```

As in `(18.6.5)` one sees that the definition given in `(18.8.7)` depends only in appearance on the choice of the set
`ūĚĒą`; we shall see further below `(18.8.8, (i) and (ii))` that `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is an object representing a functor
entirely defined by the data of `A` and `i` `(0_III, 8.1.8)`, and is therefore defined as such up to unique
isomorphism.

**Proposition (18.8.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.8 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring, `k` its residue field, `i : k ‚Üí ő©` a homomorphism of `k` into a separable closure of `k`,
`^{hs} A_{(i)}` the strict Henselization of `A` corresponding to `i`.*

*(i) `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is a strictly local ring `(18.8.2)` and the structure homomorphism `w : A ‚Üí ^{hs} A_{(i)}` is
local.*

*(ii) For every local homomorphism `u : A ‚Üí B`, where `B` is a strictly local ring, and every `k`-homomorphism
`ŌÜ : ő© ‚Üí k(B)`, there exists one and only one `A`-homomorphism `v : ^{hs} A_{(i)} ‚Üí B` such that `ŌÜ` factors as
`ő© ‚•≤ k(^{hs} A_{(i)}) ‚Üí^{vŐĄ} k(B)`, where `vŐĄ` is deduced from `v` by passage to the quotients.*

<!-- original page 147 -->

*(iii) `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is a faithfully flat `A`-module, and if `ūĚĒ™` is the maximal ideal of `A`, `ūĚĒ™ ¬∑ ^{hs} A_{(i)}` is
the maximal ideal of `^{hs} A_{(i)}`, and `k(^{hs} A_{(i)})` is a separable closure of `k`.*

*(iv) In order for `^{hs} A_{(i)}` to be Noetherian, it is necessary and sufficient that `A` be so.*

*(v) If `A` is strictly local (so `ő© = k`), one has `^{hs} A = A`.*

*(vi) If `i' : k ‚Üí ő©'` is a second homomorphism of `k` into a separable closure of `k`, then for every `k`-isomorphism
`ŌÉ : ő© ‚•≤ ő©'`, the corresponding `A`-homomorphism (by (ii)) `v_ŌÉ : ^{hs} A_{(i)} ‚Üí ^{hs} A_{(i')}` is an isomorphism.*

We have already seen above that `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is a local ring and that `w` is a local homomorphism; the fact that
`^{hs} A_{(i)}` is Henselian (and consequently strictly local) is proved as in `(18.6.6)`. The assertions of (iii)
also follow from `(0_III, 10.3.1.3)`, as does the sufficiency of condition (iv); the necessity of this condition
follows from the fact that `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is a faithfully flat `A`-module `(0_I, 6.5.2)`.

To prove (ii), let us remark, with the notations of `(18.8.6)`, that for every `őĽ ‚ąą L`, there exists one and only one
local `A`-homomorphism `v_őĽ : A_őĽ ‚Üí B`, such that the composite `k(A_őĽ) ‚Üí^{ŌČ_őĽ} ő© ‚Üí^ŌÜ k(B)` is deduced from `v_őĽ` by
passage to the quotients, by virtue of `(18.8.4)` and the hypothesis that `B` is strictly local. The uniqueness of
`v_őĽ` moreover entails that the `v_őĽ` form an inductive system, whence the existence of the homomorphism `v`
satisfying the conditions of (ii); its uniqueness follows from `(17.4.9)`. The uniqueness of `v` entails at once
assertion (vi), by considering the composites `v_ŌÉ ‚ąė v_{ŌÉ^{‚ąí1}}` and `v_{ŌÉ^{‚ąí1}} ‚ąė v_ŌÉ`. Finally, if `A` is strictly
local, and `A' = B_ūĚĒę` an essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra, where `B` is an √©tale `A`-algebra, it follows from
`(18.8.1)` that there exists a `Spec(A)`-section of `Spec(B)` taking the value `ūĚĒę` at the closed point of `Spec(A)`,
hence one deduces from `(17.4.1)` that the homomorphism `A ‚Üí A'` is bijective, which proves (v).

**(18.8.8.1)** Let `ūĚíě` be the category of strictly local rings, with the local homomorphisms as morphisms; for every
`B ‚ąą ūĚíě`, denote by `F(B)` the set of pairs `(u, Ōą)` formed of a local homomorphism `u : A ‚Üí B` and an
`A`-isomorphism `Ōą : ő© ‚•≤ k(B)` such that the composite `k(A) = k ‚Üí^i ő© ‚Üí^Ōą k(B)` is deduced from `u` by passage to
the quotients. One can say that the object `^{hs} A_{(i)}` represents the covariant functor `F : ūĚíě ‚Üí Ens`
`(0_III, 8.1.8)`.

One will note that by virtue of `(18.8.8, (vi))` the strict Henselizations `^{hs} A_{(i)}` are all `A`-isomorphic (for
the various `k`-homomorphisms `i` of `k` into separable closures of `k`), but for given `i` and `i'`, there are in
general infinitely many `A`-isomorphisms `^{hs} A_{(i)} ‚•≤ ^{hs} A_{(i')}`. In precise terms, the group of
`A`-automorphisms of `^{hs} A_{(i)}` is isomorphic to the Galois group of `ő©` over `k`.

**(18.8.9)** Now let `A` be a semi-local ring, `ūĚĒ™_j (1 ‚©Ĺ j ‚©Ĺ n)` its maximal ideals, and for each index `j`, consider a
homomorphism `i_j : k(A_{ūĚĒ™_j}) ‚Üí ő©_j` of `k(A_{ūĚĒ™_j})` into a separable closure of this field. One calls the **strict
Henselization** of `A` relative to the `i_j`, and one denotes by `^{hs} A` (when this gives rise to no confusion), the
product `‚ąŹ_j (^{hs}(A_{ūĚĒ™_j}))` of the strict Henselizations of the local rings `A_{ūĚĒ™_j}` relative to the homomorphisms
`i_j`.

<!-- original page 148 -->

It is therefore, by virtue of `(18.8.8)`, a faithfully flat `A`-module and a semi-local `A`-algebra whose maximal ideals
are the `ūĚĒ™_j ¬∑ ^{hs} A` and the radical `ūĚĒĮ ¬∑ ^{hs} A` (if `ūĚĒĮ` designates the radical of `A`). Finally, the universal
property `(18.8.8, (ii))` subsists on replacing "local homomorphism" by "semi-local homomorphism" `(18.6.7)`, and on
replacing `ő©` by `‚ąŹ_j ő©_j`.

**Proposition (18.8.10).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.10 -->

*Let `A` be a semi-local ring, `B` a finite `A`-algebra. Let `ūĚĒę_i (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ r)` be the maximal ideals of `B`. Then there
is, for each `j`, a maximal ideal `ūĚĒģ_j` of `B ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)` lying over `ūĚĒę_j`, such that `^{hs} B` is isomorphic to
the direct composite of the local rings `(B ‚äó_A (^{hs} A))_{ūĚĒģ_j} (1 ‚©Ĺ j ‚©Ĺ r)`.*

One may evidently restrict to the case where `A` is local, on replacing `A` by `A_{ūĚĒ™_i}`, where `ūĚĒ™_i` is the maximal
ideal of `A` inverse image of `ūĚĒę_i`. Set on the other hand `C = B_{ūĚĒę_i}`; by virtue of the definition of `^{hs} B`
`(18.8.1)`, everything reduces to seeing that `^{hs} C` is `C`-isomorphic to `(C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A))_ūĚĒģ`, where `ūĚĒģ` is one
of the maximal ideals of `C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)`. Let `k` and `K` be the residue fields of `A` and `C`, `ő©` that of
`^{hs} A`, which is by definition a separable closure of `k`; as `K` is a finite extension of `k`, one may suppose `K`
and `ő©` contained in the same algebraic closure of `k`; it is then immediate that `K ‚äó_k ő©` is a direct composite of a
finite number of fields, all isomorphic to the separable closure `K ő©` of `K`. On the other hand, the ring `^{hs} A`
being Henselian, `C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)` is a direct composite of the local rings `(C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A))_{ūĚĒģ_j}`, where `ūĚĒģ_j
(1 ‚©Ĺ j)` ranges over the maximal ideals of `C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)`, and these local rings are Henselian `(18.5.10)` and have
`K ő©` for residue field, hence are strictly local `(18.8.1)`. Set for brevity `C' = C ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)` and
`C'_j = C'_{ūĚĒģ_j}`, which is therefore a quotient ring of `C'`; let `f : C ‚Üí C'`, `g : ^{hs} A ‚Üí C'`, `ŌÜ_j : C' ‚Üí C'_j`
be the canonical maps.

Let `v : C ‚Üí ^{hs} C` be the canonical homomorphism; there exists one and only one local homomorphism
`u : ^{hs} A ‚Üí ^{hs} C` which, by passage to the quotients, gives the canonical injection `ő© ‚Üí K ő©` and such that the
composite `A ‚Üí ^{hs} A ‚Üí^u ^{hs} C` is equal to the composite `A ‚Üí C ‚Üí^v ^{hs} C` `(18.8.8)`; hence there exists a
unique local homomorphism `w_0 : C' ‚Üí ^{hs} C` such that `w_0 ‚ąė g = u`, `w_0 ‚ąė f = v`, and since `^{hs} C` is a local
ring, this homomorphism factors as `C' ‚Üí^{ŌÜ_i} C'_i ‚Üí^w ^{hs} C` for a well-determined index `i`, `w` being a local
homomorphism. On the other hand, the ring `C'_i` being strictly local, there exists a local homomorphism
`w' : ^{hs} C ‚Üí C'_i` which, by passage to the quotients, gives the identity automorphism of `K ő©` and which is such
that `w' ‚ąė v = ŌÜ_i ‚ąė f` `(18.8.8)`. One deduces first from this that `w ‚ąė w'` is an endomorphism of `^{hs} C` which, by
passage to the quotients, gives the identity automorphism of `K ő©`, hence is

<!-- original page 149 -->

the identity `(18.8.8)`. On the other hand, one has `w' ‚ąė w ‚ąė ŌÜ_i ‚ąė f = w' ‚ąė v = ŌÜ_i ‚ąė f`, and
`w' ‚ąė w ‚ąė ŌÜ_i ‚ąė g = w' ‚ąė u = ŌÜ_i ‚ąė g`, whence `w' ‚ąė w ‚ąė ŌÜ_i = ŌÜ_i`, in other words `w' ‚ąė w` is the identity
automorphism of `C'_i`. Q.E.D.

**Remark (18.8.11).** ‚ÄĒ The proof of `(18.8.10)` uses the fact that `B` is integral over `A`, but uses the fact that
`B` is a finite `A`-algebra only to establish that `K ‚äó_k ő©` is a direct composite of a finite number of fields. Now,
this last property is still satisfied when one supposes that `B` is a semi-local integral `A`-algebra whose maximal
ideals `ūĚĒę_i` have residue fields of finite separable degrees `[k(ūĚĒę_i) : k]_s`.

**Proposition (18.8.12).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.12 -->

*Let `A` be a semi-local ring.*

*(i) In order for `^{hs} A` to be reduced (resp. normal), it is necessary and sufficient that `A` be so.*

*(ii) Suppose `A` Noetherian. Then, for every prime ideal `ūĚĒ≠` of `A`, the ring `(^{hs} A)_ūĚĒ≠ / ūĚĒ≠ ¬∑ (^{hs} A)_ūĚĒ≠` is a
direct composite of a finite number of separable algebraic extensions of `k(ūĚĒ≠)` (which implies that the fibres of the
canonical morphism `Spec(^{hs} A) ‚Üí Spec(A)` are geometrically regular and are discrete spaces).*

**Corollary (18.8.13).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.13 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. In order for `^{hs} A` to possess one of the following properties:*

*a) being a Cohen-Macaulay ring `(0, 16.5.3)`;*

*b) satisfying condition `(S_n)` `(5.7.3)`;*

*c) being regular;*

*d) satisfying condition `(R_n)` `(5.8.2)`;*

*it is necessary and sufficient that `A` possess this same property.*

**Corollary (18.8.14).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.14 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian semi-local ring. In order for a prime ideal `ūĚĒ≠` of `^{hs} A` to belong to `Ass(^{hs} A)`, it
is necessary and sufficient that `ūĚĒ≠ ‚ą© A` belong to `Ass(A)`.*

The proofs are the same as those of `(18.6.9)`, `(18.6.10)` and `(18.6.11)` respectively.

**Proposition (18.8.15).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.15 -->

*Let `A` be a local ring; the following properties are equivalent:*

*a) `A` is geometrically unibranch (resp. reduced and geometrically unibranch).*

*b) For every essentially √©tale local `A`-algebra `A'`, `Spec(A')` is irreducible (resp. integral).*

*c) `Spec(^{hs} A)` is irreducible (resp. integral).*

One reduces as in `(18.6.12)` to the case where `A` is reduced, using the relation
`^{hs}(A_{red}) = (^{hs} A) ‚äó_A A_{red}` `(18.8.11)`, and the equivalence of b) and c) is proved as in `(18.6.12)`; the
same holds for the fact that a) implies c), taking into account `(18.8.11)` and the definition of a geometrically
unibranch integral local ring. Finally, to prove that c) implies a), let us keep the same notations as in
`(18.6.12)`; it is still a question of seeing that `^{hs} B` is integral. But, by `(18.8.10)`, `^{hs} B` is a
localized ring of the semi-local ring `B ‚äó_A (^{hs} A)`, which by flatness still identifies with a subring of the
field `L`, hence is integral; the same holds therefore for `^{hs} B`, which completes the proof.

**Corollary (18.8.16).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.16 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian local ring (resp. strictly local). In order for `A` to be unibranch (resp. geometrically
unibranch), it is necessary and sufficient that `Spec(A)` be irreducible.*

<!-- original page 150 -->

**Proposition (18.8.17).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.17 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring. If `A` is universally catenary, the same holds for `^{hs} A`.*

The proof is the same as that of `(18.7.5)`.

**Proposition (18.8.18).**

<!-- label: IV.18.8.18 -->

*(i) Every filtered inductive limit of strictly local rings (where the transition homomorphisms are local) is a strictly
local ring.*

*(ii) The functor `A ‚Ü¶ ^{hs} A` on the category of local rings (where the morphisms are the local homomorphisms)
commutes with filtered inductive limits.*

*(iii) Every strictly local ring `A` is the inductive limit of a filtered inductive system of Noetherian strictly local
rings (the transition homomorphisms being local).*

The proofs are modelled on those of `(18.6.14)`.

### 18.9. Formal fibres of Henselian Noetherian rings

**Theorem (18.9.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.1 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian Noetherian local ring whose formal fibres are geometrically normal. Then the formal fibres of
`A` are geometrically integral (hence geometrically connected).*

The two assertions of the statement are in fact equivalent, a locally Noetherian connected and normal prescheme being
integral. As every finite integral `A`-algebra is a Henselian local ring `(18.5.9 and 18.5.10)`, it follows from
`(7.3.16.2)` that one is reduced to proving that if one supposes moreover `A` integral, then the fibre of the morphism
`Spec(√ā) ‚Üí Spec(A)` at the generic point of `Spec(A)` is integral, which follows from the

**Corollary (18.9.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.2 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian Noetherian local ring that is integral, whose formal fibres are geometrically normal. Then `√ā`
is integral.*

In effect, it follows from `(18.8.16)` that `A` is unibranch, so the theorem follows from the hypothesis made on the
formal fibres of `A` and from `(7.6.3)`.

**Corollary (18.9.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.3 -->

*Under the hypotheses of `(18.9.2)`, the field of fractions `L` of `√ā` is a separable extension of the field of
fractions `K` of `A`, and `K` is algebraically closed in `L`.*

This follows from the fact that the fibre of the morphism `Spec(√ā) ‚Üí Spec(A)` at the generic point of `Spec(A)` is
geometrically integral and from `(4.3.2)` and `(4.3.5)`.

**Corollary (18.9.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.4 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian Noetherian local ring whose formal fibres are geometrically normal (for example a Henselian
Noetherian local ring that is excellent), and set `A' = √ā`. Let `X` be an `A`-prescheme, `X' = X ‚äó_A A'`,
`g : X' ‚Üí X` the canonical projection. Then the map `U ‚Ü¶ g^{‚ąí1}(U)` is a bijection of the set of subsets that are at
once open and closed in `X` onto the set of subsets that are at once open and closed in `X'`.*

As `g` is a faithfully flat and quasi-compact morphism, one knows `(2.3.12)` that the topology of `X` is the quotient
of that of `X'` by the equivalence relation defined by `g`. Everything reduces therefore to seeing that an open and
closed subset `U'` of `X'` is saturated for this relation. Now, as the morphism `Spec(A') ‚Üí Spec(A)` has its fibres
geometrically connected `(18.9.1)`, the fibres of `g` are connected, whence at once our assertion.

In particular, if `X` is locally connected (which will be the case if `X` is locally Noetherian), then, for every
connected component `U` of `X` (which is open and closed

<!-- original page 151 -->

in `X`), `g^{‚ąí1}(U)` is connected `(Bourbaki, Top. g√©n., chap. I, 3rd ed., ¬ß 11, n¬į 3, prop. 7)`. If one denotes by
`ŌÄ_0(X)` the set of connected components of `X`, the map `ŌÄ_0(X') ‚Üí ŌÄ_0(X)` canonically deduced from `g` is therefore
bijective.

**Corollary (18.9.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.5 -->

*Under the hypotheses of `(18.9.4)`, the functor*

```text
  (18.9.5.1)    Z ‚Ü¶ Z ‚äó_A A'
```

*from the category of √©tale preschemes over `X` to the category of √©tale preschemes over `X'` is fully faithful.*

Let `Z_1`, `Z_2` be two √©tale preschemes over `X`, and set `Z'_i = Z_i ‚äó_A A' (i = 1, 2)`; it is a question of proving
that every `X'`-morphism `Z'_1 ‚Üí Z'_2` arises, by base change, from a unique `X`-morphism `Z_1 ‚Üí Z_2`. Suppose first
that `Z_2` is separated over `X`; then, since `Hom_X(Z_1, Z_2)` identifies with `őď(Z_1 √ó_X Z_2 / Z_1)`, and
`Z_1 √ó_X Z_2` is √©tale and separated over `Z_1`, `Hom_X(Z_1, Z_2)` identifies functorially, by virtue of `(17.9.3)`,
with the set of open and closed subsets `U` of `Z_1 √ó_X Z_2` such that the restriction to `U` of the projection
`p : Z_1 √ó_X Z_2 ‚Üí Z_1` is a surjective and radicial morphism. The assertion follows therefore in the case considered
from `(18.9.4)` and from the fact that `Z'_1 √ó_{X'} Z'_2 = (Z_1 √ó_X Z_2) ‚äó_A A'`.

Let us pass to the general case: it will suffice to prove that the graph `őď'` of an `X'`-morphism `Z'_1 ‚Üí Z'_2` which
is open `(17.9.3)` in `Z'_1 √ó_{X'} Z'_2` is of the form `őď ‚äó_A A'`, where `őď` is induced on an open subset of
`Z = Z_1 √ó_X Z_2`. In effect, the restriction to `őď` of the projection `p : Z ‚Üí Z_1` is then an isomorphism, for by
base change `A ‚Üí A'` this restriction becomes the isomorphism restriction to `őď'` of the projection
`p' : Z' ‚Üí Z'_1` (with `Z' = Z ‚äó_A A'`), and it suffices to apply `(2.7.1, (viii))`; the conclusion then follows from
the characterization of graphs of morphisms `(I, 5.3)`.

If `q : Z' ‚Üí Z` is the canonical projection, to prove that `őď'` is of the form `q^{‚ąí1}(őď)`, where `őď` is open in `Z`,
it suffices, since `q` is a faithfully flat and quasi-compact morphism, to prove that there exists a set `U ‚äā Z` such
that `őď' = q^{‚ąí1}(U)` `(2.3.12)`. Set `Z'' = Z' √ó_Z Z' = Z √ó_X X''`, where `X'' = X' √ó_X X'`, and let
`r_1 : Z'' ‚Üí Z'`, `r_2 : Z'' ‚Üí Z'` be the canonical projections. Applying `(4.5.19.1)`, it suffices to show that
`r_1^{‚ąí1}(őď') = r_2^{‚ąí1}(őď')`. But this is a property which is true if and only if it is so after each base change
`Spec(k(x)) ‚Üí X`, where `x` ranges over `X`. In other words, it suffices to prove the corollary when `X` is the
spectrum of a field, but since every √©tale `X`-prescheme is then automatically separated over `X` `(17.6.2, c')`, one
is reduced to the case considered at the beginning of the proof.

**Remarks (18.9.6).** ‚ÄĒ (i) It is possible that, if the residue field of `A` is of characteristic `0`, the functor
`(18.9.5.1)` even induces an equivalence of the category of √©tale covers of `X` and of the category of √©tale covers of
`X'`. One can in any case prove this when `A` is excellent, using Hironaka's resolution of singularities (M. Artin). It
is plausible that the analogous statement is still true without restriction on the characteristic of the residue field,
provided one restricts to "principal Galois covers" whose group is of order prime to the residue characteristic
(cf. `[41]`).

<!-- original page 152 -->

(ii) One does not know whether the formal fibres of `A` are geometrically connected (let alone geometrically
irreducible) when `A` is an arbitrary Henselian local ring. It would suffice that, for every Noetherian Henselian
integral local ring `A`, `Spec(√ā)` be irreducible. One does not know whether this is always so.

(iii) One does not know whether, when `A` is an excellent local ring, its strict Henselization `^{hs} A` is excellent.
One can see that, to clarify this question, one may reduce to the case where `A = k[[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]]`, `k` being a field
of characteristic `p > 0`. Taking into account `(18.8.17)`, the question is whether the formal fibres of `^{hs} A` are
geometrically regular. The answer is affirmative when `n = 1`, but is not known for `n = 2`. One can show that the
answer is affirmative whenever, for every scheme `Y_1` finite over `Y = Spec(A)`, one can resolve the singularities of
`Y_1` `(7.9.1)`.

The connectedness assertion made in `(18.9.1)` generalizes in the following way:

**Theorem (18.9.7).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.7 -->

*Let `A`, `B` be two Noetherian local rings, `ŌĀ : A ‚Üí B` a local homomorphism, `f : Spec(B) = X ‚Üí Spec(A) = Y` the
corresponding morphism. Suppose the following conditions verified:*

*(i) `f` is flat and all the fibres `X_y = f^{‚ąí1}(y) (y ‚ąą Y)` are geometrically reduced `(4.6.2)`.*

*(ii) Either the ring `A` is geometrically unibranch `(0, 23.2.1)`, or the ring `A` is unibranch `(0, 23.2.1)` and
`k(B)` is a primary extension `(4.3.1)` of `k(A)`.*

*Then, if `ő∑` is the generic point of `Y`, `X_ő∑` is connected, and for every closed subset `T` of `X` such that `f(T)`
is rare in `Y`, `X ‚ąí T` is connected.*

The two conclusions stated are in fact equivalent; indeed, the hypothesis that `f(T)` is rare entails that `f(T)` does
not contain `ő∑`; on the other hand, since `{ő∑}` is pro-constructible `(1.9.6)` and `f` is flat, `X_ő∑` is dense in `X`
`(2.3.10)`, and consequently if `X_ő∑` is connected, the same holds for `X ‚ąí T` which contains it. Conversely, when `U`
ranges over the set of affine open neighbourhoods of `ő∑` in `Y`, `X_ő∑` is the projective limit of the sub-preschemes
induced on the open sets `f^{‚ąí1}(U)` of `X` `(8.1.2, a)`; if one supposes that `X ‚ąí T` is connected for every closed
subset `T` of `X` such that `f(T)` is rare in `Y`, the `f^{‚ąí1}(U)` are connected, hence the same holds for `X_ő∑`
`(8.4.1)`.

Let us first show that one may restrict to the case where `A` is integral and `Y = Spec(A)` geometrically unibranch
`(6.15.1)` (which implies that `A` is geometrically unibranch, but is not equivalent to this condition `(6.15.2)`). It
is clear that one may first suppose `Y` reduced, by considering the morphism `X √ó_Y Y_{red} ‚Üí Y_{red}`, deduced from
`f` by base change, which is flat, and has the same fibres as `f`. One may therefore suppose `A` integral and
unibranch; if `K` is the field of fractions of `A`, there then exists a finite `A`-subalgebra `A''` of `K` such that,
if `A'` is the integral closure of `A`, the morphism `Spec(A') ‚Üí Spec(A'')` is radicial `(0, 23.2.5)`; one concludes
`(6.15.5)` that `Spec(A'')` is geometrically unibranch. As `A` is unibranch, `A'` is a local ring, hence the same holds
for `A''`; let us show that the ring `B'' = B ‚äó_A A''` is also a local ring. In effect, `B''` is a finite `B`-algebra,
hence a Noetherian semi-local ring;

<!-- original page 153 -->

if `k''` is the residue field of `A''`, the maximal ideals of `B''` are the points of `Spec(B'')` lying over the closed
point of `Spec(B)`, hence the points of `Spec(k(B) ‚äó_{k(A)} k'')` `(I, 3.4.9)`, since the closed point of `Spec(A'')`
is the only point lying over the closed point of `Spec(A)`. Now, when `A` is geometrically unibranch, `k''` is a
radicial extension of `k(A)`, hence `Spec(k(B) ‚äó_{k(A)} k'')` is radicial over `Spec(k(B))` and therefore comprises a
single point. When `A` is unibranch and `k(B)` is a primary extension of `k(A)`, `Spec(k(B) ‚äó_{k(A)} k'')` is
irreducible `(4.3.2)` and since it is a finite and discrete space `(I, 6.4.4)` it is again reduced to a point, which
shows that in both cases envisaged `B''` is local. If `Y'' = Spec(A'')`, `X'' = Spec(B'') = X √ó_Y Y''`, the morphism
`f'' = f_{(Y'')} : X'' ‚Üí Y''` is flat and has its fibres geometrically reduced; moreover, if `ő∑''` is the generic point
of `Y''`, `k(ő∑'') = k(ő∑) = K` by definition, hence the fibres `X_ő∑` and `X''_{ő∑''}` are isomorphic. It suffices
therefore to prove that `X''_{ő∑''}` is connected.

**Remark (18.9.7.1).** ‚ÄĒ When the ring `A` is Japanese, one may, in what precedes, take `A'' = A'` by definition
`(0, 23.1.1)`; one sees therefore that in this case one would even be reduced to proving the theorem when `A` is
integrally closed.

**(18.9.7.2)** Let us henceforth suppose that `Y` is integral and geometrically unibranch. Note that if `T` is a closed
subset of `X` such that `f(T)` is rare in `Y`, `T` contains no maximal point of `X` since `f` is flat `(2.3.4)`, hence
is rare in `X`; as `X` is a local scheme, hence connected, to prove that `X ‚ąí T` is connected, it suffices, by virtue
of `(15.5.6.1)`, to show that for every `x ‚ąą X` such that `f(x) ‚Č† ő∑`, `Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚ąí {x}` is connected. Set
`y = f(x)`, `A_1 = ūĚí™_{Y, y}`, `B_1 = ūĚí™_{X, x}`, `Y_1 = Spec(A_1)`, `X_1 = Spec(B_1)`, `f_1 : X_1 ‚Üí Y_1` the morphism
corresponding to the local homomorphism `A_1 ‚Üí B_1` deduced from `ŌĀ`; it is clear that `f_1` is flat, and it follows
from `(4.6.1)` that its fibres are geometrically reduced; moreover `A_1` is integral and geometrically unibranch
`(6.15.1)` and `dim(A_1) ‚©ĺ 1`. One is thus reduced to proving the

**Lemma (18.9.7.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.7.3 -->

*Let `A`, `B` be two Noetherian local rings, `Y = Spec(A)`, `X = Spec(B)`, `ŌĀ : A ‚Üí B` a local homomorphism,
`f : X ‚Üí Y` the corresponding morphism. Suppose the following conditions verified:*

*(i) `f` is flat and all the fibres `X_y (y ‚ąą Y)` are geometrically reduced.*

*(ii) `A` is integral, geometrically unibranch and `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 1`.*

*Then, if `b` is the closed point of `X`, `X ‚ąí {b}` is connected.*

Let us first note that by virtue of Hartshorne's theorem `(5.10.7)`, `X ‚ąí {b}` is connected if one has `prof(B) ‚©ĺ 2`.
Now one has `(6.3.1)`

```text
  (18.9.7.4)    prof(B) = prof(A) + prof(B ‚äó_A k)
```

on denoting by `k` the residue field of `A`. On the other hand, since `A` is integral and `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 1`, one has
`prof(A) ‚©ĺ 1`, hence `prof(B) ‚©ĺ 2` except when `prof(B ‚äó_A k) = 0`; moreover, `B ‚äó_A k` is reduced by (i), so the
relation `prof(B ‚äó_A k) = 0` signifies that `B ‚äó_A k` is a field `(0, 16.4.7)`. We shall henceforth suppose that this
last condition is verified. We shall begin by treating a case where the proof is very simple.

**A) Case where `A` is integrally closed.** Then, if `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 2`, one has `prof(A) ‚©ĺ 2` `(0, 16.5.1)`, hence
`prof(B) ‚©ĺ 2`. One therefore has only to consider the case where `dim(A) = 1` and where `B ‚äó_A k`

<!-- original page 154 -->

is a field; `A` is then a discrete valuation ring, hence regular, and since `B ‚äó_A k` is a field and `f` is flat, `B`
is regular `(0, 17.3.3)`; but moreover `(6.1.1.1)`, one has `dim(B) = dim(A) + dim(B ‚äó_A k) = dim(A) = 1`, hence `B` is
also a discrete valuation ring. But then `X ‚ąí {b}` is reduced to a single point, whence lemma `(18.9.7.3)` in this
case.

One observes that this proves the statement of theorem `(18.9.7)` when one supposes moreover that, in this statement,
the ring `A` is Japanese, for by virtue of remark `(18.9.7.1)`, one is reduced to the case where `A` is integrally
closed, and then, in the reduction preceding `(18.9.7.3)`, the ring `A_1 = ūĚí™_{Y, y}` is also integrally closed and one
may apply the result that has just been proved.

**B) Case where `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 2`.** The result of `(18.9.7.3)` will then follow from the two following lemmas:

**Lemma (18.9.7.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.7.5 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian semi-local reduced ring, `A'` its integral closure in its total ring of fractions,
`X = Spec(A)`, `X' = Spec(A')`; let `S` be the set of closed points of `X` and `U = X ‚ąí S`. Suppose that for every
