point `x' ‚ąą X'` lying over a point of `S`, one has `dim(ūĚí™_{X', x'}) ‚©ĺ 2`; then the ring `A^{(1)} = őď(U, ūĚí™_X)` is
integral over `A` and is an `A`-algebra isomorphic to a sub-algebra of `A'`.*

Recall that if `ūĚĒ≠_i (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ m)` are the minimal ideals of `A`, `A'` is the direct composite of the integral closures
`A'_i` of the integral rings `A_i = A / ūĚĒ≠_i`, so that `X'` is the sum of the schemes `X'_i = Spec(A'_i)`. If `X_0` is
the sum of the schemes `X_i = Spec(A_i) (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ m)` and `U_0` the inverse image of `U` in `X_0`, the canonical
homomorphism `őď(U, ūĚí™_X) ‚Üí őď(U_0, ūĚí™_{X_0})` is injective since `X` is reduced and the morphism `X_0 ‚Üí X` (hence also
the morphism `U_0 ‚Üí U`) surjective. As `őď(U_0, ūĚí™_{X_0})` is a direct composite of the `őď(U_i, ūĚí™_{X_i}) (1 ‚©Ĺ i ‚©Ĺ m)`,
where `U_i` is the inverse image of `U` in `X_i`, everything reduces to proving that for each `i`, `őď(U_i, ūĚí™_{X_i})`
is isomorphic to a sub-`A_i`-algebra of `A'_i`. In other words, one is reduced to the case where `A` is a Noetherian
semi-local integral ring. As `X` is reduced and the morphism `X' ‚Üí X` is surjective, the homomorphism
`őď(U, ūĚí™_X) ‚Üí őď(U', ūĚí™_{X'})` (where `U'` is the inverse image of `U` in `X'`) is injective, and everything reduces to
seeing that the canonical homomorphism `A' = őď(X', ūĚí™_{X'}) ‚Üí őď(U', ūĚí™_{X'})` is bijective. Now `(I, 8.2.1.1)`,
`őď(U', ūĚí™_{X'})` is the intersection of the local rings `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}`, where `ūĚĒ≠'` ranges over `U'`, and by hypothesis,
among these local rings figure all those for which `ūĚĒ≠'` is of height `1`. But the reasoning of `(0, 23.2.7)` also
applies to a Noetherian semi-local integral ring, so `A'` is a semi-local Krull ring, and is therefore the
intersection of the local rings `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}`, where `ūĚĒ≠'` ranges over the set of prime ideals of height `1` of `A'`
`(Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. VII, ¬ß 1, n¬į 6, th. 4)`; a fortiori `A'` is the intersection of the `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}` for
`ūĚĒ≠' ‚ąą U'`, which completes the proof of the lemma.

**Remark (18.9.7.6).** ‚ÄĒ One knows `(0, 23.2.5)` that there exists a finite `A`-subalgebra `A''` of the field of
fractions `K` of `A` such that the morphism `Spec(A') ‚Üí Spec(A'')` is radicial; as this morphism is also integral and
dominant, hence surjective `(II, 6.1.10)`, it is a homeomorphism `(2.4.5)`. By virtue of the geometric interpretation
of `dim(ūĚí™_{X, x})` `(5.1.2)`, one has therefore, for every point `x''` of `X'' = Spec(A'')` lying over a point `x ‚ąą X`,
`dim(ūĚí™_{X'', x''}) = dim(ūĚí™_{X', x'}) ‚©ĺ 2` if `x'` is the unique point of `X'` lying over `x`. This

<!-- original page 155 -->

being so, it follows first from `(0, 16.1.5)` and `(0, 16.1.4.1)` that the relation `dim(ūĚí™_{X'', x''}) ‚©ĺ 2` entails
`dim(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚©ĺ 2`. Conversely, suppose that `dim(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚©ĺ 2` and that `A` is universally catenary `(5.6.2)`; then
the same holds for `ūĚí™_{X, x}` `(5.6.3)`, hence `dim(ūĚí™_{X'', x''}) = dim(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚©ĺ 2` by virtue of `(5.6.10)` and one
has therefore in this case `dim(ūĚí™_{X', x'}) ‚©ĺ 2`. The same conclusion holds if `ūĚí™_{X, x}` is geometrically unibranch,
for the fibre of the morphism `X' ‚Üí X` at the point `x` is then reduced to a single point, and `ūĚí™_{X', x'}` is an
integral `ūĚí™_{X, x}`-algebra; it then suffices to apply `(0, 16.1.5)`.

**Lemma (18.9.7.7).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.7.7 -->

*Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring of dimension `‚©ĺ 2`, integral and geometrically unibranch, `Y = Spec(A)`, `y` the
closed point of `Y`. Let `X` be a locally Noetherian connected prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a flat morphism. Then, for every
closed subset `T ‚äā f^{‚ąí1}(y)`, `X ‚ąí T` is connected.*

The same reasoning as at the beginning of `(18.9.7.2)` reduces to proving that, for every point `x ‚ąą f^{‚ąí1}(y)`,
`Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚ąí {x}` is connected. One may therefore restrict to the case where `X = Spec(B)`, `B` being a
Noetherian local ring and the homomorphism `A ‚Üí B` corresponding to `f` being local; set `U = Y ‚ąí {y}`, and note that
`V = f^{‚ąí1}(U)` is dense in `X` `(2.3.10)`; it suffices therefore to prove that `V` is connected. Let us keep the
notations of `(18.9.7.5)`, and set `A^{(1)} = őď(U, ūĚí™_Y)`, `Y_1 = Spec(A^{(1)})`, `X_1 = X √ó_Y Y_1 = Spec(B ‚äó_A
A^{(1)})`, so that one has the commutative diagram

```text
  X ‚Üź‚ÄĒ‚ÄĒ X_1
  ‚Üď f    ‚Üď f_1
  Y ‚Üź‚ÄĒ‚ÄĒ Y_1
```

By virtue of `(2.3.1)` and the definition of `A^{(1)}`, one has a canonical isomorphism
`őď(V, ūĚí™_X) ‚•≤ B ‚äó_A A^{(1)}`, and it suffices therefore to prove that this last ring is local, such a ring not being
able to be the product of two rings not reduced to `0` `(III, 7.8.6.1)`. But one has seen `(18.9.7.5)` that `A^{(1)}`
is a sub-`A`-algebra of the integral closure `A'` of `A`, since `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 2`. The hypothesis that `A` is
geometrically unibranch entails that the morphism `Spec(A') ‚Üí Spec(A)` is radicial at the point `y` `(6.15.3)`;
a fortiori `g : Y_1 ‚Üí Y` is radicial at the point `y`, and consequently `g_1` is radicial at the closed point `x` of
`X`; since moreover `g_1` is an integral morphism, the closed points of `X_1` lie over `x`, so there exists only a
single closed point of `X_1`, which completes the proof of `(18.9.7.7)`.

It is clear that `(18.9.7.7)` proves `(18.9.7.3)` when `dim(A) ‚©ĺ 2` (even without supposing the fibres `X_y`
geometrically reduced).

**C) Case where `dim(A) = 1`.** One has seen at the beginning of the proof of `(18.9.7.3)` that one may suppose
`dim(B ‚äó_A k) = 0`, and by flatness `(6.1.1.1)`, one has therefore

```text
  dim(B) = dim(A) + dim(B ‚äó_A k) = 1.
```

Since `A` is integral, `Y` consists of two points, the generic point `ő∑` and the closed point `a`. Moreover, since `f`
is flat, every irreducible component of `X` dominates `Y` `(2.3.4)`, hence `X ‚ąí {b}`, which is the set of maximal
points of `X`, is equal to

<!-- original page 156 -->

the underlying set of `f^{‚ąí1}(ő∑)`, and the fibre `f^{‚ąí1}(ő∑)` is the sum of the `Spec(k(őĺ_i))`. The hypothesis on the
`X_y` and the fact that these fibres are of dimension `0` show that they are geometrically regular `(6.7.6)`, and
a fortiori geometrically normal, in other words `f` is a normal morphism. But since `A` is integral and geometrically
unibranch, it follows from `(6.15.10)` that `B` is integral and geometrically unibranch, hence `f^{‚ąí1}(ő∑) = X ‚ąí {b}`
is reduced to a single point, and a fortiori connected; this therefore completes the proof of `(18.9.7.3)` and that
of `(18.9.7)`.

**Remark (18.9.7.8).** ‚ÄĒ In case C) of the proof of `(18.9.7.3)`, one can avoid appealing to the delicate result
`(6.15.10)` by reasoning as follows: since `A` is integral and of dimension `1`, it follows from the Krull-Akizuki
theorem `(Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. VII, ¬ß 2, n¬į 5, prop. 5)` that its integral closure `A'` is a Noetherian ring.
The same reasoning as at the beginning of the proof of `(18.9.7)` then shows that `B' = B ‚äó_A A'` is a local ring;
moreover, by flatness, `B'` is contained in the total ring of fractions `R` of the reduced ring `B` `(3.3.5)`. Now,
the reasoning that proves the Krull-Akizuki theorem `(Bourbaki, loc. cit.)` applies equally to a Noetherian local
reduced ring of dimension `1`, and shows that for such a ring `B`, every ring comprised between `B` and its total
ring of fractions is Noetherian. The ring `B'` being a Noetherian local ring and the morphism `Spec(B') ‚Üí Spec(A')`
being normal, `B'` is an integral and integrally closed ring `(6.5.4)`, and one concludes as at the beginning of the
proof of `(18.9.7)`.

**Corollary (18.9.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.8 -->

*With the notations of `(18.9.7)`, suppose condition (i) of `(18.9.7)` verified and one of the two following
conditions:*

*(ii') `A` is a strictly local ring `(18.8.2)`.*

*(ii'') `A` is a Henselian local ring and `k(B)` is a primary extension of `k(A)`.*

*Then all the fibres `X_y (y ‚ąą Y)` are geometrically connected.*

Let `ūĚĒ≠` be the prime ideal of `A` corresponding to the point `y`, and set `A' = A / ūĚĒ≠` and `B' = B ‚äó_A A' = B / ūĚĒ≠ B`,
which are evidently Noetherian local rings; it is clear that the morphism `f' : Spec(B') ‚Üí Spec(A')` satisfies
condition (i) of `(18.9.7)`; since `y` is the generic point of `Spec(A')` and `k(A') = k(A)`, `k(B') = k(B)`, one sees
that it suffices to verify that, in case (ii') (resp. (ii'')), `A'` is geometrically unibranch (resp. unibranch). But
`A'` is integral, and in case (ii') (resp. (ii'')) it is strictly local (resp. Henselian) by virtue of `(18.5.10)`.
One concludes that `A'` is geometrically unibranch (resp. unibranch) by virtue of `(18.8.15)` (resp. `(18.6.12)`).

**Corollary (18.9.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.9 -->

*Let `A` be a Henselian Noetherian local ring, universally Japanese (i.e. (`(7.6.4)` and `(7.7.2)`), whose formal fibres
`(7.3.13)` are geometrically reduced); then the formal fibres of `A` are geometrically connected.*

It suffices to apply `(18.9.8)` to the case where `B = √ā`, since `B` is a flat `A`-module and `k(√ā) = k(A)`.

**Remark (18.9.10).** ‚ÄĒ The proofs of `(18.9.4)` and `(18.9.5)` use only the fact that the formal fibres of `A` are
geometrically connected. The conclusions

<!-- original page 157 -->

of these two propositions are therefore still valid when one supposes the local ring `A` Noetherian, Henselian and
universally Japanese.

**Corollary (18.9.11).**

<!-- label: IV.18.9.11 -->

*Let `X`, `Y` be two locally Noetherian preschemes, `Y` being supposed geometrically unibranch and `X` connected. Let
`f : X ‚Üí Y` be a reduced morphism (i.e. `(6.8.1)` flat and with geometrically reduced fibres). Then, for every closed
subset `T` of `X` such that `f(T)` is rare in `Y`, `X ‚ąí T` is connected.*

As `f` is flat and `f(T)`, being rare, cannot contain any maximal point of `Y`, `T` cannot contain any maximal point
of `X` `(2.3.4)`, hence is rare in `X`. Using `(15.5.6.1)`, it suffices to show that, for every `x ‚ąą T`,
`Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚ąí {x}` is connected. Set `y = f(x)`, which is not a maximal point of `Y`. As the ring `ūĚí™_{Y, y}` is
geometrically unibranch, one may apply `(18.9.7)` to the morphism

```text
  f_1 : Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x}) ‚Üí Spec(ūĚí™_{Y, y}),
```

which satisfies conditions (i) and (ii) of this theorem, and to the closed subset `{x}` of `Spec(ūĚí™_{X, x})`, since
`f_1({x}) = {y}` is rare in `Spec(ūĚí™_{Y, y})`.

<!-- original page 157 -->

### 18.10. √Čtale preschemes over a geometrically unibranch or normal prescheme

**Theorem (18.10.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.1 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite presentation, `x` a point of `X`, `y = f(x)`. Suppose that `Y` is
integral and geometrically unibranch at the point `y`. Then, in order that `f` be √©tale at the point `x`, it is necessary
and sufficient that the following two conditions be satisfied:*

*(i) `f` is unramified at the point `x`;*

*(ii) the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,y} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is injective.*

*Moreover, if this is so, `X` is integral and geometrically unibranch at the point `x`.*

*Finally, if `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is Noetherian, one can, in the preceding criterion, replace condition (ii) by the condition*

*(ii bis) one has `dim(ūĚí™_{Y,y}) = dim(ūĚí™_{X,x})`.*

The fact that, if `f` is √©tale, `X` is integral and geometrically unibranch at the point `x` is a particular case of
`(17.5.7)`. It is clear, on the other hand, that conditions (i) and (ii) are necessary for `f` to be √©tale at the point
`x`, since `ūĚí™_{X,x}` is then a faithfully flat `ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-module `(17.6.1)`. Let us prove that these conditions are
sufficient.

Set `A = ūĚí™_{Y,y}`, `A' = ^{sh}A` `(18.8.7)`, `Y' = Spec(A')`, `X' = X √ó_Y Y'`, `f' = f_{(Y')} : X' ‚Üí Y'`. Let `y'` be the
closed point of `Y'`, and `x'` a point of `X'` lying above `x` and above `y'`; since the morphism `g : Y' ‚Üí Y` is flat
`(18.8.8)`, everything reduces to showing that `f'` is √©tale at the point `x'` `(17.7.1, (ii))`. Hypothesis (ii) implies
that the morphism `Spec(ūĚí™_{X,x}) ‚Üí Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` is dominant `(I, 1.2.7)`; since `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is integral, `Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`
has a unique generic point `y_1`, and there is therefore a generization `x_1` of `x` in `X` above `y_1`. The projection
morphism `X' ‚Üí X` being flat, there exists a generization `x'_1` of `x'` above `x_1` `(2.3.4)`; set `y'_1 = f'(x'_1)`;
one has consequently `g(y'_1) = y_1`. Now, the hypothesis on `Y` implies that `A'` is integral `(18.8.15)`, so `Y'` has
a unique generic point `ő∑`, necessarily above `y_1` since `g`

<!-- original page 158 -->

is flat `(2.3.4)`; on the other hand, `ő∑` is also a generic point of `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y_1)` `(0_I, 2.1.8)`, and one knows moreover
that the fibres of `g` are discrete spaces `(18.8.12)`, so `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y_1) = {ő∑}`, and consequently `y'_1 = ő∑`. The morphism
`Spec(ūĚí™_{X',x'}) ‚Üí Spec(ūĚí™_{Y',y'})` restriction of `f'` is therefore dominant, and since `ūĚí™_{Y',y'} = A'` is integral,
the corresponding homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y',y'} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X',x'}` is injective `(I, 1.2.7)`. On the other hand, `f'` is unramified
at the point `x'` `(17.3.3)`, so `(18.8.3, d))` there is a neighbourhood `U` of `x'` in `X'` such that `f' | U` is a
closed immersion of `U` into `Y'`; a fortiori the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y',y'} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X',x'}` is surjective, and since it is
injective, it is bijective. But then `ūĚí™_{X',x'}` is a flat `ūĚí™_{Y',y'}`-module, and `f'` is therefore √©tale at the point
`x'` `(17.6.1)`.

Finally, when `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is Noetherian, one knows that if `f` is √©tale at the point `x`, one has
`dim(ūĚí™_{Y,y}) = dim(ūĚí™_{X,x})` `(17.10.4)`. Conversely, suppose conditions (i) and (ii bis) are verified, and let us show
that they imply (ii). Let `ūĚĒć` be the kernel of the canonical homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,y} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}`; restricting `Y` if
necessary to a neighbourhood of `y`, one can suppose that `ūĚĒć = ūĚďô_y`, where `ūĚďô` is an Ideal of `ūĚí™_Y`; if `Y_1` is the
closed sub-prescheme of `Y` defined by `ūĚďô`, one can, by restricting again if necessary `X` and `Y` to neighbourhoods of
`x` and `y` respectively, suppose that `f` factors as `X ‚Üí^{f_1} Y_1 ‚Üí Y` `(I, 6.5.1)`. It is clear that `f_1` is still
unramified at the point `x`, so `(17.4.1)` quasi-finite at that point; the proof of `(5.4.1, (i))` then shows that one
has `dim(ūĚí™_{X,x}) ‚Č§ dim(ūĚí™_{Y_1,y})`. But if one had `ūĚĒć ‚Č† 0`, one would conclude that
`dim(ūĚí™_{Y_1,y}) < dim(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` since `Y` is integral `(0, 16.1.2.2)`; one would consequently have
`dim(ūĚí™_{X,x}) < dim(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` contrary to the hypothesis, which proves (ii).

**Remarks (18.10.2).** ‚ÄĒ (i) When `A = ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is Noetherian, and one knows that its completion `√ā` is integral (for
example if `A` is regular), one can, in the preceding proof, replace `A'` by `√ā`.

(ii) When `Y` is locally Noetherian, one can give of `(18.10.1)` a more rapid proof, not using strict Henselization, but
bringing in the rather delicate results of ¬ß¬ß14 and 15. Since `f` is quasi-finite at the point `x` by hypothesis
`(17.4.1)`, one can, by replacing `X` by an open neighbourhood of `x`, suppose that `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y) = {x}`. Hypothesis (ii)
implies, as one has seen, the existence of an irreducible component `Z` of `X` containing `x` and dominating the unique
irreducible component `Y_0` of `Y` containing `y`. The morphism `f | Z` being quasi-finite at the point `x`, hence
equidimensional at that point `(13.2.2)`, it follows from the hypothesis on `Y` and from Chevalley's criterion
`(14.4.4)` that `g = f | Z` is universally open at the point `x`. Moreover, since `f` (hence also `g`) is unramified at
the point `x`, `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(g(x))` is geometrically reduced over `k(g(x))` `(17.4.1)`; since `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is integral, one
concludes from `(15.2.3)` that `g` is flat at the point `x`, so `f` is √©tale at that point `(18.4.9)`.

(iii) The notations and the hypotheses on `Y` being those of `(18.10.1)`, suppose only that `f` is locally of finite
type (and not necessarily locally of finite presentation). Then, in order that `ūĚí™_{X,x}` be an essentially √©tale
`ūĚí™_{Y,y}`-algebra `(18.6.1)`, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` verify the two following conditions:

<!-- original page 159 -->

1¬į `f` is formally unramified at the point `x`;

2¬į the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,y} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is injective.

There remains only to prove the sufficiency of these conditions. Taking account of `(18.4.12)`, it suffices to show that
`f` is flat at the point `x`. Now, taking up again the proof of `(18.10.1)` and the notations used in that proof, it
suffices to show that `f'` is flat at the point `x'` `(2.5.1)`; moreover, `f'` is formally unramified at the point `x'`
`(17.1.3)`; one can therefore restrict to the proof when `Y = Spec(A)`, where `A` is strictly local and `y = f(x)` is
the closed point of `Y`. Using then `(18.8.3)` one sees that the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,y} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is surjective, hence
bijective by virtue of the hypothesis, and that suffices to show the flatness of `f` at the point `x`.

Suppose moreover that `Y` is locally integral at the point `y` `(I, 2.1.8)`. Then conditions 1¬į and 2¬į above (joined
with the fact that `Y` is geometrically unibranch at the point `y` and `f` locally of finite type) already imply that
`f` is √©tale at the point `x`. Indeed, this results from the foregoing and from `(18.4.13)`.

**Corollary (18.10.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.3 -->

*Let `Y` be an integral and geometrically unibranch prescheme, `ő∑` its generic point, `X` a connected prescheme,
`f : X ‚Üí Y` a morphism locally of finite type and such that `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is non-empty. Then, if `f` is formally unramified,
`f` is √©tale, and `X` is integral and geometrically unibranch.*

It follows from the hypothesis and from `(18.4.13)` that `f` is √©tale at all points where it is flat, and in particular
at the points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`, since `ūĚí™_{Y,ő∑} = k(ő∑)` is a field. The open set `U` of points of `X` where `f` is √©tale is
therefore non-empty. Let us show, on the other hand, that for every `x ‚ąą X`, the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,f(x)} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is
injective. Indeed, let `s` be a section of `ūĚí™_Y` above an open neighbourhood of `f(x)`, which one can always suppose to
be `Y` itself (the question being local on `X` and `Y`), and suppose that its image
`t ‚ąą őď(Y, f_*(ūĚí™_X)) = őď(X, ūĚí™_X)` has a germ zero at the point `x`, hence vanishes in an open neighbourhood `V` of `x`;
then the restriction of `t` to the open `U ‚ą© V ‚Č† ‚ąÖ` is zero; but the restriction `f | (U ‚ą© V)` is √©tale, so
`W = f(U ‚ą© V)` is open in `Y` and consequently contains the generic point `ő∑`; the homomorphism
`ūĚí™_{Y,f(z)} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,z}` being injective for every point `z ‚ąą f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`, the hypothesis that `t | (U ‚ą© V) = 0` implies that
`s | W = 0`, and since `Y` is integral, this implies `s = 0`, whence our assertion. Applying now `(18.10.2, (iii))`, one
concludes that `f` is flat at the point `x`, in other words `x ‚ąą U`, and `U` is therefore both open and closed in `X`;
since it is non-empty and `X` is connected, one has `U = X`.

Note now that since `f` is √©tale, hence locally quasi-finite, and `Y` is irreducible, the maximal points of `X` belong
to `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` `(2.3.4)` and for every `x ‚ąą X`, there is a neighbourhood of `x` which contains only a finite number of
these maximal points; in other words the set of irreducible components of `X` is locally finite. But by `(18.10.1)`, `X`
is integral and geometrically unibranch at every point, so every point of `X` belongs to only one irreducible component,
and since the latter form a locally finite set, they are open (and closed) in `X`; since `X` is connected, it is
integral.

<!-- original page 160 -->

**Remark (18.10.3.1).** ‚ÄĒ If `f : X ‚Üí Y` is locally of finite presentation, dominant and quasi-compact, the fibre
`f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is non-empty by virtue of `(1.1.5)`. On the contrary, if one does not suppose `f` quasi-compact, `f` can be
unramified and dominant without `f` being √©tale (still supposing verified the hypotheses of `(18.10.3)` for `X` and
`Y`). This is shown by the following example: one takes for `Y` the affine plane `Spec(‚Ąā[T, U])`; one considers on the
other hand a family `(X_j)_{j ‚ąą ‚Ą§}` of preschemes isomorphic to the affine line `Spec(‚Ąā[T])`, and one forms the
prescheme obtained by "gluing" `X_{2j}` and `X_{2j+1}` at the point `‚ąí1` and `X_{2j+1}` and `X_{2j+2}` at the point
`+1`. Define on the other hand `f : X ‚Üí Y` as being equal on `X_{2j}` to the closed immersion whose image is the line
of equation `y = 2j`, transforming the point `‚ąí1` into `(2j‚ąí1, 2j)` and the point `+1` into `(2j+1, 2j)`; on `X_{2j+1}`,
`f` is the closed immersion whose image is the line of equation `x = 2j+1`, transforming the point `‚ąí1` into
`(2j+1, 2j)` and the point `+1` into `(2j+1, 2j+2)`. It is clear that `f` is a local immersion (hence is unramified) and
is dominant but the fibre of the generic point of `Y` is empty and `f` is not √©tale.

**Corollary (18.10.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.4 -->

*Let `g : Y ‚Üí S`, `h : X ‚Üí S` be two morphisms locally of finite presentation, `f : X ‚Üí Y` an `S`-morphism, `x` a point
of `X`, `y = f(x)`, `s = g(y) = h(x)`. Suppose that `h` is flat at the point `x` and `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)` normal at the point `y`.
Then, in order that `f` be √©tale at the point `x`, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` be unramified at the point
`x` and that `dim_x h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s) = dim_y g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)`.*

The conditions are necessary by virtue of the fact that if `f` is √©tale at the point `x`, the same is true of
`f_s : h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s) ‚Üí g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)`, and the conclusion then results from the fact that `f_s` is flat and quasi-finite at the point
`x` `(6.1.2)`. Conversely, if the conditions of the statement are verified, to see that `f` is √©tale at the point `x`,
it suffices, by virtue of `(17.8.3)`, to see that `f_s` is. One can therefore restrict to the case where `S` is the
spectrum of a field. It results then from the hypothesis `dim_x X = dim_y Y` and from the fact that `f` is unramified at
the point `x` that one has (by `(5.2.3)` and `(17.4.1)`) `dim(ūĚí™_{X,x}) = dim(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`; since `f` is quasi-finite at the
point `x` and `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` integral, one deduces from `(0_I, 7.4.4)` and `(0, 16.3.10)` that the homomorphism
`ūĚí™_{Y,y} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is injective; it then suffices to conclude by applying `(18.10.1)`.

**Corollary (18.10.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.5 -->

*With the notations of `(18.10.4)`, suppose that `h` is flat and that `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)` is normal for every `s ‚ąą S` (which will
be for example the case if `g` is smooth). In order that `f` be an open immersion, it is necessary and sufficient that
`f` be a monomorphism and that, for every `x ‚ąą X`, one have `dim_x h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s) = dim_y g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(s)`, where `y = f(x)` and
`s = g(y) = h(x)`.*

One has only to prove the sufficiency of the conditions; now, it results from `(17.1.3)` that a monomorphism locally of
finite presentation is unramified, so the hypotheses imply that `f` is √©tale `(18.10.4)`; one concludes by `(17.9.1)`.

**Lemma (18.10.6).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.6 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a flat and locally quasi-finite morphism (EGA, 2.0).*

*(i) The set `Max(X)` of maximal points of `X` is in canonical bijective correspondence with the set
`‚čÉ_{y ‚ąą Max(Y)} f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`.*

*(ii) If `Y` is irreducible and of generic point `ő∑`, the set of irreducible components of `X` is in canonical
bijective correspondence with `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`, and in particular is finite if and only if `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is finite.*

<!-- original page 161 -->

*(iii) If the set of irreducible components of `Y` is locally finite, the same is true of the set of irreducible
components of `X`, and in particular `X` is locally connected.*

Assertion (i) results immediately from `(2.3.4)` and `(0_I, 2.1.6)` and from the fact that the fibres `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` are
discrete sets. It is clear that (ii) is a particular case of (i). Finally, to prove (iii), one can, by virtue of (i),
restrict to the case where `f` is quasi-finite and where `Y` is irreducible `(0_I, 2.1.6)`, and the conclusion then
results from (ii) and from the fact that the fibres of `f` are finite sets `(II, 6.2.2)`.

**Proposition (18.10.7).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.7 -->

*Let `Y` be a geometrically unibranch and irreducible (resp. integral) prescheme, `f : X ‚Üí Y` an √©tale morphism. Then
`X` is isomorphic to a sum of irreducible (resp. integral) preschemes `X_őĽ`, where `őĽ` ranges over the fibre `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`
of the generic point `ő∑` of `Y`. The `X_őĽ` are geometrically unibranch; if `Y` is normal, the `X_őĽ` are normal.*

Indeed, `X` is geometrically unibranch `(17.5.7)`, so every point of `X` belongs to only one irreducible component;
moreover, by virtue of `(18.10.6)` and `(17.6.1)` the set of irreducible components of `X` is locally finite, so
`(0_I, 2.1.6)` these are the connected components `X_őĽ` of `X` (`őĽ ‚ąą f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`), and `X` is the sum of the `X_őĽ`; by
virtue of `(17.5.7)`, if `Y` is integral (resp. normal), the `X_őĽ` are integral (resp. normal).

**Proposition (18.10.8).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.8 -->

*Let `Y` be a normal and integral prescheme, of generic point `ő∑`, `K = k(ő∑)` its field of rational functions,
`f : X ‚Üí Y` an √©tale morphism. Suppose that `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is finite and non-empty, so that the `K`-scheme `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is the
spectrum of a finite separable `K`-algebra `L`, direct composite of a finite number of fields `K_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ n`),
finite separable extensions of `K` `(17.6.2)`. Let `Y'` be the integral closure of `Y` in `L`, isomorphic to the sum of
the integral closures `Y'_i` of `Y` in the `K_i` `(II, 6.3.6)`. Then the morphism `f` factors in a unique way as
`f : X ‚Üí^{f'} Y' ‚Üí^g Y`, such that the restriction `f'_ő∑ : f'‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑) ‚Üí g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is the canonical isomorphism. Moreover `f'`
is a local isomorphism; in order that `f'` be an open immersion, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` be separated.*

By virtue of `(18.10.7)`, one can restrict to the case where `X` is integral and normal, `L` its field of rational
functions, `f` being dominant. The existence and uniqueness of the factorization of `f` considered then result from
`(II, 6.3.9)`. Since `f'` is birational and locally quasi-finite, the last assertions result from `(8.12.10)`.

**Corollary (18.10.9).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.9 -->

*Under the hypotheses of `(18.10.8)`, in order that `f` be a finite morphism (in other words, in order that `X` be an
√©tale cover of `Y`), it is necessary and sufficient that `X` be isomorphic to the integral closure `Y'` of `Y` in `L`.*

If `f` is finite, the canonical injection `j : X ‚Üí Y'` (which is an open immersion) is also a finite morphism
`(II, 6.1.5, (v))`, hence a closed morphism `(II, 6.1.10)`, and since `j(X)` is dense in `Y'` by `(18.10.8)`, one has
`j(X) = Y'`. Conversely, since `Y` is normal and `L` direct composite of finite separable extensions of `K`, `Y'` is
finite over `Y` (Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. V, ¬ß1, n¬į 6, cor. 1 of prop. 18), whence the corollary.

**(18.10.10)** Let `Y` be a normal and integral prescheme, `K = R(Y)` its field of rational functions. We shall say that
a `K`-algebra `L` of finite rank is *unramified over `Y`* if: 1¬į `L` is a separable `K`-algebra, hence direct composite
of finite separable extensions `K_i` of `K` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ n`); 2¬į the integral closure `Y'` of `Y` in `L` (sum of the
preschemes integral closures of `Y` in the `K_i`) is unramified over `Y` (which, by `(18.10.3)`,

<!-- original page 162 -->

amounts to saying that `Y'` is √©tale over `Y`). When `Y = Spec(A)`, where `A` is an integral and integrally closed ring,
`K` its field of fractions, one will also say (by abuse of language, and when there is no risk of confusion with the
terminology of `(17.3.2, (ii))`) that *`L` is unramified over `A`* instead of "unramified over `Y`".

**Remark (18.10.11).** ‚ÄĒ Instead of saying that `L` is unramified over `Y` certain authors also say that `L` is
"unramified over `K`"; we shall refrain from following this usage, which lends itself to confusions.

One can then express `(18.10.9)` in the following form:

**Corollary (18.10.12).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.12 -->

*Let `Y` be a normal and integral prescheme, `K = R(Y)` its field of rational functions. Then the functor `X ‚Ü¶ R(X)` is
an equivalence between the category of √©tale covers of `Y`, and the category of finite √©tale `K`-algebras which are
unramified over `Y`. One obtains a quasi-inverse functor by making correspond to every finite √©tale `K`-algebra `L`,
unramified over `Y`, the integral closure `Y'` of `Y` in `L`.*

**Proposition (18.10.13).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.13 -->

*Let `Y` be a normal and integral prescheme, `K = R(Y)` its field of rational functions.*

*(i) The field `K` is a `K`-algebra unramified over `Y`.*

*(ii) Let `L` be a finite extension of `K`, unramified over `Y`, and let `Z` be the integral closure of `Y` in `L`.
Then, if `M` is an `L`-algebra unramified over `Z`, `M` is also a `K`-algebra unramified over `Y` ("transitivity" of
non-ramification).*

*(iii) Let `Y'` be a normal and integral prescheme, `K'` its field of rational functions, `g : Y' ‚Üí Y` a dominant
morphism. If `L` is a `K`-algebra unramified over `Y`, then `L ‚äó_K K'` is a `K'`-algebra unramified over `Y'`
("translation" property). If moreover `Y = Spec(A)` and `Y' = Spec(A')` are affine, and if `C` is the integral closure of
`A` in `L`, then `C' = C ‚äó_A A'` is the integral closure of `A'` in `L ‚äó_K K'`.*

Assertion (i) is immediate, `Y` being by hypothesis its own normalization in `K`. To prove (ii), let `Z'` be the
integral closure of `Z` in `M`, which is by hypothesis an √©tale cover of `Z`. Since `Z` is √©tale and separated over `Y`,
the same is true of `Z'` `(17.3.3)` which is evidently the integral closure of `Y` in `M`. Since `M` is a finite and
separable `K`-algebra, it results from `(18.10.10)` that `M` is unramified over `Y`. Finally, to prove (iii), note that
if `Z` is the integral closure of `Y` in `L`, `Z √ó_Y Y'` is √©tale and separated over `Y'` `(17.3.3)`, finite over `Y'`
since `Z` is finite over `Y`, and admits as ring of rational functions `L ‚äó_K K'` `(I, 3.4.9)`; since `Y'` is normal,
the same is true of `Z √ó_Y Y'` `(17.5.7)`, which is therefore the integral closure of `Y'` in `L ‚äó_K K'`, which
finishes the proof.

**Corollary (18.10.14).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.14 -->

*Let `Y` and `Y'` be two normal and integral preschemes, `K`, `K'` their respective fields of rational functions,
`g : Y' ‚Üí Y` a dominant morphism, so that `K'` is an extension of `K`. Let `L` be a finite extension of `K` unramified
over `Y`, `L_1` a composed extension (Bourbaki, Alg., chap. VIII, ¬ß8, def. 1) of `L` and `K'`. Then `L_1` is an extension
of `K'` unramified over `Y'`. If moreover `Y = Spec(A)`, `Y' = Spec(A')` are affine, and if `C` is the integral closure
of `A` in `L`, then the subring `C_1 = A'[C, A']` of `L_1` is the integral closure of `A'` in `L_1`.*

Indeed, `L ‚äó_K K'` is the direct composite of finite separable extensions of `K'`, and `L_1`

<!-- original page 163 -->

is one of these extensions; therefore the integral closure of `Y` in `L` is the sum of preschemes one of which is the
integral closure of `Y` in `L_1`. The corollary therefore results at once from `(18.10.13)`.

When for example `A` is the ring of integers of a number field `K`, `K'` and `L` algebraic extensions of `K`, there are
classical examples showing that the relation `C_1 = A'[C, A']` fails when `L` is not unramified over `A`.

**(18.10.15)** Suppose that `Y = Spec(A)` is affine, `A` being integral and integrally closed; let `K` be its field of
fractions, `L` a finite separable extension of `K`, and suppose that the integral closure `C` of `A` in `L` is a
projective `A`-module of finite type (which will be for example the case if `A` is a Dedekind ring (Bourbaki,
Alg. comm., chap. VII, ¬ß4, n¬į 10, prop. 22)). To say that `L` is unramified over `A` means that `C` is √©tale
`(18.10.10)` and by virtue of `(18.2.7, (ii))`, this is therefore equivalent to saying that the discriminant `d_{C/A}`
of `Spec(C)` over `Spec(A)` is invertible in `A`. In the particular case where `C` is a free `A`-module and
`(c_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ n}` a basis of `C` over `A`, this means that `det(Tr_{L/K}(c_i c_j))` is invertible in `A`.

**Theorem (18.10.16).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.16 -->

*Let `Y` be an integral prescheme, `ő∑` its generic point, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a separated morphism locally of finite type.
Suppose that every irreducible component of `X` dominates `Y` and that the generic fibre `X_ő∑ = f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is a prescheme
finite over `K = k(ő∑)`, so that `(X_ő∑)_{red}` is equal to `Spec(L)`, where `L = ‚ąŹ L_i` is a product of finite extensions
`L_i` of `K`. One sets*

```text
  n = [L : K] = ‚ąĎ [L_i : K],    n_s = ‚ąĎ [L_i : K]_s   (sum of separable degrees of the L_i).
```

*Let `y` be a geometrically unibranch point of `Y`, and denote by `n(y)` the sum of the separable degrees over `k(y)`
of the residue fields of the isolated points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`. Then:*

*(i) one has `n(y) ‚Č§ n_s ‚Č§ n`.*

*(ii) Suppose `X` reduced and the point `y` normal in `Y`. In order that `n(y) = n_s`, it is necessary and sufficient
that there exist an open neighbourhood `U` of `y` in `Y` such that the restriction `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí U` of `f` be an √©tale and
finite morphism.*

A) *Reduction to the case where `f` is quasi-finite.* ‚ÄĒ One knows `(13.1.4)` that the set `Z` of points `x ‚ąą X` isolated
in `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(f(x))` is open in `X` and by hypothesis `Z` contains `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`. Since `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is finite, `Z` is the filtered
increasing union of quasi-compact open sets `V_őĽ` containing `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` (hence dense in `X` by hypothesis). If `n_őĽ(y)` is
the sum of the separable degrees over `k(y)` of the residue fields of the points of `V_őĽ ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, one has
`n(y) = sup_őĽ n_őĽ(y)`; to prove (i), it will therefore suffice to show that `n_őĽ(y) ‚Č§ n_s`. On the other hand, if
`n(y) = n_s`, there is an index `őĽ` such that `n_őĽ(y) = n_s`. Suppose proved that there then exists an open
neighbourhood `U` of `y` in `Y` such that the restriction `V_őĽ ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí U` of `f` is an √©tale and finite morphism.
Since `f` is separated, the canonical injection `V_őĽ ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U) ‚Üí f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` is also a finite morphism
`(II, 6.1.5, (v))`, so `V_őĽ ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` is closed in `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` `(II, 6.1.10)`; but being everywhere dense in `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)`, it is
necessarily equal to it.

B) *Reduction to the case where `X` is reduced, `f` finite and `Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`.* ‚ÄĒ One will note that the hypothesis
on `f` implies that it is also verified for `f_{red}` and that the conclusion of (i) concerns only `f_{red}`. One can
therefore replace `f` everywhere by `f_{red}` and suppose `X` reduced.

<!-- original page 164 -->

On the other hand, by virtue of `(18.12.13)` (¬Ļ), one can (after having replaced `Y` by an affine open neighbourhood `V`
of `y` and `f` by its restriction `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(V) ‚Üí V`) factor `f` as `X ‚Üí^j X' ‚Üí^g Y`, where `g` is finite and `j` an open
immersion. Since `X` is reduced, one can replace `X'` by the reduced closed sub-prescheme of underlying space `j(X)`
`(I, 5.2.2)`, hence suppose `X'` reduced and such that every irreducible component of `X'` dominates `Y`. Moreover,
`j(X)` is a dense open in `X'` and has only a finite number of irreducible components by virtue of the hypothesis; so
`(0_I, 1.2.7)` the irreducible components of `X'` are the closures of those of `j(X)`, and consequently the fibre
`g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` identifies with `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`. If one supposes the theorem proved when moreover `f` is a finite morphism, one can
apply it to `g`, and since `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` identifies with a sub-prescheme of `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, relation (i) for `g` implies the same
relation for `f`. Suppose moreover that `n(y) = n_s`; then, by virtue of what precedes, one can apply the conclusion of
(ii) to the morphism `g`, and by replacing if necessary `Y` by an open neighbourhood of `y`, one can suppose `g` √©tale,
and the same is consequently true of `f`; moreover, one has then `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y) = g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`. Since the morphism `g` is closed and
`j(X)` is open in `X'`, there exists a neighbourhood `U` of `y` in `Y` such that `j(X) ‚äÉ g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)` (`= f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)`), which
proves that (ii) is also true for `f`.

One is thus reduced to the case where `f` is moreover a finite morphism. It is immediate that, to prove (i), one can
restrict to the case where `Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`. Let us show that the same is true to prove (ii). Indeed, suppose proved
that the condition `n(y) = n_s` implies that `X √ó_Y Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` is √©tale and finite over `Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`; since this
implies that `f` is flat and formally unramified at the points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, and that `Y` is integral, one concludes from
`(18.4.13)` that `f` is √©tale at the points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, hence also in an open neighbourhood `V` of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` in `X`; but
since `f` is a finite morphism, hence closed, `V` contains an open of the form `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)`, where `U` is an open
neighbourhood of `y` in `Y`.

C) *Reduction to the case where `Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` and `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is strictly local.* ‚ÄĒ Set `A = ūĚí™_{Y,y}`, and let
`A' = ^{sh}A` be the strict Henselization of `A` `(18.8.7)`, which is an integral and geometrically unibranch local ring
`(18.8.16)`. Let `Y' = Spec(A')`, and let `y'` be the closed point and `ő∑'` the generic point of `Y'`; `y'` lies above
`y` and since the morphism `g : Y' ‚Üí Y` is flat `(18.8.8)`, `ő∑'` lies above `ő∑` `(2.3.4)`. Set `X' = X √ó_Y Y'`,
`f' = f_{(Y')} : X' ‚Üí Y'`; `f'` is finite and since `g` is flat, every irreducible component of `X'` dominates `Y'`
`(2.3.7)`. If `n'(y')`, `n'` and `n'_s` are the numbers defined for `f'` in the same way as `n(y)`, `n` and `n_s` for
`f`, one has `n'_s = n_s` and `n'(y') = n(y)` `(I, 6.4.8)`, and it therefore amounts to the same to prove assertion (i)
of the statement for `f` or for `f'`. Moreover, if `X` is reduced and `y` a normal point of `Y` (hence `A` integrally
closed), then `A'` is integrally closed `(18.8.12)`. On the other hand it results from the definition `(18.8.7)`, from
the remark `(8.1.2, a))` and from the theorem of the double inductive limit, that `A'` is the inductive limit of rings
`A_őĪ` such that the morphisms `Spec(A_őĪ) ‚Üí Spec(A) = Y` are √©tale; consequently `X'` is the projective limit of the
`X_őĪ = X ‚äó_A A_őĪ`, which are √©tale over `X`, hence reduced since `X` is `(17.5.7)`; by passage to the limit,

> (¬Ļ) The reader will verify that `(18.10.16)` is not used in the proof of `(18.12.13)`. If `f` is supposed locally of
> finite presentation (resp. quasi-projective), one can replace `(18.12.13)` by `(8.12.8)` (resp. `(8.12.6)`).

<!-- original page 165 -->

one deduces that `X'` is reduced `(8.7.1)`. This being so, if `n(y) = n_s`, one has `n_s = n` and consequently the
residue fields at the points of `X_ő∑` are necessarily separable over `k(ő∑)`; the residue fields at the points of
`X'_{ő∑'}` are consequently algebraic and separable over `k(ő∑')` `(4.6.1)`, in other words `n'_s = n' = n`, and one has
therefore by virtue of what precedes `n'(y') = n'`. The morphism `f'` therefore has the same properties as `f`, and if
one proves that `f'` is √©tale, it will result that `f` is √©tale by `(17.7.1)`.

D) *End of the proof.* ‚ÄĒ Suppose therefore `A` strictly local. Since `A` is Henselian and the morphism `f` finite, it
results from `(18.5.11)` that if `x_j` (`1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ m`) are the distinct points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, `X` is the sum of the open
sub-preschemes `Spec(ūĚí™_{X,x_j}) = X_j`, which are therefore the connected components of `X`. By hypothesis, each of
these dominates `Y`, hence meets `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)`, and consequently the number of points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(ő∑)` is `‚Č• m`; a fortiori one
has `n ‚Č• m`. But since `k(y)` is separably closed and the `k(x_j)` are algebraic over `k(y)`, these are necessarily
radicial extensions of `k(y)`, hence `m = n(y)`, and this proves (i). Suppose now verified the hypotheses of (ii); the
relations `n(y) = n_s = n` imply first, since every irreducible component of `X` dominates `Y`, that each of the `X_j`
is *irreducible*; moreover, since the `X_j` are reduced, they are integral; finally the relations `m = n_s = n` imply
that for the generic point `őĺ_j` of `X_j`, `k(őĺ_j)` is a separable extension of `k(ő∑)` of degree 1, hence isomorphic to
`k(ő∑)`; in other words, the restriction `f | X_j : X_j ‚Üí Y` of `f` is a *finite and birational* morphism; moreover,
`X_j` is integral and by hypothesis `Y` is normal; so `(8.12.10.1)` `f | X_j` is an isomorphism, which proves that `f`
is √©tale, and finishes the proof of `(18.10.16)`.

**Remark (18.10.17).** ‚ÄĒ The method of "√©tale localization" used in the proof of `(18.10.16)` also allows one to improve
the results of `(15.5.1)` by eliminating the Noetherian hypothesis. One has first the following result which generalizes
`(15.5.2)`:

**(18.10.17.1)** *Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a separated and quasi-finite morphism, `y` a point of `Y` such that `f` is
universally open at the points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`. Then, for every generization `y'` of `y`, one has `n(y') ‚Č§ n(y)`.*

One can replace `Y` by the reduced closed sub-prescheme `Z` of underlying space `{y'}^-` and `X` by `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(Z)`, hence
suppose `Y` integral of generic point `y'`. Using `(18.12.13)` as in `(18.10.16, B)`, one can suppose that `X` is dense
open in a prescheme `X'` and that `f` is the restriction to `X` of a *finite* morphism `g : X' ‚Üí Y`. Let us show that
one has `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') = f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')`. Indeed, let `i : g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') ‚Üí X'` be the canonical injection, which is a flat morphism since
`y'` is a generic point of `Y` `(I, 3.6.5)`; one can write `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') = i‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(X)`. On the other hand, the canonical
injection `j : X ‚Üí X'` is a morphism of finite type since the composite `g ‚ąė j = f` is of finite type and `g` is
separated `(1.5.4)`; so `X` is a retrocompact open in `X'`, and consequently is pro-constructible in `X'`
`(1.9.5, (v))`. One concludes therefore from `(2.3.10)` that one has `i‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(X)^- = i‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(X^-)` in other words `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` is
dense in `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')`; but since `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` is discrete, one has necessarily `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') = g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')`.

One is thus reduced to proving that the sum of the numbers `[k(x) : k(y)]_s` where `x` ranges over the set of points of
`g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` where `g` is universally open, is at most equal

<!-- original page 166 -->

to the number `n(y')` (for the morphism `g`). Using next the fact that the property of being universally open at a
point is preserved under base change, one shows, as in `(18.10.16, C)`, that one can reduce to the case where
`Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`, with `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` strictly local. Then, with the notations of `(18.10.16, D)`, `X` is the sum of the
open sub-preschemes `X_j = Spec(ūĚí™_{X,x_j})`, where `x_j` ranges over `g‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`; the hypothesis that `g` is open at one of
these points `x_j` implies that the corresponding sub-prescheme `X_j` dominates `Y` `(1.10.3)`; one concludes as in
`(18.10.16, D)`.

One next derives from `(18.10.17.1)` that the conclusions of `(15.5.1)` are still valid when one no longer supposes `Y`
locally Noetherian, but only that `f` is separated, quasi-finite and of finite presentation.

Indeed, to prove assertion (i) of `(15.5.1)`, one remarks that, since `f` is of finite presentation, the set `E` of
points `y ‚ąą Y` such that `n ‚Č• n(y)` is locally constructible `(9.7.9)`; to show that `y` is interior to `E`, it
suffices, by virtue of `(1.10.1)`, to see that every generization `y'` of `y` belongs to `E`, which is none other than
`(18.10.17.1)`.

To prove assertion (ii) of `(15.5.1)`, one can, since `f` is of finite presentation, use `(8.10.5, (xii))` applied
following the method of `(8.1.2, a))`, and one can therefore already suppose that `Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`. Applying next
`(2.7.1, (vii))`, one sees that one can by the faithfully flat base change `Y' ‚Üí Y`, where `Y' = Spec(^{sh}ūĚí™_{Y,y})`,
suppose that the ring `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is strictly local. Applying `(18.5.11, c))`, one sees then that if `x_j` (`1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n`)
are the points of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)`, `X` is the sum of `n` open sub-preschemes `X_j = Spec(ūĚí™_{X,x_j})` which are finite over `Y`,
and of an open prescheme `X''`. If one proves that `X'' = ‚ąÖ`, one will have shown that `f` is finite, hence proper. Now,
the fields `k(x_j)` being algebraic extensions of `k(y)`, are radicial, and consequently `n(y) = n`; since `f` is open
at each of the points `x_j`, `X_j` dominates `Y` `(1.10.3)`, hence the restriction of `f` to `X_j`, being a finite
morphism, is surjective `(II, 6.1.10)`. The hypothesis that `n(z)` is constant in `Y` therefore implies that
`X'' ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(z) = ‚ąÖ` for every `z ‚ąą Y`, that is to say `X'' = ‚ąÖ`.

Finally, the proof of (iii) reproduces without change that given in `(15.5.1)`.

**(18.10.17.2)** By analogous methods using strict Henselization, one can, in most of the results of ¬ß¬ß14 and 15, rid
oneself of the Noetherian hypotheses.

**Lemma (18.10.18).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.18 -->

*Let `X`, `Y` be two preschemes, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a birational morphism (¬Ļ), `x` a point of `X`. In order that `f` be a
local isomorphism at the point `x`, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` be √©tale at the point `x`. In order that
`f` be an open immersion, it is necessary and sufficient that `f` be √©tale and separated.*

The conditions stated being trivially necessary, everything reduces to seeing that they are sufficient. For the first
assertion, the question is local on `X` and `Y`, so one can suppose `f` √©tale, `X` and `Y` affine, hence `f` separated,
and one is reduced to proving the second assertion. By virtue of `(17.9.1)`, it is a matter of proving that `f` is
radicial. Let then `y ‚ąą Y` and let us prove that `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` is radicial over `k(y)`. The base change
`Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y}) ‚Üí Y` not changing the fact that `f` is √©tale, separated and birational, one can restrict to the case
where `Y = Spec(A)`, with `A = ūĚí™_{Y,y}`. Let then `A' = ^{sh}A`, which is a local ring and a faithfully flat `A`-module

> (¬Ļ) We mean by this the notion defined in `(6.15.4)`, but where one does not suppose `X` and `Y` reduced.

<!-- original page 167 -->

`(18.8.8)`, the homomorphism `A ‚Üí A'` being local. Set `Y' = Spec(A')`, `X' = X √ó_Y Y'`, `f' = f_{(Y')} : X' ‚Üí Y'`, so
that `f'` is √©tale and separated; moreover, since the morphism `Y' ‚Üí Y` is flat, the reasoning of `(6.15.4.1)` shows
that `f'` is also birational. If `y'` is the closed point of `Y'`, it will suffice, by virtue of `(2.6.1, (v))`, to
prove that `f'‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` is radicial over `k(y')`; since `f'` is √©tale, it will suffice to prove that `f'‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` contains
at most one point `(17.6.1, c'))`.

Suppose therefore `Y = Spec(A)`, `A` being strictly local, `f` separated, √©tale and birational, and let us show that
`f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` cannot contain more than one point. Indeed, if there existed in `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y)` two distinct points `x_1`, `x_2`,
there would exist two `Y`-sections `u_1`, `u_2` of `f` such that `u_1(y) = x_1` and `u_2(y) = x_2` `(18.8.1)`; but since
`f` is √©tale and separated, `u_1` and `u_2` would be isomorphisms of `Y` onto two (open) connected components of `X`
`(17.9.4)` and there would therefore be at least two maximal points of `X` above every maximal point of `Y`, which
contradicts the hypothesis that `f` is birational.

**Proposition (18.10.19).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.19 -->

*Let `Y` be a reduced prescheme whose set of irreducible components is locally finite, `f : X ‚Üí Y` a separated morphism,
locally of finite type and formally unramified, `g` a rational `Y`-section of `f` (i.e. a rational `Y`-map of `Y` into
`X`), `U` the domain of definition of `g` `(I, 7.2.1)`, and let `Z` be the closure of `g(U)` in `X`. Then, for every
point `y ‚ąą Y ‚ąí U` such that `Y` is geometrically unibranch at the point `y`, one has `Z_y = Z ‚ą© f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y) = ‚ąÖ`. In
particular, if `Y` is geometrically unibranch, `g(U)` is a subset both open and closed of `X`. If, for every closed
irreducible part `T` of `X`, `f(T)` is closed in `Y`, `g` is defined at every geometrically unibranch point of `Y`.*

Since `Y` is reduced and `f` separated, it results from `(I, 7.2.2)` that there exists a `U`-section `u` of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(U)`
belonging to the class `g`; moreover `(17.4.1.2)`, since `f` is formally unramified, `u` is an isomorphism of `U` onto
the prescheme induced on the open `u(U)` of `X`. If one denotes again by `Z` the reduced sub-prescheme of `X` having `Z`
as underlying space, `u(U) = u(U)`, being reduced, is also induced by `Z`, so the restriction `f' = f | Z` of `f` is a
birational morphism of `Z` into `Y`, moreover formally unramified, since `f` is `(17.1.3)`. The question being local on
`Y`, since `Y` is reduced and geometrically unibranch (hence integral) at the point `y`, one can restrict (by replacing
`Y` by an open neighbourhood of `y`) to the case where `Y` is integral; then `u(U)` is irreducible, hence so is `Z`, and
consequently `Z` is also integral. Then `(I, 8.2.7)` since `f'` is dominant, the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Y,f'(z)} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{Z,z}` is
injective for every `z ‚ąą Z_y`, and one concludes from `(18.10.2, (iii))` that `f'` is an √©tale morphism; being
separated and birational, it is a local isomorphism at the point `z` by virtue of `(18.10.18)`; but this would imply
that the `U`-section `u` would be extendible to an open distinct from `U`, contrary to the definition of the latter.
One therefore necessarily has `Z_y = ‚ąÖ`, which establishes the first assertion; the second is an evident consequence.
Moreover, under the hypotheses of the last assertion of the statement, since `Z` is closed and irreducible, `f(Z)` is
closed in `Y`, hence equal to `Y`, i.e. one has `Z_y ‚Č† ‚ąÖ` for every `y ‚ąą Y`, which finishes the proof.

**Remark (18.10.20).** ‚ÄĒ Let `Y` be a locally Noetherian prescheme. One says that a morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y` is *essentially
proper* if it is locally of finite type and if,

<!-- original page 168 -->

for every `Y`-scheme of the form `Y' = Spec(A)` where `A` is a discrete valuation ring, the canonical map
`(II, 7.3.2.2)` relative to the `Y'`-prescheme `X √ó_Y Y'`, is bijective. The reasoning of `(II, 7.3.8)` proves that it
amounts to the same to say that `f` (supposed locally of finite type) is separated and that for every base change
`Y'' ‚Üí Y`, where `Y''` is locally Noetherian, the image by `f_{(Y'')} : X_{(Y'')} ‚Üí Y''` of every closed irreducible
part of `X_{(Y'')}` is closed. To say that `f` is proper (for `Y` locally Noetherian) therefore signifies that `f` is
essentially proper and of finite type `(II, 7.3.8)`; but one encounters important examples of essentially proper
morphisms which are not of finite type (for example certain "Picard preschemes" or certain "N√©ron-Severi preschemes"
(chap. VI)). It results evidently from `(18.10.19)` that *if `Y` is locally Noetherian, reduced and geometrically
unibranch, and if the morphism `f : X ‚Üí Y` is essentially proper and unramified, then every rational `Y`-section of `f`
is everywhere defined*.

The following proposition generalizes the criterion `(17.15.5)` of smoothness of a morphism `X ‚Üí Y` when `Y` is not the
spectrum of a field:

**Proposition (18.10.21).**

<!-- label: IV.18.10.21 -->

*Let `f : X ‚Üí Y` be a morphism locally of finite presentation, `x` a point of `X`, `y = f(x)` and suppose the ring
`ūĚí™_{Y,y}` integral and geometrically unibranch. Let `r` be the minimum number of generators of the
`ūĚí™_{X,x}`-module `(ő©_{X/Y}^1)_x` (equal to the rank of the `k(x)`-vector space `(ő©_{X/Y}^1)_x ‚äó_{ūĚí™_x} k(x)`). Then, in
order that `f` be smooth at the point `x`, it is necessary and sufficient that, if `y'` is the generic point of the
unique irreducible component of `Y` containing `y`, there exist a generization `x'` of `x` such that `f(x') = y'` and
`dim_{x'} f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') ‚Č• r`.*

If `f` is smooth at the point `x`, it is also smooth in an open neighbourhood `U` of `x`, hence at every generization
`x'` of `x` and `dim_{x'}(f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(f(x'))) = r` at each of these points, by virtue of `(17.10.2)`. Since `f` is moreover flat
in `U`, there exists a generization of `x` above every generization of `y` `(2.3.4)`, which proves that the condition is
necessary. To show that it is sufficient, note first that by `(17.5.1)`, it suffices to prove that the morphism deduced
from `f` by the base change `Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y}) ‚Üí Y` is smooth at the point `x`; one can therefore suppose that
`Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})`.

The question is local on `X`, so `((16.4.22)` and `(0_I, 5.2.2))` one can suppose that `X` is affine, and that there
exist `r` sections `s_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`) of `ūĚí™_X` above `X` such that the sections `d_{X/Y}(s_i)` generate `ő©_{X/Y}^1`.
Let

```text
  g : X ‚Üí Z = Y[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_r] = ūĚźē(ūĚí™_Y^r)
```

be the `Y`-morphism corresponding to the homomorphism `ūĚí™_Y^r ‚Üí f_*(ūĚí™_X)` of `ūĚí™_Y`-Modules defined by the `s_i`
(considered as sections of `f_*(ūĚí™_X)` above `Y`) `(II, 1.2.7)`. One has the exact sequence `(16.4.19)`

```text
  g*(ő©_{Z/Y}^1) ‚Üí ő©_{X/Y}^1 ‚Üí ő©_{X/Z}^1 ‚Üí 0.
```

Since the `dT_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`) generate `ő©_{Z/Y}^1`, it results from the definition of `g` that the homomorphism
`g*(ő©_{Z/Y}^1) ‚Üí ő©_{X/Y}^1` is surjective, hence `ő©_{X/Z}^1 = 0`, and `g` is consequently unramified `(17.4.1)`. We
shall see that `g` is √©tale at the point `x`, and taking account of `(17.3.8)`, this will prove that `f` is smooth at
the point `x`. Since `ūĚí™_{Y,y}` is supposed integral and geometrically

<!-- original page 169 -->

unibranch, the same is true of `ūĚí™_{Z,z}` (where `z = g(x)`), since the structural morphism `Z ‚Üí Y` is smooth
`(17.3.8)`. By virtue of `(18.10.1)`, it suffices to show that the homomorphism `ūĚí™_{Z,z} ‚Üí ūĚí™_{X,x}` is injective; it
amounts to the same to see that the corresponding morphism `Spec(ūĚí™_{X,x}) ‚Üí Spec(ūĚí™_{Z,z})` is dominant, since `ūĚí™_{Z,z}`
is integral `(I, 1.2.7)`. Let `y'` be the generic point of `Y = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y})` and let `ő∂` be the generic point of
`Z = Spec(ūĚí™_{Y,y}[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_r])` which is integral; if `h : Z ‚Üí Y` is the structural morphism, one has `h(ő∂) = y'`. It
suffices to prove that there exists in `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` a generization `x'` of `x` such that the image of `x'` by the morphism
`g_{y'} : f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') ‚Üí h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` is equal to `ő∂`. Moreover, `ő∂` is also the generic point of
`h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y') = Spec(k(y')[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_r])`; since the morphism `g_{y'}` is unramified, hence quasi-finite `(17.4.2)`, the
same is true of its restriction to every irreducible component of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')`; since `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` and `h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` are locally
Noetherian and `dim(h‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')) = r`, it results from `(5.4.1)` that the restriction of `g_{y'}` to every irreducible
component of dimension `‚Č• r` of `f‚ĀĽ¬Ļ(y')` is a dominant morphism. Now, there exists by hypothesis such a component
containing a generization of `x`; a fortiori its generic point is a generization of `x`, whence the conclusion.


### 18.11. Application to complete Noetherian local algebras over a field

The following lemma generalizes `(0, 21.9.1)` and `(0, 21.9.2)`:

**Lemma (18.11.1).**

<!-- label: IV.18.11.1 -->

*Let `k` be a field, `A` a complete Noetherian local ring which is a `k`-algebra, `K` the residue field of `A`.*

*(i) If the `K`-vector space `ő©_{K/k}^1` is of finite rank, the `A`-module `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1` is of finite type.*

*(ii) If moreover `A` is a formally smooth `k`-algebra (for the adic topology), `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1` is a free `A`-module of rank
equal to*

```text
  dim(A) + rg_K(ő©_{K/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_K(ő•_{K/k}).
```

*In addition, for every subfield `k_0` of `k` such that `ő©_{k/k_0}^1` is a `k`-vector space of finite rank,
`ő©Őā_{A/k_0}^1` is a free `A`-module of rank equal to*

```text
  dim(A) + rg_K(ő©_{K/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_K(ő•_{K/k}) + rg_k(ő©_{k/k_0}^1).
```

Assertion (i) is only mentioned for memory, having been proved in `(0, 20.7.15)`. To prove (ii), note that this
assertion was in fact established by the proof of `(0, 21.9.2)`, the statement of `(0, 21.9.2)` alone bringing in the
finiteness of the transcendence degree of `K` over `k` (by means of Cartier's equality `(0, 21.7.1)`).

**Lemma (18.11.2).**

<!-- label: IV.18.11.2 -->

*Let `k` be a field of characteristic `p > 0`, `C` a Noetherian, complete local `k`-algebra, whose residue field is a
finite extension of `k`, and which is integral; let `n` be its dimension, `L` its field of fractions. Then `ő©_{L/k}^1`
and `ő•_{L/k}` are `L`-vector spaces of finite rank, and one has*

```text
  (18.11.2.1)    rg_L(ő©_{L/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_L(ő•_{L/k}) ‚Č§ n.
```

<!-- original page 170 -->

*Suppose moreover that `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`. Then one has the equality*

```text
  (18.11.2.2)    rg_L(ő©_{L/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_L(ő•_{L/k}) = n.
```

*Moreover, `ő©_{C/k}^1` is then isomorphic to `ő©Őā_{C/k}^1`, hence by `(18.11.1)` is a `C`-module of finite type.*

One knows indeed `(0, 19.8.9)` that there exists a sub-`k`-algebra `C_0` of `C` isomorphic to a formal power series
algebra `k[[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]]` and such that `C` is a finite `C_0`-algebra. Consequently, `L` is a finite extension of the
field of fractions `L_0 = k((T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n))` of `C_0`. Now, one has the exact sequence of `L`-vector spaces obtained by
applying `(0, 20.6.17.1)` to the prime subfield of `k` and to the three fields `k ‚äā L_0 ‚äā L` (and taking account of
`(0, 20.6.21, (i))`)

```text
  0 ‚Üí ő•_{L/k} ‚Üí ő•_{L_0/k} ‚äó_{L_0} L ‚Üí ő•_{L/L_0} ‚Üí ő©_{k/?}^1 ‚äó_k L ‚Üí ő©_{L_0/k}^1 ‚äó_{L_0} L ‚Üí ő©_{L/L_0}^1 ‚Üí 0.
```

Since `L` is a finite extension of `L_0`, `ő©_{L/L_0}^1` and `ő•_{L/L_0}` are `L`-vector spaces of finite rank having the
same rank, by virtue of Cartier's equality `(0, 21.7.1)`. Since `L_0` is separable over `k` `(0, 21.9.6.4)`, one has
`ő•_{L_0/k} = 0` `(0, 20.6.3)`; one therefore already deduces from the preceding exact sequence that `ő•_{L/k}` is of
finite rank and that one has in all cases `(0_III, 11.10.2)`

```text
  rg_L(ő©_{L/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_L(ő•_{L/k}) = rg_{L_0}(ő©_{L_0/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_{L_0}(ő•_{L_0/k}).
```

To prove `(18.11.2.1)` or `(18.11.2.2)`, one can therefore restrict to proving these relations by replacing `C` and `L`
by `C_0` and `L_0`. Since `L_0` is separable over `k` `(0, 21.9.6.4)`, one has `ő•_{L_0/k} = 0` `(0, 20.6.3)`; on the
other hand `ő©_{L_0/k}^1 = ő©_{C_0/k}^1 ‚äó_{C_0} L_0` `(0, 20.5.9)`. One knows `(0, 21.9.4)` that if
`C_1 = k[[T_1^p, ‚Ä¶, T_n^p]]`, `ő©_{C_0/C_1}^1` identifies with `ő©Őā_{C_0/k[C_0^p]}`; on the other hand, one has
`ő©Őā_{C_0/k[C_0^p]} = ő©Őā_{C_0/k}^1` `(0, 21.1.5)`, and since `C_0^p = k^p[[T_1^p, ‚Ä¶, T_n^p]]`, one has
`k[C_0^p] ‚äā C_1`, with equality when `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`. Now, one has an exact sequence
`ő©Őā_{C_0/k[C_0^p]} ‚Üí ő©Őā_{C_0/C_1} ‚Üí 0` `(0, 20.5.7)`, whence an exact sequence
`ő©Őā_{C_0/k[C_0^p]} ‚Üí ő©_{C_0/C_1}^1 ‚äó_{C_0} L_0 ‚Üí 0`. Since `ő©_{C_0/C_1}^1` is a free `C_0`-module of rank `n`
`(0, 21.9.3)`, one sees that one has in all cases `rg_{L_0}(ő©_{L_0/k}^1) ‚Č§ n`, with equality when `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`;
this already proves `(18.11.2.1)` and `(18.11.2.2)`.

Finally, to see that `ő©_{C/k}^1` is isomorphic to `ő©Őā_{C/k}^1` when `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`, it suffices to prove that
`ő©_{C/k}^1` is a `C`-module of finite type, since `C` is a complete Noetherian local ring `(0_I, 7.3.3)`. Since
`ő©_{C/k}^1` is isomorphic to `ő©_{C/k[C^p]}^1` and `k[C^p] ‚äā k[C_0^p]`, everything reduces to proving that `C` is a
`k[C_0^p]`-module of finite type `(0, 20.4.7)`; but this results from the fact that `C` is a `C_0`-module of finite type
and `C_0` a `k[C_0^p]`-module of finite type by virtue of the hypothesis `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`.

**Lemma (18.11.3).**

<!-- label: IV.18.11.3 -->

*Let `k` be a field, `p` its characteristic exponent, and suppose that `[k : k^p] < +‚ąě`. Let `A` be a complete
Noetherian local `k`-algebra, whose residue field is a finite extension of `k`. Let `ūĚĒ≠` be a prime ideal of `A` such
that `A_ūĚĒ≠` is geometrically regular over `k` `(6.7.6)`, so that there exists a single minimal prime ideal `ūĚĒģ` of `A`
contained in `ūĚĒ≠`. Then `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` is a free `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module of rank equal to `dim(A/ūĚĒģ)`.*

Since `A` is Noetherian, and `Spec(A)` regular (and a fortiori integral) at the point `ūĚĒ≠`, `ūĚĒ≠` belongs to only one
irreducible component of `Spec(A)`, hence contains

<!-- original page 171 -->

only one minimal ideal `ūĚĒģ` of `A`, and moreover one has `ūĚĒģ_ūĚĒ≠ = 0`. If one sets `B = A/ūĚĒģ`, the sequence `(0, 20.7.20)`

```text
  ūĚĒģ/ūĚĒģ^2 ‚Üí^j ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A B ‚Üí ő©_{B/k}^1 ‚Üí 0
```

is exact; indeed, `ő©_{A/k}^1` is an `A`-module of finite type `(18.11.1)`, so one has
`ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A B = ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A B = ő©_{A/k}^1/ūĚĒģ ¬∑ ő©_{A/k}^1`, and since this `B`-module is of finite type, every
sub-`B`-module of it is closed `(0_I, 7.3.5)`, taking account of `(0, 20.4.5)`. Since the image of `ūĚĒģ/ūĚĒģ^2` is dense in
the kernel of the homomorphism `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A B ‚Üí ő©Őā_{B/k}^1` `(0, 20.7.20)`, it is necessarily equal to this kernel,
whence our assertion. Localizing at `ūĚĒ≠` the preceding exact sequence, one sees moreover that the canonical homomorphism
`(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ ‚Üí (ő©_{B/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` is bijective. One sees thus that one can restrict to the case where `ūĚĒģ = 0`, in other
words to the case where `A` is integral. Let us then distinguish two cases:

I) `p > 1`. Then one can apply `(18.11.2)` to the quotient algebra `C = A/ūĚĒ≠`, whose field of fractions `K` is none
other than the residue field of `A_ūĚĒ≠`; one sees therefore that one has

```text
  (18.11.3.1)    rg_K(ő©_{K/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_K(ő•_{K/k}) = dim(A/ūĚĒ≠).
```

Note now `(0, 19.6.6)` that `A_ūĚĒ≠` is a `k`-algebra formally smooth for its `ūĚĒ≠ A_ūĚĒ≠`-preadic topology; consequently
`ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1` is formally projective `(0, 20.4.9)` for the `ūĚĒ≠`-preadic topology `(0, 20.4.5)`; on the other hand,
`ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1 = (ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` `(0, 20.5.9)` is an `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module of finite type, by virtue of `(18.11.2)` applied to `A`.
For every integer `j`, `ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1/ūĚĒ≠^{j+1} ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1` is therefore an `(A_ūĚĒ≠/ūĚĒ≠^{j+1} A_ūĚĒ≠)`-module projective of rank
`m = rg_K(ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1 ‚äó K)` `(0, 19.2.4)`; one concludes therefore from `(0_III, 10.2.1 and 10.1.3)` that
`ő©_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1` is a free `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module of rank `m`. Let `A' = (A_ūĚĒ≠)^‚ąß` be the completed algebra of `A_ūĚĒ≠` for its
`ūĚĒ≠ A_ūĚĒ≠`-preadic topology; `A'` is still a `k`-algebra formally smooth `(0, 19.3.6)` for its adic topology, and it
results from `(0, 20.7.14)` and from `(0, 20.4.5)` that `ő©Őā_{A'/k}^1 = ő©Őā_{A_ūĚĒ≠/k}^1`; one concludes that
`ő©Őā_{A'/k}^1` is a free `A'`-module of rank `m`. But it then results from `(18.11.1)` and from the fact that
`dim(A') = dim(A_ūĚĒ≠)` `(0, 16.2.4)` that one has

```text
  (18.11.3.2)    m = dim(A_ūĚĒ≠) + (rg_K(ő©_{K/k}^1) ‚ąí rg_K(ő•_{K/k}))
```

whence, by virtue of `(18.11.3.1)`

```text
  m = dim(A/ūĚĒ≠) + dim(A_ūĚĒ≠).
```

But since `A` is a complete Noetherian local ring, it is a quotient of a regular ring `(0, 19.8.8)`, hence
`(0, 16.5.12)` one has `dim(A) = dim(A/ūĚĒ≠) + dim(A_ūĚĒ≠)`, which finishes the proof in this case.

II) `p = 1`. One has, as above, `dim(A) = dim(A/ūĚĒ≠) + dim(A_ūĚĒ≠)`; set `n = dim(A)`, `r = dim(A/ūĚĒ≠)`, `s = dim(A_ūĚĒ≠)`; we
shall see that `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` is an `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module free of rank `n`. We shall first prove the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.11.3.3).** *Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring, `ūĚĒ≠` a prime ideal of `A` such that `A_ūĚĒ≠` is a Cohen-Macaulay
ring. For every system of parameters `(t_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s}` of `A_ūĚĒ≠` there exists a sequence `(x_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s}` of
elements of `ūĚĒ≠` such that the `x_i` are part of a system of parameters of `A`*

<!-- original page 172 -->

*and such that, for every `i`, `x_i/1` is congruent to `t_i` modulo the ideal `t_1 A_ūĚĒ≠ + ‚čĮ + t_{i-1} A_ūĚĒ≠`. In
particular, if `A_ūĚĒ≠` is regular and if the `t_i` form a regular system of parameters of `A_ūĚĒ≠`, the same is true of the
`x_i/1`.*

This lemma will itself be a consequence of the following:

**Lemma (18.11.3.4).** *Let `A` be a Noetherian local ring, `ūĚĒ≠` a prime ideal of `A`, `x` an element of `A`; then there
exists an element `x' ‚ąą A` such that `x'/1 = x/1` in `A_ūĚĒ≠` and that `x'` not belong to any of the minimal prime ideals
of `A` not contained in `ūĚĒ≠`.*

Let us first show how `(18.11.3.4)` implies `(18.11.3.3)`. By multiplying if necessary the `t_i` by invertible elements
of `A_ūĚĒ≠`, one can suppose that they are of the form `x_i/1`, with `x_i ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s`. Reasoning by recurrence on
`s`; since `t_1` is part of a system of parameters of `A_ūĚĒ≠`, it does not belong to any of the minimal prime ideals of
`A_ūĚĒ≠` `(0, 16.5.5)`, hence no `x_1 ‚ąą A` such that `x_1/1 = t_1` can belong to a minimal prime ideal of `A` contained in
`ūĚĒ≠`. By virtue of `(18.11.3.4)`, one can moreover choose `x'_1` such that `x'_1/1 = t_1` (hence `x'_1 ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠`) and that
`x'_1` not belong to any minimal prime ideal of `A`, hence be part of a system of parameters of `A`
`(0, 16.3.4 and 16.3.7)`. One then reasons by recurrence, by considering the ring `A' = A/x'_1 A` and the prime ideal
`ūĚĒ≠' = ūĚĒ≠/x'_1 A` of this ring; since `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'} = A_ūĚĒ≠/t_1 A_ūĚĒ≠`, `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}` is also a Cohen-Macaulay ring `(0, 16.5.5)`,
and the images `t'_i` (`2 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s`) of the `t_i` in `A'_{ūĚĒ≠'}` form a system of parameters of this ring; it suffices to
apply to `A'` and to the `t'_i` (`2 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s`) the hypothesis of recurrence. The last assertion of `(18.11.3.3)` results
from the fact that in `A_ūĚĒ≠`, a system of parameters which generates the maximal ideal is a regular system of parameters
`(0, 17.1.1)`.

Let us therefore prove `(18.11.3.4)`. Let `(ūĚĒ≠_k)_{1 ‚Č§ k ‚Č§ r}` be the sequence of minimal prime ideals of `A` not
contained in `ūĚĒ≠` and such that `x ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠_k`, and let `(ūĚĒ≠'_j)_{1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n'}` be the sequence of minimal prime ideals of `A`
other than the `ūĚĒ≠_k`; one can suppose that `r ‚Č• 1`. Since `ūĚĒ≠_k` does not contain `‚čā_{1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n'} ūĚĒ≠'_j`, there exists
an element `y ‚ąą ‚čā_{1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n'} ūĚĒ≠'_j` which is contained in none of the `ūĚĒ≠_k` (Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. II, ¬ß1, n¬į 1,
prop. 2); moreover `y/1` belongs to all the minimal prime ideals of `A_ūĚĒ≠`, hence is nilpotent; if `(y/1)^h = 0`, the
element `x' = x + y^h` will answer to the conditions of the statement, for on the one hand `y^h ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠_k` for
`1 ‚Č§ k ‚Č§ r`, and by definition of the `ūĚĒ≠_k` one has indeed `x' ‚ąČ ūĚĒ≠_k` for `1 ‚Č§ k ‚Č§ r`, and on the other hand, if
`ūĚĒ≠'_j` is a minimal prime ideal of `A` not contained in `ūĚĒ≠` but such that `x ‚ąČ ūĚĒ≠'_j`, one has also `x' ‚ąČ ūĚĒ≠'_j` since
`y^h ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠'_j`.

Let us return to the proof of `(18.11.3)` when `p = 1`. By virtue of `(18.11.3.3)`, there exists a regular system of
parameters `(t_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s}` of `A_ūĚĒ≠` such that `t_i = x_i/1`, where the `x_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ s`) belong to `ūĚĒ≠` and are
part of a system of parameters `(x_j)_{1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n}` of `A`. Set `A_0 = k[[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_n]]`; since `A` is a complete
`k`-algebra and the `x_j` belong to the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™` of `A`, there exists a local `k`-homomorphism `u : A_0 ‚Üí A`
such that `u(T_j) = x_j` for `1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n` (Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. III, ¬ß4, n¬į 5, prop. 6); if `ūĚĒę` is the ideal of
`A` generated by the `x_j` (`1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ n`), it is by hypothesis an ideal of definition of `A`; one therefore deduces from
`(0_I, 7.4.4 and 7.4.3)` that `u` makes `A` into a finite `A_0`-algebra.

Set `ūĚĒ≠_0 = ‚ąĎ_{j=1}^s A_0 T_j`, `B_0 = (A_0)_{ūĚĒ≠_0}` and `B = A ‚äó_{A_0} B_0`, so that `u_1 : B_0 ‚Üí B` makes

<!-- original page 173 -->

`B` into a finite `B_0`-algebra; moreover, if `ūĚĒ≠'` is the ideal of `B` generated by `ūĚĒ≠`, one has `B_{ūĚĒ≠'} = A_ūĚĒ≠`; since
`ūĚĒ≠'` contains `ūĚĒ≠_0 B` by construction, it lies above the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ≠_0 B_0` of `B_0`. Let us show that the morphism
`Spec(B) ‚Üí Spec(B_0)` is unramified at the point `ūĚĒ≠'`: this results indeed from the fact that `k(ūĚĒ≠')` is a finite
extension of the field `k(ūĚĒ≠_0)` of characteristic `0`, hence is necessarily separable, and that one has
`B_{ūĚĒ≠'}/ūĚĒ≠_0 B_{ūĚĒ≠'} = k(ūĚĒ≠')` by virtue of the choice of the `x_j` for `1 ‚Č§ j ‚Č§ s` `(17.4.1)`.

Let us now note the following lemma:

**Lemma (18.11.3.5).** *Let `k` be a field, `R`, `S` two complete Noetherian local `k`-algebras, such that, if `K` is
the residue field of `S`, `ő©_{K/k}^1` is of finite rank over `K`; let `u : R ‚Üí S` be a `k`-homomorphism making `S` into
a finite `R`-algebra; then one has `ő©Őā_{S/R}^1 = ő©_{S/R}^1`, and the sequence*

```text
  (18.11.3.6)    ő©_{R/k}^1 ‚äó_R S ‚Üí^v ő©_{S/k}^1 ‚Üí^w ő©_{S/R}^1 ‚Üí 0
```

*(cf. `(0, 20.7.17.3)`) is exact.*

The first assertion results from the fact that `ő©_{S/R}^1` is an `S`-module of finite type `(0, 20.4.7)` and from
`(0_I, 7.3.6)`. On the other hand, one knows `(0, 20.7.17.3)` that the image of `v` is dense in `Ker(w)` and that `w` is
surjective; but it results from `(18.11.1)` that `ő©_{S/k}^1` is an `S`-module of finite type; one concludes that every
sub-`S`-module of `ő©_{S/k}^1` is closed `(0_I, 7.3.5)`, whence the lemma.

Let us apply this lemma to the case where `R = A_0`, `S = A`, and note that `ő©_{A_0/k}^1` is a free `A_0`-module of rank
`n` `(0, 21.9.3)`; hence one has `ő©Őā_{A_0/k}^1 ‚äó_{A_0} A = ő©_{A_0/k}^1 ‚äó_{A_0} A` and this `A`-module is free of rank
`n`. Since `Spec(B)` is unramified over `Spec(B_0)` at the point `ūĚĒ≠'`, one has `(ő©_{A/A_0}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ = ő©_{B/B_0}^1)_{ūĚĒ≠'} = 0`
`(2.5.10 and 17.4.1)`. If one localizes the exact sequence `(18.11.3.6)` (applied to `A_0` and `A`) at `ūĚĒ≠`, one
therefore obtains a surjective homomorphism

```text
  (ő©_{A_0/k}^1)_ūĚĒĮ ‚äó_{(A_0)_ūĚĒĮ} A_ūĚĒ≠ ‚Üí (ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠,
```

whence one concludes that the `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` admits a system of `n` generators. But the `A`-module
`ő©_{A/k}^1` is of rank `n`, by virtue of `(0, 21.9.5)`, which is applicable to the complete and integral ring `A`
because of Cohen's theorem `(0, 19.8.8, (ii))` and the fact that the field of fractions of `A` is of characteristic
`0`. The `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` is therefore also of rank `n`, and since its quotient by its torsion sub-module
admits a system of `n` generators, this quotient is necessarily free; one deduces at once that the `n` generators of
`(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` obtained above form a free system, whence the conclusion.

**Lemma (18.11.4).**

<!-- label: IV.18.11.4 -->

*Let `k` be a field, `A` a complete Noetherian local `k`-algebra, whose residue field is a finite extension of `k`. Let
`k'` be an arbitrary extension of `k`, and set `√ā' = A ‚äóŐā_k k'`. Then:*

*(i) `√ā'` is a complete Noetherian semi-local ring, direct composite of complete local rings `A'_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`) which
are faithfully flat `A`-modules, and whose residue fields are finite extensions of `k'`; if `ūĚĒ™` is the maximal ideal of
`A`, `ūĚĒ™ √ā'` is an ideal of definition of `√ā'`; one has `dim(A'_i) = dim(A)` for every `i`.*

*(ii) For every `i`, `ő©Őā_{A'_i/k'}^1` is canonically isomorphic to `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A A'_i`.*

<!-- original page 174 -->

(i) The first assertions result at once from `(7.5.5)` and `(0_I, 6.6.2)`; the fact that `ūĚĒ™ √ā'` is an ideal of
definition of `√ā'` also results from `(7.5.5)`, for if `K` is the residue field of `A`, `K ‚äó_k k'` is a finite
`k'`-algebra. Finally `A'_i/ūĚĒ™ A'_i` is one of the direct-composite Artinian local rings of `K ‚äó_k k'` `(7.5.5)`, so it
is of dimension `0`; since `A'_i` is a flat `A`-module, the equality of the dimensions of `A` and of `A'_i` results from
`(6.1.2)`.

(ii) Since `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1` is, by virtue of the hypothesis on `K`, an `A`-module of finite type `(18.11.1)`,
`ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A √ā'` is complete and identifies with the completed tensor product `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äóŐā_A √ā'`; by virtue of the
associativity of the completed tensor product `(0_I, 7.7.4)`, `ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äóŐā_A (A ‚äóŐā_k k')` identifies with
`ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äóŐā_k k'`. But `ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_k k'` identifies with `ő©_{A''/k'}^1`, where `A'' = A ‚äó_k k'` `(0, 20.5.5)`, and
since `√ā'` is by definition the separated completion of `A''`, the separated completion of `ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_k k'`
identifies by construction with that of `ő©_{A''/k'}^1` `(0, 20.7.4)`; in other words, one has a canonical isomorphism

```text
  ő©Őā_{A/k}^1 ‚äóŐā_k k' = ő©Őā_{√ā'/k'}^1.
```

The conclusion of (ii) results now from the fact that `ő©Őā_{√ā'/k'}^1` is the direct sum of the `ő©Őā_{A'_i/k'}^1`
`(0, 20.4.13)`, and if `ūĚĒĮ` is the radical of `√ā'`, the `ūĚĒĮ`-preadic topology on `ő©_{√ā'/k'}^1` identifies with the product
of the `ūĚĒĮ A'_i`-preadic topologies on the `ő©_{A'_i/k'}^1` (where `ūĚĒ™'_i` is the maximal ideal of `A'_i`); finally, it
results that the separated completion `ő©Őā_{√ā'/k'}^1` for the `ūĚĒĮ`-preadic topology identifies with the product of the
separated completions `ő©Őā_{A'_i/k'}^1` for the `ūĚĒ™'_i`-preadic topologies, and it suffices to use `(0, 20.4.5)`.

**Proposition (18.11.5).**

<!-- label: IV.18.11.5 -->

*Let `k` be a field, `A` a complete Noetherian local `k`-algebra whose residue field is a finite extension of `k`, `ūĚĒ≠`
a prime ideal of `A`, distinct from the maximal ideal `ūĚĒ™`, such that there exists a minimal prime ideal `ūĚĒģ ‚äā ūĚĒ≠` for
which `dim(A/ūĚĒģ) = dim(A)` (which will hold in particular when `A` is equidimensional), `m` an integer `‚Č• 0`. The
following conditions are equivalent:*

*a) The `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` admits a system of `m` generators.*

*b) There exists a local `k`-homomorphism `u : B ‚Üí A`, where `B = k[[T_1, ‚Ä¶, T_m]]`, making `A` into a finite
`B`-algebra, and such that the corresponding morphism `Spec(A) ‚Üí Spec(B)` is unramified at the point `ūĚĒ≠`.*

To prove that b) implies a), note that by virtue of lemma `(18.11.3.5)`, one has then the exact sequence

```text
  ő©_{B/k}^1 ‚äó_B A ‚Üí ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚Üí ő©_{A/B}^1 ‚Üí 0
```

since `A` is a finite `B`-algebra; localizing at `ūĚĒ≠` and noting that by hypothesis one has then
`(ő©_{A/B}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ = 0` `(17.4.1)`, one obtains a surjective homomorphism `(ő©_{B/k}^1 ‚äó_B A)_ūĚĒ≠ ‚Üí (ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠`, and the
conclusion results from the fact that `ő©_{B/k}^1` is a free `B`-module of rank `m` `(0, 21.9.3)`.

To prove that a) implies b), let us first prove the following lemmas.

**Lemma (18.11.5.1).** *Let `k` be a field, `A` a complete Noetherian local `k`-algebra, whose residue field is a finite
extension of `k`, `ūĚĒ≠` a prime ideal of `A`, `ūĚĒģ` a minimal ideal of `A` contained in `ūĚĒ≠`. Then the minimum number of
generators of the `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` is at least equal to `dim(A/ūĚĒģ)`.*

<!-- original page 175 -->

Set `n = dim(A/ūĚĒģ)`, and let `m` be the minimum number of generators of the `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠`, which is equal
to `rg_{k(ūĚĒ≠)}(ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A k(ūĚĒ≠))` (Bourbaki, Alg. comm., chap. II, ¬ß3, n¬į 2, cor. 2 of prop. 4). Note that one has
`(ő©_{A/ūĚĒģ/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ = (ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A A/ūĚĒģ)_ūĚĒ≠`, so the minimum number of generators of the `(A/ūĚĒģ)_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/ūĚĒģ/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠`
is at most equal to `m`. It suffices consequently to consider the case where `ūĚĒ≠ = ūĚĒģ` is minimal. In the second place,
let us show that one can restrict to the case where `k` is algebraically closed. Indeed, let `k'` be an algebraic
closure of `k`, and set `√ā' = A ‚äóŐā_k k'`, which is direct composite of local `k'`-algebras `A'_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`), whose
residue fields are isomorphic to `k'`, and which are faithfully flat `A`-modules `(18.11.4)`. Applying `(2.3.4)` and
`(6.1.1)`, one sees that in a given `A'_i`, there exists a minimal ideal `ūĚĒģ'_i` above `ūĚĒģ` such that
`n = dim(A'/ūĚĒģ'_i) = dim(A/ūĚĒģ) = n`. On the other hand `(18.11.4)`, one has
`(ő©Őā_{A'_i/k'}^1)_{ūĚĒģ'_i} = (ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒģ ‚äó_{A_ūĚĒģ} (A'_i)_{ūĚĒģ'_i}`, so the minimum number `m'` of generators of the
`(A'_i)_{ūĚĒģ'_i}`-module `(ő©Őā_{A'_i/k'}^1)_{ūĚĒģ'_i}` is at most equal to `m`, whence our assertion.

Suppose therefore `k` algebraically closed, and let us show that one can moreover restrict to the case where `ūĚĒģ = 0`.
Indeed, one has `ő•_{A/ūĚĒģ/A} = 0` `(0, 20.4.12)`, so the exact sequence `(18.11.3.6)` furnishes a surjective homomorphism
`(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ ‚äó_A (A/ūĚĒģ) ‚Üí (ő©_{A/ūĚĒģ/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠`, whence one deduces at once that the minimum number of generators of the
`K`-module `(ő©_{A/ūĚĒģ/k}^1) ‚äó_A K`, where `K` is the field of fractions of `A/ūĚĒģ`, is at most equal to `m`.

But if `A` is integral and `K` its field of fractions, `K` is separable over the algebraically closed field `k`, hence a
geometrically regular `k`-algebra `(6.7.6)`; one can therefore apply `(18.11.3)` for `ūĚĒ≠ = 0` observing that `k = k^p`
since `k` is algebraically closed, and in this case one has `m = n`.

**Lemma (18.11.5.2).** *Let `k` be a field, `A` a complete Noetherian local `k`-algebra, whose residue field is a finite
extension of `k`, `ūĚĒ™` its maximal ideal, `ūĚĒ≠ ‚äā ūĚĒ™` a prime ideal of `A`. Let `m` be the minimum number of generators of
the `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠`, and let `(x_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ m}` be a family of elements of `ūĚĒ™` not belonging to `ūĚĒ≠`. Then
there exist `m` invertible elements `u_i` (`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ m`) of `A` such that if one sets `y_i = u_i x_i`, the canonical
images of the `dy_i` in `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠` generate this `A_ūĚĒ≠`-module.*

For every `x ‚ąą A`, let us denote by `őī(x)` the canonical image of `dx (= d_{A/k} x)` in
`(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ ‚äó_{A_ūĚĒ≠} k(ūĚĒ≠) = ő©_{A/k}^1 ‚äó_A k(ūĚĒ≠)`; since this `k(ūĚĒ≠)`-vector space is by hypothesis of rank `m`, it
suffices (by virtue of Nakayama's lemma) to prove that one can determine the `u_i` such that the `őī(y_i)` form a free
system. Reasoning by recurrence and supposing that, for an integer `r < m`, one has determined the `u_i`
(`1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`) such that the `őī(y_i)` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r` are linearly independent over `k(ūĚĒ≠)`; if `őī(x_{r+1})` is not a
linear combination of the `őī(y_i)` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`, it suffices to take `u_{r+1} = 1` to continue the recurrence. In
the contrary case, note that for `u ‚ąą A`, `őī(u x_{r+1})` is the canonical image of `u(dx_{r+1}) + x_{r+1}(du)`; since
`u(dx_{r+1})` has a canonical image linear combination of the `őī(y_i)` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`, and that on the other hand the
canonical image of `x_{r+1}` in `k(ūĚĒ≠)` is `‚Č† 0`, one sees that it suffices to prove that there exists an invertible
element `u ‚ąą A` such that `őī(u)` is not a linear combination of the `őī(y_i)` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`. Now, it results from
`(0, 20.7.15)` and from `(0_I, 7.2.9)` that the `őī(x)` generate the `k(ūĚĒ≠)`-vector space `(ő©_{A/k}^1)_ūĚĒ≠ ‚äó_{A_ūĚĒ≠} k(ūĚĒ≠)`;

<!-- original page 176 -->

since by hypothesis `r < m`, there therefore exists `v ‚ąą A` such that `őī(v)` is not a linear combination of the
`őī(y_i)` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ r`. If `v ‚ąČ ūĚĒ™`, one will take `u = v`; otherwise, `1 + v` is invertible and one has
`őī(1 + v) = őī(v)` since `d(1 + v) = dv`; one will then take `u = 1 + v`, which finishes proving `(18.11.5.2)`.

Let us now return to the proof of the implication a) `‚áí` b) in `(18.11.5)`. Note first that since `ūĚĒ≠ ‚Č† ūĚĒ™`, there exists
a system of parameters `(x_i)_{1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ n}` of `A` (with `n = dim(A)`) such that `x_i ‚ąČ ūĚĒ≠` for `1 ‚Č§ i ‚Č§ n`: indeed, one
cannot have `x_i ‚ąą ūĚĒ≠` for every `i`, otherwise `A/ūĚĒ≠` would be of finite length, and `ūĚĒ≠` would be maximal, contrary to
